E1.3.A. IS HAPPINESS FOUND IN THE WAITING?

We often find ourselves waiting for future events rather than rejoicing in the present. Could happiness lie in this anticipation? The expectation of an event—whether we actively work towards it or not—can be a wellspring of joy. Thus, the pursuit of happiness might be justified, not for its ability to create lasting contentment, but for the joy it brings during the chase.

However, this argument loses strength when we consider the frequent disappointment we face upon reaching our goal. Can we break free from this endless cycle of waiting? Should we reshape our desires? Are there mental exercises to help us overcome this constant anticipation?

Ultimately, finding happiness in waiting might be a form of escapism and a dead end for lasting joy. Yet, we must acknowledge that there's genuine pleasure in anticipation when it heightens our self-awareness and reaffirms our desires.

E1.3.A.1. IF HAPPINESS WERE ONLY IN WAITING, ONE SHOULD STRIVE TO PROLONG THE PROCESS THAT LEADS US THERE — even if it were a "path of suffering" — for this path of waiting is, in fact, happiness itself.

"Why do intelligent people spend their time accumulating possessions rather than enjoying what they already have? Is it because they're following social norms? Or is it because hope itself is pleasurable?"
(Aristotle, Problem XXX)

E1.3.A.1.1. "HE ASSERTED THAT THE PRESENT WAS NEVER A HAPPY STATE TO ANY HUMAN BEING; but that, as every part of life, of which we are conscious, was at some point of time a period yet to come, in which felicity was expected, there was some happiness produced by hope. Being pressed upon this subject, and asked if he really was of opinion, that though, in general, happiness was very rare in human life, a man was not sometimes happy in the moment that was present, he answered, ‘Never, but when he is drunk.’"
(James Boswell, Boswell’s Life of Johnson, 1791)

E1.3.A.1.2. “MAKING PLANS. - Making plans and resolutions brings many pleasant feelings; and he who has the strength to be, throughout his life, nothing but a maker of plans would be a very happy man: but he will occasionally have to let himself rest from this activity by executing a plan - and then will come for him anger and disillusionment.”
(Friedrich Nietzsche, Human, Too Human II, Aphorism 85, 1878)

That their difficulties are of their own making is the last thing they will admit usually."
(Henry Miller, The books in my life, 1969)

E1.3.A.1.2_REF2_LIT ONLY MADMEN, DERANGED PEOPLE, PEOPLE WITH FIXED IDEAS CAN PERSEVERE FOR LONG IN THE FIRE OF THE ECSTATIC SOUL "Ideals and morality are the best means of filling the big hole that one calls the soul. [...]
God knows—as has already been said—what a soul is anyway! There can be no doubt whatsoever that the burning wish to hearken to it alone leaves one with an immeasurable field open for action, a thorough state of anarchy, and we have examples of so to speak chemically pure souls com mitting downright crimes. On the other hand, as soon as a soul has morality or religion, philosophy, and intensive bourgeois education and ideals in the realms of duty and of the beautiful, it is endowed with a system of regulations, conditions and directives for operation, which it has to fill out before it is entitled to think of itself as a respectable soul, and its heat, like that of a blast-furnace, is conducted into beautiful squares of sand. What remains then is fundamentally only logical problems of interpretation, of the kind as to whether an action comes under this or that commandment; and the soul presents the tranquil panorama of a battlefield after the battle, where the dead lie quiet and one can at once observe where a scrap of life yet stirs or groans. And so man makes this transition as fast as he can. If he is tormented by religious doubts, as occasionally happens in youth, he goes straight over to the persecution of unbelievers; if love deranges him, he turns it into marriage; and if any other enthusiasm overwhelms him, he disentangles himself from the impossibility of living perpetually in the fire of it by beginning to live for that fire. That is, instead of filling the many moments of his day, each of which needs a content and an impetus, with his ideal state, he fills them with the activity for the sake of his ideal state, in other words, with the many means to the end, the hindrances and incidents that are a sure guarantee that he never need reach it. For only fools, the mentally deranged, and people with idée fixes, can endure unceasingly in the fire of the soul’s rapture. A sane man must content himself with declaring that life would not seem worth living without a flake of that mysterious fire."
(Robert Musil, The Man Without Qualities, Volume 1, 1930)

"WHAT GORGEOUS THING

I do not know what gorgeous thing 
the bluebird keeps saying, 
his voice easing out of his throat, 
beak, body into the pink air 
of the early morning. I like it 
whatever it is. Sometimes 
it seems the only thing in the world 
that is without dark thoughts. 
Sometimes it seems the only thing 
in the world that is without 
questions that can't and probably 
never will be answered, the 
only thing that is entirely content 
with the pink, then clear white 
morning and, gratefully, says so."
(Mary Oliver, Blue horses, 2014)

E1.3.A.1.2_REF4_PO “THE SCAFFOLD

The time I’ve spent dreaming—
Years and years of my life!
Ah, how much of my past
Was only the false life
Of a future I imagined!

Here on the bank of the river
I grow calm for no reason.
Its empty ﬂowing mirrors,
Cold and anonymous,
The life I’ve lived in vain.

How little hope ever attains! 
What longing is worth the wait? 
Any child’s ball
Rises higher than my hope, 
Rolls farther than my longing.

Waves of the river, so slight 
That you aren’t even waves,
The hours, days and years
Pass quickly—mere grass or snow 
Which die by the same sun.

I spent all I didn’t have.
I’m older than I am.
The illusion that kept me going
Was a queen only on stage:
Once undressed, her reign was over.

Soft sound of these slow waters 
Aching for shores you’ve passed, 
How drowsy are the memories
Of misty hopes! What dreams
All dreaming and life amount to!

What did I make of my life?
I found myself when already lost. 
Impatient, I let myself be,
As I might let a lunatic go on 
Believing what I’d proved was wrong.

Dead sound of these gentle waters 
That ﬂow because they must,
Take not only my memories
But also my dead hopes—
Dead, because they must die.

I’m already my future corpse.
Only a dream links me to myself— 
The hazy and belated dream
Of what I should have been—a wall 
Around my abandoned garden.

Take me, passing waves,
To the oblivion of the sea!
Bequeath me to what I won’t be— 
I, who raised a scaffold
Around the house I never built.”
(Fernando Pessoa, 29 August 1924)

E1.3.A.1.3. DON'T THE MASTERS OF EXPECTATION, BEING NO FOOLS, POSTPONE THE END OF THEIR JOURNEY PRECISELY BECAUSE THEY KNOW DISAPPOINTMENT AWAITS THEM? As Spinoza demonstrates, fear underlies all expectations.
"PROPOSITION 47
Emotions of hope and fear cannot be in themselves good.

PROOF
Emotions of hope and fear cannot exist without pain. For fear is pain  [...] , and hope [...] cannot exist without fear ; therefore  [...]  these emotions cannot be good in themselves, but only in so far as they can restrain excessive pleasure  [...].

NOTE
We may add, that these emotions show defective knowledge and an absence of power in the mind ; for the same reason confidence, despair, joy, and disappointment are signs of a want of mental power. For although confidence and joy are pleasurable emotions, they nevertheless imply a preceding pain, namely, hope and fear. Wherefore the more we endeavour to be guided by reason, the less do we depend on hope ; we endeavour to free ourselves from fear, and, as far as we can, to dominate fortune, directing our actions by the sure counsels of wisdom."
(Baruch Spinoza, The Ethics, Part 4)

Read the Ethics online: http://app.ethica-spinoza.net/en/text

E1.3.A.2. ACCORDING TO NICOLAS GRIMALDI, WAITING IS THE FABRIC OF CONSCIOUSNESS ITSELF. The present is elusive because consciousness' very nature is to wait. Life is reflected in consciousness and life is becoming/propensity; life is therefore reflected in waiting and we never hold on to the present because the attention we give to the future distracts us from the present. The present hurts us because it takes away an expectation which conclusion constantly eludes us.
Inherent to waiting is the sense of a condition where we would have nothing more to expect. When you don't have anything more to expect you have access to the infinite - the absolute, to which nothing can be added; it is also death: to wait is to live on the background of one's own death, of eternity, of the supernatural. As you wait; your conscience becomes religious.

E1.3.A.2.1. "EVERYTHING WE KNOW SEEMS LIMITED, [...] WE ALWAYS EXPECT SOMETHING ELSE. [...] Life is spent waiting for who knows what, which would be so intense, so dense, so overwhelming that there would be nothing left to wait for".
(Nicolas Grimaldi, Ordinary madness, 2009)
Tags: Orange

E1.3.A.2.1_REF1_PH ACCORDING TO NICOLAS GRIMALDI, IN WHAT SITUATIONS DO WE HAVE NOTHING TO LOOK FORWARD TO?

- The trance of love is so saturated that it leaves nothing to look forward to.

- The appeal of music lies in the alternation between memory and the satisfaction of having nothing more to look forward to. It is the memory/presence of the past in the present that makes the tension of music possible.

- Films
"With our expectations as habitually postponed as they are disappointed, films bring us the lively vitality of adventures and the reassuring relaxation of their end. For the transitivity of our expectation is twofold. It only makes sense if two conditions are met. The first is that something happens, which is all the more unexpected because it's always new. That is the flow of events. This is adventure. Just as emptiness awaits for fullness, the second condition is that our expectation must be filled or fulfilled by something that leaves nothing more to wait for. That's what we call happiness in the movies.
(Nicolas Grimaldi, An ordinary madness, 2009)

E1.3.A.2.1_REF1.1_PH NICOLAS GRIMALDI TELLS US THAT MUSIC OFFERS US A REFUGE WHERE THERE'S NOTHING TO WAIT FOR. Beyond the reminiscence and permanence of the past in the present offered by musical variation on a repeated theme, doesn't music offer us liberation from expectation in another way?

It completely fills our attention span, fully satisfies us in the present - and, thanks to variation, also offers us surprise. This theme, enchanting in itself, has even more to offer. The unfolding of the music destroys expectation through the surprise of variation. In this sense, it educates our perception and shows us that enchantment is possible if we develop our capacity to pay attention to reality.

E1.3.A.2.1_REF3_PH THE SOLUTION: LIVE IN THE PRESENT. NICOLAS GRIMALDI, HOWEVER, SUGGESTS THIS MAY BE IMPOSSIBLE. 
"Without us beeing aware of it, our secret hope for the ultimate - always waiting for what would leave nothing more to wait for -  is also always the secret of our disappointment. Surreptitiously, we imagine that the hardwork of the quest and of waiting will come to an end with the exultant plenitude of some immediacy. When what we had so much waited for finally happens: [...] it is still a mediation. By a kind of sarcastic alternative that Schopenhauer had perfectly characterized, we either experience the mortifying experience of immediacy in boredom while dreaming of something else, or we experience mediation in expectation, worry and effort, dreaming of finally getting perfection. We either do not know what we want, and are disappointed by the absence of taste of everything. Or we despair, and are disappointed that we never get what we expected.
The present could therefore only be curred of its ordinary inconsistency which disappoints us by being removed from the mediations of temporality. Without a past which anticipates and prepares it, without a foreseeable future for which it must prepare in turn, the only present that does not disappoint us has the vivacity and suddenness of a miracle. There is no dense presence except that of surprise."
(Nicolas Grimaldi, Brief treatise on disenchantment, 1998)

E1.3.A.2.1_REF4_CUR IN BOYHOOD, MASON JR.'S MOTHER DID NOT INHERIT HIS FATHER'S ABILITY OF WONDER. In a dialogue between Mason Jr and his mother as he leaves home for college, she tells him: “I just thought there would be more”.

If we're always waiting for something else, we can't taste happiness. A perfect moment would be a moment fulfilled in one's interiority, no longer threatened by externalities. A moment that, as Nicolas Grimaldi puts it, leaves nothing else to wait for.

E1.3.A.2.2. "COULD WE DESIRE ANYTHING WITHOUT IMAGINING IT, WITHOUT REPRESENTING ITS POSSIBILITY, WITHOUT PERCEIVING ITS DELAY AND THUS EXPERIENCING TIME? On the other hand, isn't it enough to imagine, to desire, to consider the infinity of possibilities, to immediately experience the limits, the scarcity and the precariousness of the present? Imagination, possibility, desire and time are four figures in the same metaphysical paradox: the obsessive specter of the unreal in reality and of nothingness in being.
(Nicolas Grimaldi, A brief treatise on disenchantment, 1998)

E1.3.A.2.2.c. "ADAM KNEW THAT HE WAS HAPPY BUT HE DID NOT FEEL IT. [...] One can neither conceive, imagine or desire something more than infinity. Adam lived face to face with the infinite. Not this undifferentiated, indeterminable, unrepresentable infinity, which is that of being in general. Adam lived in the presence of The Infinite Being, so infinitely simple that each of his infinite perfections expressed the infinity of the whole. God exceeded all possible understanding, always offering more to discover and to marvel at, as more was discovered. The presence of God therefore should have taken away all possible meaning for innovation, adventure, desire as well as for any expectation, yet Adam was tempted.
This would not have been possible if he had been fulfilled with the infinite satisfaction of an infinite presence. Therefore, here is the lesson of the myth [...]: even the presence of the infinitely infinite is not what one might expect. Since even the presence of God may have lacked in intensity, fullness, consistency and density, it is quite clear that it is not the present which is in itself deficient in that it lacks of something that we desire, but conscience which in itself feels like incompleteness and awkwardness. Consciousness therefore does not feel the present as inconsistent because it is disappointed not to find what it expected in it, but because expectation is its very essence."
(Nicolas Grimaldi, Brief treatise on disenchantment, 1998)

E1.3.A.3. FOR SCHOPENHAUER, MAN'S REALITY IS IN INFINITE WAITING: this reality is inscribed in the will to live, which is at odds with happiness: "aimless striving, nothingness, absence of end".
"WILL TO LIVE 
Schopenhauer's main concept, which he described as "a blind and irresistible force", one and universal (and therefore impersonal), both free and necessary, absurd and indestructible. It is the undifferentiated essence of all being, the true "thing-in-itself", like a cosmic force, but of which the world we know, or the individuals we think we are, are merely phenomena or representations. What does this power want? It wants itself: to want and to live, and the two are one and the same. No external goal, and therefore no chance of achieving it: "Every particular act has a purpose; the will itself has none. [The absence of any goal or limit is essential to the will itself, which is a never-ending effort. The will to live imposes itself everywhere as an eternal striving, all the more insane because it strives for nothing ("aimless striving," writes Schopenhauer, "nothingness, absence of end"). [...]
The will to live, because it knows neither rest nor cessation, condemns us constantly to dissatisfaction or disappointment. It is "aimless": how can it reach its goal? It is "boundless": how can it stop? "Desperate thought," writes Schopenhauer. What could be more futile than an "endless effort" (for the will to live) or with no other end than death (for the individual)? Like the Buddha, Schopenhauer teaches that "suffering is the basis of all life". All satisfaction is fleeting: "Never a true goal, never final satisfaction, nowhere a place of rest!" Happiness? It's just a dream, impossible to realise. All we are entitled to is an endless alternation of suffering and boredom [...].
Schopenhauer's will to live is more or less the counterpart of Spinozism's conatus (but less individualised), Nietzsche's will to power (plus pessimism), Bergson's élan vital (minus optimism), and even Freud's libido (which tends less towards pleasure than towards life itself, however painful it may be). The only wisdom, for Schopenhauer, is to free oneself from this blind will to live: not through suicide, which remains its prisoner ("He who kills himself wants to live; he is only dissatisfied with the conditions under which life has been granted to him"), but through art (which liberates less than it soothes or comforts), asceticism or holiness. One might think that it would be easier to love life in spite of everything; but that's what Schopenhauer never achieved or agreed to".
(André Comte-Sponville, Philosophical Dictionary, 2013)

E1.3.A.3_REF1_PH "FOR SCHOPENHAUER, AESTHETIC EXPERIENCE ALLOWS ME TO CEASE BEING AN AGENT OF DESIRE AND INSTEADVTO BECOME A SPECTATOR, THUS CEASING TO SUFFER THROUGH CONTEMPLATION.
One can admire and benefit from the irrationalist thinking that brings Schopenhauer's intellectualism to its knees, without subscribing to (and even being disturbed by) its pessimistic conclusion. This is the man for whom intuition counts before intellect, for whom desire comes before intelligence, will before representation - very close to Bergson in this sense. Both are philosophers of wonder at existence - which for Schopenhauer has no cause. For Schopenhauer, wisdom means accepting the sadness that comes with existence and not suffering too much - whereas Clément Rosset (like Spinoza) builds a philosophy of joy on the diagnosis of the absurdity of existence. The tragic diagnosis that would give rise to despair is the same as that of Schopenhauer or Cioran, but the conclusion is quite different: life is still joy.
For the pessimist, even if there were a God and an afterlife, despair would persist because we are insignificant in terms of time and space. As Clément Rosset points out in Force Majeure, for the pessimist, the eternity of life after death merely prolongs man's insignificance in terms of time and space, beyond the duration of this life: 'Wouldn't that be worse for the pessimist, an eternity of suffering and boredom!'"
(Clément Rosset in À voie nue, broadcast on France Culture on 05/13/2018)

Listen to the podcast: https://www.radiofrance.fr/franceculture/podcasts/a-voix-nue/clement-rosset-c-est-tres-simple-de-vivre-quand-on-n-est-pas-instruit-du-tout-4834987

E1.3.A.3_REF2_PH DIVERSION AS A STRATEGY?
"Diversion is the opposite of what our psychiatrists today call rumination. It's less a weakness than a strategy, less an attempt to forget our nothingness, as in Pascal, than a legitimate refusal to be overwhelmed by it. An art of evasion and hygiene. Let's say it's a lucid form of entertainment: it's a refusal to take the tragic seriously".
(André Comte-Sponville, Philosophical Dictionary, 2013)

"We are less bored than our ancestors were, but we are more afraid of boredom. We have come to know, or rather to believe, that boredom is not part of the natural lot of man, but can be avoided by a sufficiently vigorous pursuit of excitement."
(Bertrand Russell, The Conquest of Happiness, 1962)

E1.3.A.3_REF3_CUR "THE BODIES REVEALED BY FRANCIS BACON DO NOT INSPIRE DESIRE - was he disgusted by them? He saw in reality an extraordinary violence: 'When talking about the violence of paint, it's nothing to do with the violence of war. It's to do with an attempt to remake the violence of reality itself... the violence of suggestions within the image which can only be conveyed through paint.' Is this violence in reality, or was it in his gaze, his psyche marked by a hard life of rejection (his family's non-recognition of his sexual preference, for example)?

This reminds us of Schopenhauer, for whom desire is, by its very nature, suffering, because satisfaction quickly leads to satiation and the realisation that 'the goal was illusory; possession robs it of its attraction'."
(Pierre Schndler)

1. by suffering to a point when the will to live is killed in you or;
2. by the realization that your own perspective is limited and that you can expand it -  in which case, you realize your joy is someone else's suffering, so that the will ceases to will."

E1.3.A.4. ARE THERE MENTAL EXERCISES TO CURE US OF ENDLESS WAITING?

E1.3.A.4.1. EPICURUS ENCOURAGES US TO "WORK" ON OUR DESIRES TO ACHIEVE GREATER HAPPINESS. HE ADVISES DETACHING OURSELVES FROM UNNATURAL AND UNNECESSARY DESIRES, AS THESE LEAD TO UNHAPPINESS. A happy life, according to Epicurus, is founded on satisfying natural and necessary desires—occasionally including natural but unnecessary desires (during festivities, for instance), provided they don't create dependencies. Following Epicurus, numerous thinkers (such as Tocqueville, Adorno, Lacan, Fromm, and Foucault) have explored how society shapes our desires. Can their insights help us overcome consumerism?

E1.3.A.4.1.a. INDIFFERENCE WILL BE AN ART AGAINST THE ILLUSION OF NEED - WHICH IS ALWAYS SOCIAL BY DEFINITION “The absence of relationship between gods and men is the first word of the Maxims, as it is the first of the Letter to Menecaeus. We must give it its full scope.
“The being who is happy and immortal does not himself, or cause anyone else, any pain. He does not get angry or begrudge anything: these feelings are marks of weakness” (Maximus I) Epicurus allows himself the luxury of positing gods who serve no purpose [...].
Now, since the bond is not in things but in the ideas that men have of them and in the relationships they have with one another, indifference is the political crowning achievement and civic expression of atomistic irreligion. [...]
In fact, the indifference of the wise man has nothing to do with the reasonable abandonment of the Stoic in the face of the disproportionate power of Tyche [the Greek goddess of luck, fortune and chance]. Where the Stoic aims for the most complete possible mastery of all that depends on himself, the Epicurean tends, on the contrary, to free himself as far as possible from rational servitude. This is particularly clear in Maxim XIV: “Since the tranquility that can be obtained by means of other men does not go up to a certain point, there is an art to obtaining a perfect one for oneself: it is to simplify one's needs, to free oneself from many things”. The repeated invitation to independence cannot be understood as an echo of ethical naturalism. On the contrary, indifference will be an art against the illusion of need - which is always social by definition”
(Guillaume Badoual, alain Chareyre-Méjan, Charles Floren, Elsa Grasso and Guillaume Pigeard de Gurbert (Collective), Epicurus or straightforward happiness, 1993)

E1.3.A.4.1.a_REF1_PH "FOR WHEN HE [EPICURUS] SAW
THAT MORTALS HAD BY NOW ATTAINED WELLNIGH ALL THINGS
WHICH THEIR NEEDS CRAVE FOR SUBSISTENCE,
and that, as far as they could, their life was established in safety,
that men abounded in power through wealth
and honours and renown, and were haughty
in the good name of their children,
and yet not one of them for all that had at home
heart less anguished, but with torture of mind
lived a fretful life without any respite,
and was constrained to rage with savage complaining,
he then did understand
that it was the vessel itself
which wrought the disease,
and that by its disease all things were corrupted within,
what soever came into it gathered from without,
in part because he saw that it was leaking
and full of holes, so that by no means
could it ever be filled;
in part because he perceived that it tainted
as with a foul savour all things within it,
which it had taken in."
(Lucretius, On the Nature of Things, VI, 8-25)

E1.3.A.4.1.b. ALREADY TOCQUEVILLE, WARNED US: "MATERIALISM IS A DANGEROUS DISEASE OF THE HUMAN MIND IN ALL NATIONS; but it is especially to be feared in a democratic people, because it combines wonderfully with the vice of the heart which is most familiar to these peoples."
In the 1960s, capitalism, which had begun as a form of asceticism, promoted consumerism through advertising and triggered an endless pursuit of happiness. Has this culture in which we are immersed strengthened desire's grip on Western populations in recent decades? Does it impose a "duty to be happy"? Now that a growing number of people are free from religion's influence, and for those living in democracies, we have become our own worst enemy in the pursuit of happiness. Are we unhappy because we fail to achieve happiness according to the standard model proposed by consumer society?

E1.3.A.4.1.b_REF1_PH ON EVERYONE IS PREOCCUPIED WITH THEIR OWN PLEASURES, SO AS TO BETTER FORGET THE EFFORT INVOLVED IN THE SEARCH FOR PLENITUDE. But, as Michel Foucault perfectly points out, democracy produces democratic individuals who have less and less taste for true discourse and self-concern, in the Platonic sense of the term. We are in a parody of the taste for truth and self-care. On the one hand, everyone is preoccupied with their own pleasures, so as to better forget the effort involved in the search for plenitude. On the other hand, everyone envies access to this pleasure, which they rightly consider unequal. Inequality redoubles our desire and resentment to experience such pleasures, and gives us the perfect alibi for avoiding true self-care. [...]

A similar description can be found in Tocqueville, forming Act II of democratization, or the age of the first great betrayal of principles: “Materialism is in all nations a dangerous disease of the human spirit; but it is especially to be feared in a democratic people, because it combines wonderfully with the vice of the heart which is most familiar to these peoples.” Tocqueville combines this pursuit of pleasure or material well-being, or the derangement of desires, with the end of transcendence: “In centuries of incredulity, it is always to be feared that men will not ceaselessly surrender themselves to the daily chance of their desires, and that, renouncing entirely what can only be acquired with long efforts, they will not create anything great, peaceful and lasting. [...] When everyone is constantly trying to change places, when immense competition is open to all, when wealth accumulates and dissipates in a few moments amid the tumult of democracy, the idea of sudden and easy fortune, of great goods easily acquired and lost, the image of chance, in all its forms, presents itself to the human mind. The instability of the social state encourages the natural instability of desires. In the midst of these perpetual fluctuations of fortune, the present grows; it hides the fading future, and men want to think only of tomorrow.”

From this observation he draws a definition of the role that a kind of moderate courageous person could play: 'The moralist must learn to defend himself in the spirit of his century and his country. Let him strive every day to show his contemporaries that, in the midst of the perpetual motion that surrounds them, it is easier than they suppose to conceive and carry out long undertakings. Let them see that, although the face of humanity has changed, the methods by which men can secure the prosperity of this world have remained the same, and that, among democratic peoples, as elsewhere, it is only by resisting a thousand small, particular passions of daily life that we can succeed in satisfying the general passion for happiness that torments us'. Without aiming for utopia, Tocqueville remains optimistic about the possibility of a democratic morality and an untainted access to happiness. Behind his description of the moralist, we can read a kind of Socrates, more prepared, more experienced in democratic behavior, i.e., more able to make his interlocutors hear the need for self-care without knowing the price it costs. But like Socrates, Tocqueville seems to prefer philosophical courage to political courage, or at least to believe in its superior efficacy. 'This apparent explanation,' Foucault writes, 'is the malfunctioning of democratic courage, or, more generally, of political courage; it's the inability, when dealing with political institutions, to play the courageous role properly, to play it fully, to play it to the end. Why is that? Simply because of the danger involved. [If I had devoted myself to politics long ago, I would have died long ago' [...] And Socrates adds: "Oh, do not be angry when I tell you truths [...]: there is no man who can avoid perishing if, for noble reasons, he generously opposes either you or any other popular assembly, and 'if he sets out to prevent injustices and illegalities in his city.' If we pass quickly over this text, the message is clear: I didn't go into politics, why? Because if I had come forward to speak to you, to tell you the truth, you would have had me perish, like all those who generously want to prevent injustices and illegalities in their city.
(Cynthia Fleury, The End of Courage, 2010)

E1.3.A.4.1.c. "A PART OF THE WORLD HAS TURNED RESOLUTELY TO THE SERVICE OF GOODS, REJECTING EVERYTHING THAT CONCERNS MAN'S RELATIONSHIP TO DESIRE. The only thing that can be said is that they don't seem to realize that by formulating things this way, they are merely perpetuating the eternal tradition of power—namely: "Keep on working, and as for your desire, you'll have to come back later." But who cares?"
(Jacques Lacan, Seminar VII, The Ethics of Psychoanalysis, July 6, 1960)

"The message of all masters and all tyrants—whatever aspiration for freedom inspired the revolutionary uprising that brought them to power—can be summed up thus, once the crisis has passed and the time has come for the restoration of order: "Go back to work and give up your desire."
(Jacques Darriulat, Lacan, Listening to Madness, V- The Master, 2019)

E1.3.A.4.1.e_REF1_CUR "THE ARTIST WAS INSPIRED BY ADVERTISING BILLBOARDS HE SAW IN PANAMA, which feature women's faces hat have been smoothed out to the point of rasing their personality. As a film maker would lo, he zoomed on this face so close that it is partially cut out and incomplete. 
By moving his "paintbrush camera" so close he treats this female face as if it was an object. which is precisely the purpose of advertising, in articular when selling beauty products. This approach renders the face empty, almost dead, and.	especially because it is not contextualized. 
But by zooming this way, the artist also highlights the gaze of this woman who has been so objectified that she doesn't even have a name. just a letter, K. And her gaze is very expressive, ri as if she tried to exist beyond the image and the commercial profit that is sought through her. She seems defiant, aware that she is exploited md ready to stand up as she looks far ahead, maybe towards a future where women will not be treated as objects. 
The treatment is smooth, flat, and the pastel colors highlight the contrast between the artificial aspect of advertising imagery and the humanity of all women. K's mouth is shut, but she smiles discreetly and her silence speaks volumes."
(Caroline Bourgeois, from the catalogue of the La Pelle / Luc Tuymans exhibition at Palazzo Grassi, Venice, 2019)

E1.3.A.4.1.e_REF2_PH CRITIQUE DU MATÉRIALISME, Edward Hopper, painting time that stretches out

Drugstore, 1927: By painting a display of laxatives, Hopper makes us think about the multiplier effect of consumerism.

Gas, 1940: Is the subject of this painting oil or the gas station attendant? Perhaps Hopper is interested in the symbol of Pegasus, who in mythology gave birth to a fountain from which the muses came to drink. Pegasus, under the yoke of materialistic society, has become the symbol of the mobile oil company. The oil that lubricates cars is the subject of a new mythology in the USA. 

Automat, 1927: Automat is the name given to the vending machines installed in restaurants in Hopper's time. It shows how society can dehumanize the individual in the name of the triumph of technology.

New York Movie, 1939: Like the other paintings about entertainment (The Sheridan Theatre, 1937; Two on the Aisle, 1927; First Row Orchestra, 1951; Intermission, 1963), it shows an empty stage, a hollow entertainment to fill the void. In the half-light, is the New York movie usher a Eurydice waiting to return to the light?
(Didier Ottinger on Les chemins de la philosophie, broadcast on France Culture on 20/12/2018)

Listen to the podcast: https://www.radiofrance.fr/franceculture/podcasts/les-chemins-de-la-philosophie/edward-hopper-peindre-le-temps-qui-s-etire-2566341

E1.3.A.4.1.e_REF3_CUR "THE VITRINES [Show Cases], led to the series of "Store Fronts," which Christo principally developed in the United States, with the exception of these two Store Fronts, built to scale in France, as well as a few smaller-scale projects. The first storefronts were realized with recycled material. Subsequently. their elaboration became more sophisticated, with Christo giving them a minimalist appearance that reflected the work of American artists of the time, while simultaneously contrasting this with a critical, offbeat approach. These monumental realizations function according to the same principle as the first Show Cases: transparent walls are covered with paper or fabric, then enhanced by an internal light source."
(Curatorship: Centre Pompidou)

E1.3.A.4.1.e_REF4_CUR WE MUST PRACTICE ACTIVE VIGILANCE TO CHALLENGE THE MANIPULATIONS OF CONSUMER SOCIETY. Understanding its mechanisms helps free us from conditioning, rather than ignoring them and assuming we're immune.

On Fuse, we often illustrate ideas with advertisements, offering a dual perspective: 1) we highlight references to concepts also—and more profoundly—explored in the humanities, and 2) we remind ourselves that advertising, even when invoking noble concepts, ultimately aims to sell.

E1.3.A.4.2. WILL YOU EVER BE ABLE TO TASTE THE DISPOSITION THAT LEADS YOU TO LOVE AND CHERISH? "Will you ever be, O my soul, good, simple, one, naked and more apparent than the body that surrounds you? Will you ever be able to taste the disposition that leads you to love and cherish? Will you ever be satisfied, without need, without desire, without having to expect your pleasure from what is animate or inanimate; without having, to make it last longer, to expect your pleasure from time, from a place, from a country, from a more favorable air, and from a better understanding between men? But will you be satisfied with your present condition, will you rejoice in all that is happening to you now, will you convince yourself that everything is good for you, that everything comes to you from the gods, and all that it pleases them to send to you, and all that they will have to assign to you for the salvation of the perfect being, good, just, beautiful, who creates all, holds all, contains and understands all that dissolves to give birth to other similar things? Will you ever be such that you can live in the company of gods and men without complaining about them and without giving them cause to accuse you?
(Marcus Aurelius, Meditations, Book X, I, 180)

E1.3.A.4.2_REF1_PH “LEARNING TO WAIT […]
Perplexity is an appropriate response to this ground whose groundlessness is like an abyss from which all things spring forth. But to call this ground the “Sublime” or “One Mind,” “Emptiness,” or “God” is to risk being ensnared once more by Mara. For rather than resting in the boundless unknown that animates perplexity, we return to the safe bounds of a concept that we believe we understand. Instead of embracing the ineffability of our existence, we scramble for the consolation of a manageable religious or philosophical idea. One must be prepared to relinquish even the minimal consolation of a “groundless ground.”
You just wait in the abyss of perplexity without expecting anything. You open yourself to the uncanniness of what unfolds without construing it as this or that. You abandon any notion of a result of such practice. In exhorting his followers to “kill Buddha,” Lin-chi insists that we rid the mind of every image or idea we have ever had of awakening. “If you seek Buddha,” he declares, “you’ll be held in the grip of the Buddha-Mara.” However noble, refined, and erudite one’s idea of Buddha, it can never be more than a collage of memories and concepts drawn from your own and others’ past experience. It encloses you in the realm of what is already known.
Instead of settling into a calm awareness of the effortless unfolding of experience, part of us strives to be already one step ahead of ourselves. Mara is impatient. He is never content to sit still and wait. His goal is always to be elsewhere. Like a print which is slightly out of register, our wraithlike double strains to slip off into an alternative world. The unease of such restlessness lies in this rebellious longing to be elsewhere when it is impossible to be anywhere but here. In making a concerted effort to remain here by returning again and again to the present moment, it is as though we are deliberately provoking the devil. 
The forces that Mara summons to strike back are sensual desire, ill-will, restlessness, torpor, and doubt. These hindrances “lead away from nirvana” by destroying the calm, focused awareness of what is unfolding here and now. One moment we are mindfully attending to the breath or asking “What is this thing?” only in the next to be besieged by an erotic fantasy or a furious resentment. Or our equanimity is rattled by a visceral anxiety; or we slump into a mire of lethargy; or niggling doubts resurface to mock our convictions and aims. Once possessed by these demons, the mind quickly loses its natural suppleness and radiancy and becomes brittle, dull, and unfocused. Buddha describes such hindrances as “encirclers of the mind,” comparing them to giant creepers that bind themselves round other trees, making their hosts “bent, twisted and split.” Once more we find ourselves enclosed in Mara’s snare.
Just as a waiter attends to the needs of those at the table he serves, so one waits with unknowing astonishment at the quixotic play of life. In subordinating his own wants to those of the customer, a waiter abandons any expectation of what he may be next called to do. Constantly alert and ready to respond, the oddest request does not faze him. He neither ignores those he serves nor appears at the wrong time. He is invisible but always there when needed. Likewise, in asking “What is this thing?” one does not strain ahead of oneself in anticipation of a result. One waits at ease for a response one cannot foresee and that might never come. The most one can “do” is remain optimally receptive and alert.
We hate waiting. […] Instead of regarding it either as an affront to one’s dignity or a waste of time, waiting can be seen as a cipher of nirvana. Since life is ultimately a situation over which we have no control, waiting is a response that accords with its fleeting and unreliable nature. The practice of waiting is to learn how to rest in the nirvanic ease of contingent things. Yet waiting is not passive inaction any more than emptiness is nothingness. As an alert stillness that cradles perplexity, it is the ground from which we can respond in unpredictable ways to life’s unfolding and the inevitable encounter with others.”
(Stephen Batchelor, Living with the Devil, 2004)

E1.3.A.4.3. "I KNOW NOT ANYTHING MORE PLEASANT, OR MORE INSTRUCTIVE, THAN TO COMPARE EXPERIENCE WITH EXPECTATION, or to register from time to time the difference between idea and reality. It is by this kind of observation that we grow daily less liable to be disappointed. You, who are very capable of anticipating futurity, and raising phantoms before your own eyes, must often have imagined to yourself an academical life, and have conceived what would be the manners, the views, and the conversation, of men devoted to letters ; how they would choose their companions, how they would direct their studies, and how they would regulate their lives. Let me know what you expected, and what you have found. At least record it to yourself before custom has reconciled you to the scenes before you, and the disparity of your discoveries to your hopes has vanished from your mind. It is a rule never to be forgotten, that whatever strikes strongly, should be described while the first impression remains fresh upon the mind."
(James Boswell, Boswell’s Life of Johnson, 1791)

E1.3.A.4.3_REF1_CUR OFTEN THE EXPERIENCE IS VERY DIFFERENT FROM WHAT WE EXPECTED. Sometimes it's disappointing because we expected something else; sometimes we're pleasantly surprised. Claudio Tozzi allows us to experience the second type of surprise, the pleasant one, and in order to delay our satisfaction - and perhaps encourage us to reflect on the mechanism of expectation - he uses an original stratagem: From a distance, we see a booth with the tempting slogan "See the nude". But on the stand, all we see is a shiny metal cylinder and scribbles on the surface where it's laid out. When we get closer, however, the installation fulfills its promise: when viewed from close up, the curvature of the metal cylinder's surface reorganizes the scribbles and reflects the image of a naked woman on its surface.

where genesis perfects itself. [...]

E1.3.A.4.5. "AVOIDING THINKING ABOUT BETTER FUTURES
When the iPhone 13 was released in the United States, it sold out in a matter of hours (Swingle 2021). Is it possible that people's obsessions with the latest and greatest upcoming technology can cause them to enjoy their current models less? Across three studies, O'Brien (2022) tested whether knowing about an upcoming product upgrade dampens the enjoyment people gain from the current version. In one study, MTurkers played a novel video game; some participants simply saw a flyer for the video game they were about to play, while others saw a flyer for a forthcoming version of this game or for an unrelated forthcoming video game (n’s ≈ 269). Participants enjoyed playing the game significantly more when they were not made aware of the newer, better version of the game. The author successfully replicated this pattern in two additional studies examining enjoyment of a virtual reality game (n’s ≈ 104) and an immersive virtual city tour (n’s ≈ 244).

In sum, this single set of studies provides evidence for the hypothesis that remaining in the dark about exciting future technologies may help us enjoy the present versions more—though it remains to be seen whether this strategy can be harnessed in everyday life."
(Dunigan Folk and Elizabeth Dunn, How Can People Become Happier? A Systematic Review of Preregistered Experiments, 2023)

Read the full article: https://www.annualreviews.org/doi/10.1146/annurev-psych-022423-030818#abstractSection

E1.3.A.4.6. "TO BECOME INTIMATE IS TO BECOME VULNERABLE NOT ONLY TO WHAT I WANT AND DESIRE IN MY LIFE, BUT TO THE FEAR I HAVE OF MY DESIRE BEING MET.
“This is the paradox of longing: Because longing can be an addiction, because no active addict ever wants to give up their addiction — or can without a great deal of suffering — it can be terrifying and almost unbearably vulnerable to surrender to an intimacy so amply fulfilling that it leaves nothing to long for. And yet in that vulnerability lies our power and our freedom to transform a relationship from a tether of dependency into a slender cord of grace.”
(Maria Popova commenting on a passage form David Whyte’s Consolations II, in the Marginalian, 2024)

Read it in The Marginalian: https://www.themarginalian.org/2024/12/03/david-whyte-consolations-intimacy/?mc_cid=9bee2e7557&mc_eid=0b6483decb

E1.3.A.4.6.a INTIMACY IS THE ART AND PRACTISE OF LIVING FROM THE INSIDE OUT. “Intimacy is presence magnified by our vulnerability, magnified by increasing proximity to the fear that underlies that vulnerability. Intimacy and the vulnerabilities of intimacy are our constant, invisible companions, yet companions who are always wishing to make themselves visible and touchable to us, always emerging from some deep interior, to ruffle and disturb the calm surface of our well apportioned lives. Intimacy is a living force, inviting me simultaneously from the inside as much as the outside. Something calling from within that wants to meet something calling in recognition from without. Intimacy is the art and practise of living from the inside out. […]
Our need and our fear of intimacy is felt through an ever present almost volcanic force emerging from some unknown origin inside us, exhibiting to all and sundry, our previously hidden unspoken desires, flowing out against all efforts to the contrary, through our unconscious and conscious behaviours. […]
To become intimate is to become vulnerable not only to what I want and desire in my life, but to the fear I have of my desire being met. […]
Intimacy cannot occur without a robust sense of vulnerability, and is tied to the sense of being pulled along in the gravitational field of any newly felt openness. In that new openness we feel as if we are pulled through the very doorway of our needs for something we desire deeply but cannot fully identify, partly because what we are about to identify is intimately connected with our own ability or inability to love. […]
Intimacy is intimately related to our sense of having been wounded, and the startling intuition that my way forward into life, or into another person’s life will be through the very doorway of the wound itself. Intimacy invites me to learn to trust the way being wounded has actually made me more available, more compassionate and possibly more intimate with the world, by being opened in ways I never realised it was possible to be open… Intimacy is always calibrated by the letting go of or the taking on of fear. Almost always our fear is experienced as an intimate invitation to understand and feel fully our particular form of woundedness.”
(David Whyte, Consolations II, 2024)


E1.5.F.7_REF1_PH.wE1.3.A.4.

E1.3.A.5. SØREN KIERKEGAARD WARNS US ABOUT LIVING OUTSIDE THE PRESENT MOMENT, STATING, "ONLY HE WHO IS PRESENT TO HIMSELF IS HAPPY.” A memory of a past situation we know we can't recreate becomes a source of sadness. A future hope that might come true brings joy, while a hope we know is impossible leads to sadness.

E1.3.A.5_REF1_PH "THE UNHAPPY ONE IS ABSENT. BUT ONE IS ABSENT WHEN LIVING IN THE PAST OR LIVING IN THE FUTURE. The form of expression is important, for it is evident, as philology also teaches us, that there is a tense that expresses present in the past, and a tense that expresses presence in the future; but the same science also teaches us that there is a pluperfect tense in which there is no present, as well as a future perfect tense with the same characteristics. These are the hoping and remembering individuals. Inasmuch as they are only hoping or only remembering, these are indeed in a sense unhappy individuals, if otherwise it is only the person who is present to himself that is happy. However, one cannot strictly call an individual unhappy who is present in hope or in memory. For what one must note here is that he is still present to himself in one of these. From which we also see that a single blow, be it ever so heavy, cannot make a person the unhappiest. For one blow can either deprive him of hope, still leaving him present in memory, or of memory, leaving him present in hope."
(Søren Kierkegaard, Either/Or: A Fragment of Life, 1843)

E1.3.A.5_REF1.1_PH "CONSIDER FIRST THE HOPING INDIVIDUAL. WHEN, AS A HOPING INDIVIDUAL (AND OF COURSE TO THAT EXTENT UNHAPPY), HE IS NOT PRESENT TO HIMSELF, HE BECOMES UNHAPPY IN A STRICTER SENSE. An individual who hopes for an eternal life is, indeed, in a certain sense an unhappy individual to the extent that he renounces the present, but nevertheless is strictly not unhappy, because he is present to himself in the hope and does not come in conflict with the particular moments of finitude. But if he cannot become present to himself in hope, but loses his hope, hopes again, and so on, then he is absent from himself not just in the present but also in the future, and we have a type of the unhappy. Though the hoping individual does not hope for something that has no reality for him, he hopes for something he himself knows cannot be realized. For when an individual loses hope, and instead of becoming a remembering individual, wants to remain a hoping one, then we get this form.
Similarly if we consider the remembering individual. If he finds himself present in the past, strictly he is not unhappy; but if he cannot do that but remains constantly absent from himself in a past, then we have a form of the unhappy.
Memory is pre-eminently the real element of the unhappy, as is natural seeing the past has the remarkable characteristic that it is gone, the future that it is yet to come; and one can therefore say in a sense that the future is nearer the present than is the past. That future, for the hoping individual to be present in it must be real, or rather must acquire reality for him. The past, for the remembering individual to be present in it, must have had reality for him. But when the hoping individual would have a future which can have no reality for him, or the remembering individual remember a past which had had no reality for him, then we have the genuinely unhappy individuals. Unhappy individuals who hope never have the same pain as those who remember. Hoping individuals always have a more gratifying disappointment. The unhappiest one will always, therefore, be found among the unhappy rememberers."
(Søren Kierkegaard, Either/Or: A Fragment of Life, 1843)

E1.3.A.6. FINDING HAPPINESS IN WAITING MIGHT BE A FORM OF ESCAPISM and a dead end for lasting joy. Yet, we must acknowledge that there's genuine pleasure in anticipation when it heightens our self-awareness and reaffirms our desires.

E1.3.A.6_REF1_CUR FROM A DISTANCE IN THE EXHIBITION, WE SEE A PHOTOGRAPH OF A SUNSET. It almost looks like a window in the museum wall, allowing us to contemplate this moment that always radiates peace. In front of a sunset, we wait without waiting. We wait for the sun to set, but we're not impatient for it to disappear; on the contrary, we appreciate every moment of the disappearing movement.
As we get closer to the work, we realize that there's something written in the middle of the picture. You have to get close enough to read it: "What we did was stand around and wait for something to happen". Then we realize that the sunset is a metaphor for presence in the world, which everyone has already experienced. Can we live more often in joyful contemplation - instead of being absent from the present, waiting for something else to happen?

E1.3.A.6_REF2_PH I WOULD SAY THAT LIFE ITSELF IS CONSTANTLY SEEKING OUT ALIENATION "Living beings devoid of initiative, curiosity and imagination have to compete for the ecological niches they occupy; those who, on the contrary, exercise their initiative have at their disposal the ecological niches they invent. And the interesting thing is that, from the outset, ecological niches are built by living beings. I'd like to make a brief comment on this subject: in my opinion, we talk far too much about “alienation”. I would say that life itself is constantly seeking out alienation. It constantly tears itself away from its natural ecological niche, rushing into the adventure of a new environment. When the gene invents a membrane or when we put on a coat, it's a phenomenon of alienation from nakedness. I regard talk of alienation as dangerous and ridiculous. Quite simply, it's about the adventure of a living being seeking out a new, foreign environment, and taking the risk. This plays a vital role in evolution towards a higher stage, but it does not entirely explain evolution towards this stage; it is an element that plays a decisive role in it. [...]
Every single living being seeks a better world, or at least seeks to stop or slow down its movement where the world is better. And this extends from the amoeba to us. Our desire, our hope, our utopia is always the discovery of an ideal world. In a way, it's inscribed in us by Darwinian selection. And we mustn't ignore this. It's simply not true that we are 'shaped' by the environment. We seek out an environment and actively shape it. [...] We constantly seek to reform and modify our immediate environment, our more distant environment and, finally, the whole world. Our will thus plays a key role in the course of history. [...] We are seeking beings; life is skeptical - from the Greek to examine, to seek - from the outset. It is never quite satisfied with the conditions it finds itself in. And it is daring in its adventures.”
(Karl Popper, The Future is Open, 1983)

E1.3.A.6_REF2.1_CUR JENNY HOLZER CHALLENGES THE PASSERBY BY DECLARING THAT ALIENATION PRODUCES EITHER ECCENTRICS OR REVOLUTIONARIES. This recalls Karl Popper's view that living beings shape their environment by carving out niches under the pressure of survival: "It is simply not true that we are 'shaped' by the environment. We seek an environment and we actively shape it. [...] We are constantly trying to reform and modify our immediate environment, our more distant environment, and ultimately the whole world. Our will thus plays a key role in the course of history. [...] We are searching beings, and life is skeptical - from the Greek, to examine, to seek - from the very beginning. It is never completely satisfied with the conditions in which it finds itself. And it is bold in its adventures." *
Karl Popper deduces from the study of the living world that lack of freedom stimulates human creativity and can be positive in this sense; Jenny Holzer, on the other hand, makes us aware of the suffering associated with alienation.
* (Karl Popper, The Future is Open, 1983)

 E1.3.A.6_REF2.2_CUR "THIS PAINTING EVOKES A KEY EPISODE IN PSYCHE'S LIFE. As she seeks to reconquer Eros (the god of love), Aphrodite imposes many challenges on her. As a final task, she asks Psyche to deposit a fragment of Persephone's beauty in a golden box and then forbids Psyche to open it. The latter manages to accomplish this task, but succumbing to her curiosity and hoping to be able to use this fragment to win back Eros, she opens the box and is plunged in a deep sleep similar to death, from which Eros will save her by throwing his arrows at her."
(Wikipedia)

"Living beings devoid of initiative, curiosity, imagination must compete for occupied ecological niches; those who, on the contrary, exercise their spirit of initiative have at their disposal the ecological niches they invent. The interesting thing is that from the start ecological niches are built by living beings. I would also like to make a small remark on this subject: in my opinion there is too much talk of « alienation ». I would say that life itself is constantly seeking alienation. It is constantly tearing itself away from its natural ecological niche by rushing into the adventure of a new environment. When the gene invents a membrane or when we put on a coat, it is a phenomenon of alienation from nudity. I consider the discourse on alienation to be a dangerous and ridiculous discourse. It is quite simply the adventure of the living which seeks a new and foreign setting, assumes the risk and throws itself in it."
(Karl Popper, The Future is Open, 1983)

E1.3.A.6_REF2.3_PH ROBERT WRIGHT ON WHY BUDDHISM IS TRUE
"What has often been translated as the suffering evoked by the Buddha can actually be translated as "dissatisfaction" - which is consistent with the law of evolution (see Darwin and Popper on alienation as an incentive to innovation). It would be the human condition, according to Buddhism, to experience dissatisfaction - but practice would enable us to welcome our desires without despair.
(Robert Wright on Why Buddhism is True, Philosophy Bites)

Listen to the episode: http://philosophybites.com/2018/05/robert-wright-on-why-buddhism-is-true.html

E1.3.A.6_REF3_PH WAITING COULD BE A SPACE THAT OPENS UP FOR THE CONTEMPLATION OF OUR DESIRE. If boredom rushes into this space, it might signal that our love for reality is too weak (or ill) and that we've failed to recognize our true desire. If mimetic desire rushes in instead, it's because we've fallen for the illusion of need—which is always social, as Epicurus tells us. We must then seek out the desire that is uniquely our own.
(Pierre Schindler)

E1.4.C.5.wE1.3.A.

E1.3.A.7. TAOIST THOUGHT: AN ALTERNATIVE TO THE DESIRE-BOREDOM CYCLE—THE IN-BETWEEN
François Jullien proposes that ambiguity is the in-between of unresolved opposites. It eludes ontology because the in-between doesn't "exist." The Greeks, focused on opposites, overlooked this in-between. The ethical consequence? A dilemma between insatiable desire and boring satiety. Socrates and Callicles exemplify this conflict. Trapped in the empty/full dichotomy, the Greeks failed to consider the space between desire and satiety. This impasse led them to conceive of an "other" life.

Plato may have created metaphysics because he couldn't conceptualize the in-between. The Zhuangzi offers an alternative: "Pour without filling, draw without exhausting." The Tao is this indefinite in-between where one can "evolve." This thinking resolves the Greek antagonism through the logic of transition, akin to breathing. Taoism encourages us to avoid extremes, to dwell in the in-between of activity—thus escaping the desire/satiety dilemma.
(Pierre Schindler)

E1.3.A.7. TAO OFFERS AN ALTERNATIVE TO THE DESIRE-BOREDOM OSCILLATION: THE IN-BETWEEN
François Jullien suggests that ambiguity is the in-between of undecided opposites. It escapes ontology because the in-between does not "exist. The Greeks, fixated on opposites, neglected this in-between. The ethical consequence: the dilemma between insatiable desire and dull satiation. Socrates and Callicles illustrate this conflict. The Greeks didn't think through the in-between of desire and satiation, stuck in the empty/full alternative. This impasse led them to the idea of "another" life.

Plato is said to have created metaphysics because he could not think through the in-between. The Zhuangzi proposes an alternative: "Pouring without filling, drawing without exhausting. The Tao is that indeterminate in-between where we can "evolve. This way of thinking unravels the Greek antagonism through the logic of transition, like breathing. Taoism invites us to avoid extremes, to remain in the in-between of activity, thus escaping the dilemma of desire/satiety.
(Pierre Schindler)

E1.3.A.7_REF1_PH TAOISM OFFERS AN ALTERNATIVE TO THE DESIRE-BOREDOM OSCILLATION: THE IN-BETWEEN.

"BETWEEN (VS. BEYOND)

Ambiguity is that which cannot be decided—not because opposites coexist within it, as in a "mixture," but because these opposites lack clear boundaries. We might say that ambiguity is the "in-between" of their non-separation, or that the space of ambiguity itself is this "in-between." Consider the emotions of joy and sadness: the most profound feelings, such as those evoked by a landscape, resist division into these opposing states. This isn't because they blend or oscillate between the two, but because they don't give rise to such distinctions at all.

However, we must acknowledge that this in-between space is, paradoxically, a non-space. It can only be conceived in relation to what it's between, possessing no inherent qualities. It has no determination, essence, or "property" of its own, thus eluding ontological grasp. In the strictest sense, the in-between doesn't exist. How, then, can we speak of it except in negative terms?
This is why the Greeks, with their eyes fixed on opposites, [...] which alone are determined, which alone are defined in marked features, in terms of "being," did not recognize any capacity for the "in-between," inconsistent as it appears; and consequently did not conceive of it. They have thought of it only in terms of its boundaries, and these alone are qualified. Thus they have thought of presence or absence, the one forming an alternative with the other and contradicting it; or either emptiness or fullness, lack or satisfaction - but they have neglected the in-between, or the middle, which escapes characterization. What, then, is the ethical consequence, a priori tragic, of trapping life in this dilemma? Either, says Socrates (Gorgias, 492), life is seen as a leaky barrel that never stops leaking and into which we are condemned to keep pouring in order to satisfy this unfulfillable desire: we then live under the constant pressure of desire without ever satisfying it. Or we can plug the hole of desire, and the barrel will be full, with no more to pour into it: we'll finally live in peace, liberated, free from need and satisfied. In this last state, in this perfect rest, we're no longer alive, Callicles rebels, we lack the desire that makes us stand up, that makes us passionate, and we're reduced to the state of 'stone, no longer striving. But in the other state, replies Socrates, always pouring without ever saving, never satiated, we live no more than the miserable life of a 'plover,' a bird that immediately evacuates what it has just ingested.... Strangely enough, but as a logical consequence of ontology, the Greeks thought of nothing but these opposites: either emptying or filling. But they didn't think of the space between desire and satiation; they didn't deploy a median time of our experience-between the screaming emptiness on the one hand and the satiated fullness on the other. They have held our existences, and philosophy in their wake, in the cruelly tightening grip of desire, which, once fulfilled, becomes disgust: either desire makes us suffer from its lack, or satiation wearies us and turns into disappointment. Hence the difficulty of getting out of this contradiction, which Pascal would see as our 'condition': either we still desire, so we're still suffering; or our desires are satisfied, but we're bored. Or, as Augustine once put it: 'If I say you will not be satisfied, it means you will be hungry; if I say you will be satisfied, I fear your disgust.' And so, unable to see a way out of this dilemma, unable to escape this vicious circle of either lack or lack of desire, the Greeks, or at least 'Platonism,' were unable to see any consistency in this life; and had to postpone 'life' to 'another' life. - But does the idea of paradise itself escape this conundrum?


In other words, I no longer believe that it was through asceticism and renunciation, his desire having been corrupted, as Nietzscheanism would have it, that Plato, the handsome, proud aristocrat, shifted his thinking to the 'out there,' ekei, splitting the world and fleeing the here and now. But rather for lack of a tool: the thought of the in-between, which in his eyes could not find anything to hold on to, could only be a matter of mixing or 'blending,' of blurring identities, of confusing them, of disentangling them, of 'saving oneself' from them. [...]
Unable to think of the "in-between", the metaxu, they had to think of the meta of 'meta-physics'. This is where they feel comfortable and where we can build. [...]
Now, a formula from Zhuangzi is enough to protect us from this mirage of the tranquil state reached in the 'beyond' [...]. It protects us from the brutal light of the antipodes. It discreetly pushes us out of this rut and keeps us in an endless in-between. 'Pour without ever filling, draw without ever exhausting,' says one conceptual figure to another, 'obscure authenticity' (if it is ever possible to translate this evasiveness of substance) to 'fruitful wind' (Zhuangzi, ch. 12). Because the Tao is 'without demarcation,' that which stands out, emerges, and 'shines' is not the Tao; and similarly, "the word that distinguishes does not reach'. In other words, the most distinctive is also the most arbitrary. The Tao, the 'Way', on the other hand, is that in-between, which does not allow itself to be defined on either side, but which never ceases to allow passage: where we can therefore 'evolve' at ease. [...]

To evolve (you in Chinese) is the verb that means precisely - by eliminating extremes and no longer assuming a beginning or an end - a departure or a destination, a variation in between, without fixation or focus, renewing itself by alternation and not exhausting itself. The sage 'evolves' in the Tao, it is said, 'like a fish in water.'
So, if we continue the tripartite exchange, if we allow this other voice to shine through in that tense, unfinished dialogue between Socrates and Callicles - one of the most violent on the Greek stage - the Taoist thinker, by the very sweep of his formula, loosens this alternative, undoes or rather relaxes this antagonism: of the barrel full to the brim that finally frees us from desire, or of the pierced barrel that constantly rekindles it. To pour but not to fill, to draw but not to exhaust, through the alternation of weight produced by the sentence, one verb responding to the other and both holding back from the end - this formula is enough to make us understand the logic of transition, but isn't it already, more elementarily, that of breathing, which keeps us alive? For in its constant exchange, in which one stage already calls for the next and protects against the unsustainable, disjunctive states of complete emptiness and fullness, isn't breathing what actually exists in the in-between of activity - the tension that regenerates? The in-between that Chinese thought prioritized is the most resolute argument against being stuck in one or the other of the opposing positions of all-consuming activity or inert rest. Greek philosophy, as we can see, gave priority to visual perception, to distinguishing, assigning and constructing the object, but not to breathing (because it was too rooted in the vital, not cerebral enough?). 'Let's not give in to the fascination of the extreme,' says the Taoist, 'as if we could finally discover the truth in it' (or, if it remains tied to the extreme, then let's challenge the truth). In other words, let's not naively believe that we can gain knowledge by touching the end. Neither that of emptiness, which calls for relentless pouring, nor that of fullness, which is as restful as if we were dead. Let's avoid the critical stage, on either side, and leave some 'leaway' or reserve - the bottomless bottom of the Tao - on both sides. By staying within the limit, by not tipping over into one or the other determination, by neither 'leaving' nor 'sticking', bu ji bu li, as the Chinese wonderfully (and so simply) put it, i.e. By not 'getting stuck' and by not depriving yourself, but by illuminating this in-between of activity, you'll avoid hypostasizing one or the other as an alternative: both the greedy hunger of desire (Callicles) and the satiety of rest (Socrates); you'll escape the pain of deprivation as well as the disgust of fulfillment."
(François Jullien, Chinese Thought versus Philosophy, 2015)

E1.3.A.8. “HOW TO WAIT BETTER: THE PSYCHOLOGY OF MISSING AND WITHSTANDING ABSENCE.
With its fusion of frustration and hope, waiting is one of the most singularly maddening human experiences, and one of the great arts of living. To wait for something is to value it, to want it, to yearn for it, but to face its absence, its attainment forestalled by time and circumstance. All true waiting — which is different from abstinence, delayed gratification, and other forms of self-discipline — has an element of helplessness to it and is therefore training ground for mastering the vital, incredibly difficult balance of control and surrender that gives shape to our entire lives.

Because, as Tom Waits so unforgettably observed, the way we do anything is the way we do everything, our style of waiting is a miniature of our style of living: There is impatient and petulant waiting; there is waiting with the humility that while we may be worthy of the object of our hope, we are not entitled to it or to the mercies of time; there is waiting with an open heart and a willingness to be surprised, for the wait itself may reveal something we did not yet know about ourselves that might change our desire for the awaited outcome. (“I said to my soul, be still and wait without hope,” T.S. Eliot wrote knowing this, “for hope would be hope for the wrong thing.”)
At its core, waiting is a frustrated relationship between desire and time — a surplus of desire with no temporal agency over its fulfillment. In that sense, it is the opposite of boredom — another singularly maddening experience, marked by total temporal agency hollowed of desire.”
(Maria Popova, The Marginalian)

Read it in The Marginalian: https://www.themarginalian.org/2024/08/27/adam-phillips-better-waiting/?mc_cid=9d078af8ad&mc_eid=0b6483decb

E1.3.A.8_REF1_PSI WHEN YOU ARE WAITING FOR SOMEONE YOU ARE LOOKING FORWARD TO SEEING, CAN YOU DO ANYTHING OTHER THAN WAIT? 
“The child experiences the mother’s absence as a withholding of something that could be given. The mother forbears to come into presence, and the child can’t help but react, respond, mobilize something in or of himself to manage the withdrawal in the first instance, often rage. Everything depends in this developmental story on how mother and child deal with the absences. It is in one sense a matter of time, of how long the wait is before the mother reappears. “It is a matter,” Winnicott writes in *Playing and Reality*, “of days, or hours, or minutes. Before the limit is reached the mother is still alive; after this limit has been overstepped the mother is dead.” That is to say it feels to the child that the mother he has in his mind has died; and/or he has killed her in his mind out of rage at her absence. In this story it is all about what happens in the absence — what Winnicott calls the “gap” — and, more pragmatically, what can be done in, or with, the gap. […]
When you are waiting for someone you are looking forward to seeing, can you do anything other than wait? And can you enjoy them when they finally arrive? How you wait is who you are, and everything depends on your sense of an ending.”
(Adam Phillips, On Getting Better, 2021)

E1.3.B. DESIRING NOTHING BUT REALITY?
Upon reflection, it appears that a prerequisite for joy is to embrace existence as it is—with all its flaws—in the present moment, and to cease yearning for anything beyond what's real.

E1.3.B.1. NIETZSCHE IDENTIFIES THE FUTILITY OF DESIRE THAT EXHAUSTS ITSELF SEEKING AN UNATTAINABLE OBJECT, AS WE TIRE OF EVERYTHING — EVEN OF OURSELVES.
Nietzsche's work presents the concept of "wisdom of love" in contrast to "love of wisdom." He argues that it's too easy to maintain a perpetually frustrated relationship with wisdom. Instead, we should embrace love now, despite imperfection.

E1.3.B.1.1. THIS PHOTO TAKEN FROM THE LIBRARY OF LOVE — a collection of artists' books on love featuring 200 artists' work — highlights a loving gesture that acknowledges reality's imperfections. It reminds us of Nietzsche's view that the wisdom of love surpasses the love of wisdom. Pursuing wisdom in a state of constant frustration is futile; we must love now, imperfections and all. Cristina Suzuki illustrates this by describing a simple act as a loving gesture towards reality: placing a wedge under a wobbly furniture leg to live harmoniously with imperfection.

E1.5.E.2.2_REF2_PH.wE1.3.B.1.

E1.3.B.1.2. "THE WISE MAN IS THE ONE WHO DOESN'T NEED TO LIE TO HIMSELF, TELL HIMSELF STORIES, OR EVEN BE LUCKY TO BE HAPPY. It's as if he's self-sufficient; that's why he's free. But the truth is, he's got enough of everything, or anything is enough for him. This distinguishes him from the ignorant, for whom everything is never enough. The ignorant wants to take, to possess, to keep, whereas the wise man is content to know, to taste (sapere, from which sapiens comes, means "to have taste"), to enjoy, and to rejoice. He's less a scholar than a connoisseur. Less an expert than an amateur (in the double sense of the word: one who loves, one who does not make a profession of it). Less an owner than a free man (the jivan mukta of the Orient: the living free man). The wise man is without master, but also without dominion, if not over himself, without church, without belonging, without ties, without attachments (what he loves, he neither possesses nor is possessed by). Even his happiness does not belong to him: it is only a gust of joy in the great wind of the world. He is deprived of himself and everything. Maybe that's why he's happy: because he no longer needs to be. And wise: because he no longer believes in wisdom.
(André Comte-Sponville, Philosophical Dictionary, 2013)

E1.3.B.1.2_REF1_PH "WISDOM 
IT'S ONLY AN IDEAL, FROM WHICH IT'S ALSO IMPORTANT TO FREE ONESELF. WISDOM DOESN'T EXIST (THERE IS NO IDEAL); THERE ARE ONLY WISE PEOPLE, AND THEY ARE ALL DIFFERENT, AND NONE OF THEM BELIEVE IN WISDOM. 
A successful life? That can only be decided after it's over, and what's the point? The truly wise man doesn't care about achieving anything: his life is no more or less important to him than anyone else's. He is content to live it, and he finds in it a sufficient satisfaction, which is the only true wisdom. 'For me, I love life,' Montaigne said. That's why he was wise, because he didn't wait for life to be lovable (easy, pleasant, successful...) before he loved it. A question of temperament? A question of doctrine? Probably a little of both. We are more or less gifted for life or wisdom; those who are less gifted need to philosophize more, that's my lot. But no one is absolutely or completely wise: everyone needs to philosophize, if only to free himself from philosophy itself, as well as from wisdom. Wisdom can only be attained by ceasing to believe in it. The wisest man in the world, a clot or a virus is enough to drive him mad. A sorrow greater than his wisdom is enough to make him unhappy. He knows this and accepts it in advance. His failures are no less true than his successes. Why should they be less wise? Wisdom, true wisdom, is not an all-risk insurance policy, nor a panacea, nor a work of art. It's about resting, joyfully and freely, in the truth. A knowledge? That is indeed the meaning of the word, both Greek (sophia) and Latin (sapientia). But it's a very special kind of knowing. 'Wisdom can be neither a science nor a technique,' said Aristotle; it is less about what is true or effective than about what is good, for oneself and for others. Is it knowledge? Perhaps it is. But it's knowledge about life, a way of life."
(André Comte-Sponville, Philosophical Dictionary, 2013)

E1.3.B.1.2_REF1.1_PH "WISDOM [...]
THE GREEKS DISTINGUISHED BETWEEN THEORETICAL OR CONTEMPLATIVE WISDOM (SOPHIA) AND PRACTICAL WISDOM (PHRONESIS). But one is hardly possible without the other, or rather true wisdom would be the combination of the two. It can be recognized by a certain serenity, but even more by a certain joy, a certain freedom, a certain eternity (the wise man lives in the present: he feels and experiences, as Spinoza said, that he is eternal), a certain love... Of all the goods that wisdom provides for the happiness of life as a whole," said Epicurus, "friendship is by far the greatest" (Capitular Maxims, XXVII). Self-love, fear, absence or lies no longer stand in the way. There is only the joy of knowing: there is only love and truth. That's why we all have our moments of wisdom, when love and truth are enough for us. And of folly, when they tear us apart or fail us. True wisdom must be disillusioned with itself, as we see in Ecclesiastes. It is no longer an ideal; it is a state, always approximate, always unstable (it is eternal, like love, only as long as it lasts), it is an experience, it is an act. It is not an absolute, despite the Stoics (we are more or less wise), but a maximum (as such relative): it is the maximum of happiness, in the maximum of lucidity. It depends on the situation of this or that person, on the abilities of this or that person (wisdom isn't the same in Auschwitz or Paris, for Etty Hillesum or Cavaillès) - in short, on the state of the world and of oneself. It's not an absolute; it's the way, always relative, of inhabiting reality, which is the only true absolute. This wisdom is worth more than all the books written about it, which run the risk of separating us from it. It's up to each of us to invent our own. Montaigne said: "Even if we can learn from the knowledge of others, we can be wise only from our own wisdom" (Essais, 1, 25).
(André Comte-Sponville, Philosophical Dictionary, 2013)

E1.3.B.1.2_REF1.2_PH “THE MARK OF A NOBLE SOUL.—A noble soul is not that which is capable of the highest flights, but that which rises little and falls little, living always in a free and bright atmosphere and altitude.”
(Friedrich Nietzsche, Human, All Too Human II, Aphorism 397, 1878)

E1.3.B.1.2_REF2_PO “LIVE, YOU SAY, IN THE PRESENT.
Live only in the present.

But I don’t want the present, I want reality.
I want the things that exist, not the time that measures them.

What is the present?
It’s something in relation to the past and the future.
It’s something that exists by virtue of other things existing. I want only reality, the things themselves, without any
     present.
I don’t want to include time in my awareness of what exists. I don’t want to think of things as being in the present; I want
     to think of them as things.
I don’t want to separate them from themselves, calling them
     present.

I shouldn’t even call them real. 
I shouldn’t call them anything.

I should see them, just see them,
See them until I can no longer think about them,
See them without time or space,
See with no need of anything besides what I’m seeing. 
This is the science of seeing, which is no science at all.”
(Fernando Pessoa, 19 July 1920 - signed as Albert Caeiro)
