E1.3.B.1.3. "CHILDREN'S HEAVEN. - The happiness of children is as much a myth as the happiness of the Hyperboreans spoken of by the Greeks. If happiness really dwells on earth," said the Greeks, "it must surely be as far away from us as possible, perhaps over there, at the ends of the earth. People of a certain age think the same way: if man can really be happy, it must surely be as far away from our age as possible, at the limits and beginnings of life. For some, through the veil of this myth, the appearance of the child is the greatest joy they can have: he himself enters the courts of heaven when he says: "Let the little children come to me, for theirs is the kingdom of heaven. In one way or another, the myth of the children's heaven has currency wherever in the modern world there is anything like sentimentality.
(Friedrich Nietzsche, Human, Too Human III, Aphorism 265, 1878)

E1.3.B.1.3.a. NIETZSCHE WOULD PROBABLY HAVE JUDGED THIS PASSAGE BY JOHN BANVILLE TO BE A FANTASY OF CHILDHOOD RECREATED BY A SENTIMENTAL ADULT.
“Happiness was something else in my childhood. Back then it was practically a simple matter of accumulation, of taking things - new experiences, new emotions - and applying them like tiles to the walls of what would one day become the beautifully finished pavilion of my being. And disbelief, too, was responsible for much of the happiness. I'm talking about the euphoric inability to fully believe in the simple luck I had.”
(John Banville, The Sea)

E1.3.B.2. IN CAMUS' WORK, THERE IS NO "NO" WITHOUT A PRIOR "YES." HE CONCLUDES THAT MAN'S REVOLT AGAINST HIS CONDITION IS POSSIBLE BECAUSE HE HAS PREVIOUSLY AFFIRMED LIFE. THIS APPROACH HELPS CAMUS AVOID THE PITFALL OF REVOLUTION.

We are in love with a perishable truth. In "Nuptials at Tipasa," he writes: "What use have I for a truth that is not to my measure?"

E1.3.B.2_REF1_PH UNDER THE SIGN OF THE SUN: CAMUS' PHILOSOPHY ARTICULATES THE TENSION BETWEEN THE LOVE OF LIVING AND THE DESPAIR OF LIVING, UNDERLINING THE IMPORTANCE OF MAINTAINING THIS BALANCE FOR AN AUTHENTIC, ETHICAL EXISTENCE.
“From Nuptials to The Stranger, the sun is transformed from a luminous metaphor to that of death. Over this period, Camus became aware of what the world was like. He learned that misfortune exists, that life is tragic. From then on, the sun could no longer be just a sign of joy. The Stranger is part of the cycle of the Absurd, which is not Camus' last word, since he had already thought of the next cycle of writing, which would be the Revolt: the love of living does not exclude the despair of living. This tension is constitutive of human life. You don't want to lock yourself into either love or despair; you have to live as a pendulum. In the first case, we risk losing all lucidity; in the second, we condemn ourselves to sterility. It's an ethical duty to maintain the tension between the two poles, as he develops in The Rebel. Despair stems from our finitude, and also from the fact that we can never quite live up to what life or beauty has to offer. We need to turn it into something that will give us an even greater love of life.
See the extract from the preface to Betwixt and Between, written in 1937, in which Camus takes stock of his philosophy: “Poverty... stupidity”. The tension between love and despair was already present in Betwixt and Between. It's surprising that Camus says: 'to correct a natural indifference, I was placed halfway between misery and the sun'; What is this indifference to be corrected? Is it the trace of his strange mother's indifference, which he had to work on after childhood? The older he gets, the more he discovers his mother's dignity, despite her crushing condition (deaf, illiterate, poor), as well as her courage and tenderness that were never expressed.
He also works on his 'excessiveness'. He is not someone who is satisfied with what he is. He wrote: 'you must learn to be born to others' - for Camus, we need to found our ethics, and it's in destitution that we see what's essential. In the preface to Betwixt and Betweent, he wrote that he understood the meaning of life in poverty.
Of his father, whom he never knew, he remembers the sentence he uttered during the Moroccan war, restraining violent action out of vengeance against the other side: “A man can be restrained”.
Camus defined himself as an artist in reaction to the 19th-century sacralization of the artist as a being inspired by the gods. For Camus, the artist can never be silent, and must understand everything - in the sense of entering into the mystery - putting it into context. 'The sun taught me that history is not everything' - existence finds its completeness beyond politics. 'Art is not everything for me, at least not as a means' - he says in his notebooks in 1935; would he say it again in his Nobel Prize acceptance speech in Sweden, in which art is also an answer?
The meditation on death, also present in Nuptials in Tipasa, is not there to extinguish everything, but on the contrary to enhance life, to celebrate it more: If I think I have so much happiness swimming in the light, it's because I have a body and I'm mortal. There's no love of living without despair of living; it's thanks to this body that I'm in communion with the world and with others.
Philosophy doesn't come first, you have to live first. “Live Tipasa, write Tipasa, think” - live first, write and think later: Camus theorizes his existence in a second stage. 
'Lucidity is the wound closest to the sun', writes René Char, whom Camus defines as ‘essential’.
The last lines he wrote in the manuscript of The First Man before his accident: 'A pure passion for living, confronted with total death, felt today life, youth, beings slipping away from him, without being able to save them in any way, and abandoned only to the blind hope that this obscure force which for so many years had lifted him above the days, nourished him without measure, equal to the harshest of circumstances, would also provide him, and with the same tireless generosity as it had given him his reasons for living, reasons to grow old and die without revolt.' 
It's not the meaning of life, but reasons for living.
He also writes: 'We'll have to resign ourselves to dying obscure to ourselves.'"
(Agnès Spiquel on Les chemins de la philosophie, France Culture, 06/26/2019)

Listen to the podcast: https://www.franceculture.fr/emissions/les-chemins-de-la-philosophie/sous-le-soleil-34-camus-sous-le-signe-du-soleil

E1.3.B.2_REF1.1_PH "CAMUS WROTE: “'IN THE MIDDLE OF WINTER, I DISCOVERED WITHIN MYSELF AN INVINCIBLE SUMMER'.
Our usual idea is that summer follows winter, joy follows sorrow, good weather follows rain, you're feeling bad... don't worry, one day it'll get better. That's what the usual wisdom tells us; basically, we need to learn to put everything into perspective. I think that's not good enough - what Camus invites us to do is open up a whole new perspective: to learn to recognize, in the very midst of winter, in the midst of what seems frozen and lifeless, the dazzling emergence of summer. 
We are rarely told about this possibility: discovering that there are no happy or unhappy events in themselves, but that in each of them, in each moment of our lives, there is a deeper dimension. This is what great literature is all about. I had this experience when my grandmother, whom I loved dearly, died. She was a very old lady at the time, and we all knew that the end was approaching. One morning, my aunt called to warn me that Grandma was living out her final hours. I arrived at the hospital, and the family was there, talking in hushed tones in the corridor. The situation was heavy - every time a family member entered the room, they did so with a somber step and leaved overwhelmed. I, too, entered the room apprehensively, taking the time to sit down and stand there with my grandmother. To my own surprise, what I experience is the kindness of my grandmother, who has accompanied me throughout my childhood. I stayed in the room all day, bathed in a deep, poignant peace with the feeling that I was supporting her through her ordeal. Of course I was very sad that my grandmother was dying, but in the midst of that winter I could feel the beauty of the love in which she had never ceased to bathe me, I could feel that this love was even appearing with a particularly poignant intensity. That moment was one of the most important in my life, and it gave me a strength and confidence that I still carry with me today. But how do you make this discovery on a daily basis? It's not just a question of finding a ray of sunshine in the middle of winter, but of discovering an invincible summer that will never desapear again, a summer that without being able to mask or replace winter, remains there within us. Like a forgotten friend - this forgotten friend lives in your very being, in your heart, in your chest, in every cell of your body. He's the memory of everything you've learned in life; he's the love your grandmother or anyone else gave you. Now you can reach out to it: you were my first ally, and I want to make a pact of friendship, peace and trust with you. I entrust myself to you in complete confidence, I entrust myself to that part of me which is greater than myself.”
(Fabrice Midal, 3 minutes of philosophy to become human again)

Listen to the podcast: https://www.franceculture.fr/emissions/3-minutes-de-philosophie-pour-redevenir-humain/deja-essaye-deja-echoue-peu-importe-essaie-encore-echoue-encore-echoue-mieux-resolution-de-samuel

E1.3.B.2_REF1.2_PH "THIS WORLD IS RADIANT WITH BEAUTY. THIS WORLD IS ALSO CAPABLE OF BONE-CHILLING BRUTALITY and the small, corrosive daily cruelties that salt our days with sorrow. For a sensitive person to live with the duality, to keep the light aflame without turning away from the darkness that needs illumination, may be the most difficult thing in life — and the most rewarding."
(Maria Popova)

E1.3.B.2_REF2_PH "REVOLT 
Resolute or violent opposition: refusal to obey, submission, resignation. The word is used above all, and increasingly, to denote an individual attitude (for collective revolts, we'd rather speak of riots, uprisings, revolutions...). This is because Camus visited the concept. What is a rebel? A man who says no. But if he refuses, he doesn't give up: he is also a man who says yes, from his first movement" (The Rebel, I). Yes to what? To his rebellion, to his struggle, to the values that underpin it or spring from it. The rebel, in the etymological sense, makes a U-turn. 'He has walked under the master's whip. Now he's facing it' (ibid.). This 'passionate affirmation' distinguishes revolt from resentment: 'Apparently negative because it creates nothing, revolt is profoundly positive because it reveals what is always to be defended in man' (ibid.). Here we emerge from solitude: revolt is 'a commonplace that establishes the first value of all human beings. I rebel, therefore we are' (ibid.). It's the way out of nihilism. It's where we come out of rebellion, or rather, it's where, without coming out of it, we include it in a greater whole, which is life, in a higher value, which is love. Rebellion is 'the very movement of life'; it is 'love and fruitfulness, or it is nothing' (ibid., V). 'Man is the only creature who refuses to be what he is' (ibid, Introduction). We must at least accept this. So revolt is only a passage, between the absurd and love, between no and yes. That's why we have to enter it, because 'the absurd is only a starting point' (ibid.). That's why you can't get out of it completely. It's like a ternary rhythm. First the world's no to man (the absurd); then man's no to the world (revolt); finally the great yes of wisdom or love ('all is well' in Sisyphus). But this yes does not cancel any of the two nos that precede and prepare it. It prolongs them. It accepts them. This is true of the absurd, which is only a starting point, but which remains undented (wisdom is neither justification nor hermeneutics). This is even more true of revolt. To say yes to everything, which is the only wisdom, is also to say yes to the no of revolt and of man".
(André Comte-Sponville, Philosophical Dictionary, 2013)

E1.3.B.2_REF3_PH REVOLT IS A MAD GENEROSITY THAT GIVES EVERYTHING TO THE PRESENT, REFUSING INJUSTICE AND EMBRACING LIFE IN ALL ITS FULLNESS, CHOOSING HUMANITY AND FAITHFUL EARTH OVER DIVINITY, AND FINDING IN THIS STRUGGLE A STRANGE JOY THAT HELPS ONE LIVE AND DIE.
"This insane generosity is the generosity of rebellion, which unhesitatingly gives the strength of its love and without a moment's delay refuses injustice. Its merit lies in making no calculations, distributing everything it possesses to life and to living men. It is thus that it is prodigal in its gifts to men to come. Real generosity toward the future lies in giving all to the present. Rebellion proves in this way that it is the very movement of life and that it cannot be denied without renouncing life. Its purest outburst, on each occasion, gives birth to existence. Thus it is love and fecundity or it is nothing at all. Revolution without honor, calculated revolution which, in preferring an abstract concept of man to a man of flesh and blood, denies existence as many times as is necessary, puts resentment in the place of love.[...]

The only original rule of life today: to learn to live and to die, and, in order to be a man, to refuse to be a god.

At this meridian of thought, the rebel thus rejects divinity in order to share in the struggles and destiny of all men. We shall choose Ithaca, the faithful land, frugal and audacious thought, lucid action, and the generosity of the man who understands. In the light, the earth remains our first and our last love. Our brothers are breathing under the same sky as we; justice is a living thing. Now is born that strange joy which helps one live and die, and which we shall never again postpone to a later time. On the sorrowing earth it is the unresting thorn, the bitter brew, the harsh wind off the sea, the old and the new dawn."
(Albert Camus, L´homme révolté)

E1.7.D.6.19.b.wE1.3.B.2

E1.3.B.3. THE MODALITY THROUGH WHICH PROUST EXPERIENCES THE JOY OF REALITY WITH THE MEMORY OF THE MADELEINE IS FIRMLY ANCHORED IN THE PRESENT, AS CLEMENT ROSSET SUGGESTS.
"This does not mean that reality can only be perceived through memory. The access of reality to consciousness, which occurs after the fact, is not a memory. It is the real that comes to consciousness, rather than returning to it: not insofar as it is past, but insofar as it is real, and even if its reality thus manifests itself only through a gap between the then real and its present perception. In such "returns from the past," which are actually arrivals of the real, it is not the past that returns, but the real that appears. 
Proust's famous descriptions provide an eloquent and ambiguous illustration here. In three key moments of In Search for Lost Time, the narrator associates the arrival of an indescribable happiness with the return of the past to consciousness: when he recognizes in the perfume of a "madeleine" dipped in tea the taste of certain moments of his childhood in Combray [...].
This key passage in Le Temps retrouvé sums up what was closest to Proust's heart, at least philosophically: I am happy when I experience something that, being related to the past as well as to the present, has nothing to do with the present that is passing - and therefore has nothing to do with the present and its poverty (Baudelaire would add: with reality and its banality). 
Proust's error here is not so much the privileging of the imagination at the expense of the appearance of reality, a privileging he himself explicitly acknowledges: 
"So often in the course of my life, reality had disappointed me because, at the moment I perceived it, my imagination, which was my only organ for the enjoyment of beauty, could not apply itself to it, by virtue of the inescapable law that one can only imagine what is absent" - but to take as memory the simple appearance of reality, its "revelation," to take as second representation what is in fact first representation. Proustian "reminiscence" is not a memory for the simple reason that it does not reproduce an image. A memory repeats a representation, which is why it can, in a pinch, constitute a "re-presentation." But Proust's memories are nothing of the sort. Proust's memory remembers nothing, precisely because it is - and this is where its interest lies - the first "representation" of the real, that is, its inaugural presentation, which signals the emergence of a certain real on the surface of consciousness. [...] 
It's a pity that Proust, so attentive to this emergence of the real on the surface of consciousness, thought he had to dismiss it as soon as it was born, and based the intimacy of his happiness on his ability to evacuate it.
(Clément Rosset, The Real Treatise on Idiocy, 1977)

E1.3.B.3.1. “TRANSFORM EVERY PHENOMENON INTO AS MANY ENIGMAS OF HAPPINESS OR GAIETY”.
(Marcel Proust)

E1.3.B.3.2. THE EXERCISE OF MEMORY TESTIFIES TO EXISTENCE AND ITS PREROGATIVE. "One is entitled to wonder about the origin of such a keen interest in a past that is generally felt in the form of indifference, if not vague annoyance, when it was present. This fastidiousness of memory can only be interpreted as a sign of recognition, in both senses of the word, of existence as such, of the interest inherent in every existence. The exercise of memory thus testifies to existence and its prerogative, even if it only becomes aware of this after the fact: its mania for exactitude, for conformity with past reality, the belated admission that "things that exist are important," to borrow a word from Claudel in Le Pain dur."
(Clément Rosset, Force majeure, 1983)

E1.3.B.3.3. NICOLAS GRIMALDI SHARES CLEMENT ROSSET'S ANALYSIS: PROUST REMEMBERS WHAT HE EXPERIENCED THROUGH INVOLUNTARY RECOLLECTIONS, BECAUSE AT THE MOMENT OF EXPERIENCING THESE MOMENTS, EXPECTATION DISTRACTED HIM FROM THE PRESENT. Of waiting, there can be no memory. Proust's involuntary memories are more intensely experienced through recollection than when they were experienced in the present. They are of the order of re-presentation.

E1.3.B.4. A JOY TAKEN MORE FROM THE FACT THAT EXISTENCE IN GENERAL EXISTS RATHER THAN FROM THE FACT OF ONE'S PERSONAL EXISTENCE

E1.3.B.4_REF1_PH “THE EMOTION OF A MAN WHO HAD PREPARED FOR THE EVENTUALITY OF SOME HAPPINESS AND SUDDENLY FINDS HIMSELF CONFRONTED WITH SOMETHING VERY DIFFERENT AND ALSO MORE INTENSE: Not only is his satisfaction not imperfect, but still it outweighs any forecast in perfection. I would happily say that the sculptor's chisel caught the racer's gaze at the precise moment when the latter ceases to think of his happiness at having won to think of something totally different: to the general joy which consists in living, in realizing that the world exists and that one is part of it. [...] The joy in question here is in no way distinguished from the joy of being alive, from the simple pleasure of existing (even if the analysis of this pleasure reveals a rather complex rejoicing: a pleasure rather taken from the fact that there is existence in general than from the fact of one's personal existence)."
(Clément Rosset, The Major Force, 1983)

E1.3.B.4_REF2_PO "I KNOW, YOU NEVER INTENDED TO BE IN THIS WORLD.
But you’re in it all the same.

So why not get started immediately.

I mean, belonging to it.
There is so much to admire, to weep over.

And to write music or poems about.

Bless the feet that take you to and fro.
Bless the eyes and the listening ears.
Bless the tongue, the marvel of taste.
Bless touching.

You could live a hundred years, it’s happened.
Or not.
I am speaking from the fortunate platform
of many years,
none of which, I think, I ever wasted.
Do you need a prod?
Do you need a little darkness to get you going?
Let me be as urgent as a knife, then,
and remind you of Keats,
so single of purpose and thinking, for a while,
he had a lifetime."
(Mary Oliver, The Fourth Sign of the Zodiac, part 3, 2014)

E1.3.B.5. ISN'T ROUSSEAU'S WITHDRAWAL FROM THE WORLD DISGUISED AS A HAPPY ENDING? "Meditating on the dispositions of my soul, in every situation of life which I have passed through, I am extremely surprised at the disproportion I perceive between the different combinations of my destiny, and the habitual conceptions of good and evil with which they have affected me. The several intervals of my short-lived prosperity have scarcely left one permanent agreeable remembrance of the manner in which I enjoyed, them; while, on the contrary, during the greatest miseries of my life, I constantly experienced the most tender sentiments, which, though affecting, were delicious; these, shedding a salutary balm on the sorrows of my wounded heart, seemed to convert grief into enjoyment, and the amiable remembrance of those pleasing sensations, frequently returns, unaccompanied by those sorrows that formerly attended them. It appears to me that I have given more into the pleasure of my existence, and more truly lived, when the peculiarity of my fate had concentrated all my feelings, as it were, about my own heart, than when they evaporated outwardly, in pursuit of those objects which merit so little in themselves, yet constitute the whole felicity of those we think most happy.

When all was right around me, when I was content with everything, and satisfied with the sphere I was to occupy, I filled it with my affections, while my expansive soul, extending itself to other objects, was perpetually attracted by a thousand different inclinations, and by amiable attachments, which continually employed my heart: in these situations I forgot myself in some measure, thinking principally on what was foreign to me, and experiencing in the continual agitation of my feelings, all the vicissitude of earthly things. This exquisite sensibility left me neither inward peace, nor outward repose; happy in appearance only, I had not a single sentiment that could have borne the proof of reflection, or with which I could truly have been content. Never was I perfectly satisfied either with others or myself; the tumult of the world made me giddy, solitude wearied me, I perpetually wished for a change of situation, and met with happiness in none. Meantime, I was entertained well, and caressed everywhere; I had not a single enemy, none who bore me ill-will, none that were envious, everyone sought to oblige me, and I frequently had it in my power to oblige others. Without wealth, employment, or flatterers; without any display or reputation of particular talents, I enjoyed every advantage that could have resulted from them all, and saw no one in a situation which I thought preferable to my own. What then was wanting to make me happy? I cannot answer this; but I am fully sensible I was not so. What additional misfortune is wanting at this time to make me the most miserable of mankind? Nothing that human malice can add; yet in this deplorable situation, I would not change my being and destiny with the most fortunate among my persecutors; but would rather be myself in the midst of these misfortunes, than any of those who figure in all the glare of prosperity. Cut off from every connection, my soul must nourish itself on its own substance; but she is not exhausted, though I ruminate on a void, if I may be allowed the expression, my clouded imagination and faded ideas no longer furnishing sufficient aliment to my heart, while my soul dimmed and obstructed by the weakness of its bodily organs, sinks daily under the weight of them, having no longer strength sufficient to free itself, and dart as heretofore from its aged and feeble covering.

This entering into ourselves, is what we are naturally forced to by adversity, and this, perhaps, renders it so insupportable to the generality of mankind: for my part (who have only weaknesses to reproach myself with) I easily find consolation, for never did premeditated evil approach my heart [...]

It is in this deplorable situation, after long and dreadful agonies, that, instead of despair, which seemed my inevitable portion forever, I once more found serenity, tranquillity, peace, and even happiness; since each day of my life I recall the occupations of the preceding one with pleasure, and desire no variation for tomorrow.

From whence proceeds this difference? From one single circumstance, which is, that I have learned to bear the yoke of necessity without murmuring. Formerly, I was attached to a thousand things, all which connections have been rent asunder successively, and I am reduced to depend on myself, and adopt my own plan for happiness. Now that I am pressed on all sides, I maintain my equilibrium, because, no longer attached to anything, I depend on myself alone."
(Jean-Jacques Rousseau , The Reveries of the Solitary Walker, Eight walk, an anonymous translation to English, published in 1796)

E1.3.C. FULLY EMBRACING REALITY AS A PRODUCT OF CHANCE
An unconditional acceptance of reality rejects the comforting notion of a purposeful nature. Instead, it embraces a universe born of randomness—one where our joy must flourish despite the absence of inherent meaning.

E1.3.C.1. THE UNCONDITIONAL APPROVAL OF THE EXISTING ORDER: This is the amor fati of the Stoics upon which Christian logic has built its doctrine. Is Stoic resignation a path only if one eventually leaves it? Can this philosophy be wise only at its limit, as it tends toward affirmation?

Watch the animation by Massimo Pigliucci, TedEd - The philosophy of Stoicism:
https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=R9OCA6UFE-0

E1.3.C.1.1. STOICISM GUIDES US IN SELF-IMPROVEMENT, TEACHING US TO DESIRE ONLY WHAT WE CAN CONTROL — our judgments, perceptions, and aversions. For elements beyond our control — such as our bodies, power, wealth, and recognition — we learn to accept and align with the natural order.

E1.3.C.1.1.a. "HUMAN LIFE.
Duration: momentary. Nature: changeable. Perception: dim. Condition of Body: decaying. Soul: spinning around. Fortune: unpredictable. Lasting Fame: uncertain. Sum Up: The body and its parts are a river, the soul a dream and mist, life is warfare and a journey far from home, lasting reputation is oblivion.
Then what can guide us?
Only philosophy.
Which means making sure that the power within stays safe and free from assault, superior to pleasure and pain, doing nothing randomly or dishonestly and with imposture, not dependent on anyone else’s doing something or not doing it. And making sure that it accepts what happens and what it is dealt as coming from the same place it came from. And above all, that it accepts death in a cheerful spirit, as nothing but the dissolution of the elements from which each living thing is composed. If it doesn’t hurt the individual elements to change continually into one another, why are people afraid of all of them changing and separating? It’s a natural thing. And nothing natural is evil."
(Marcus Aurelius, Meditations, Book II)

E1.3.C.1.1.a.i. THE STOICS' "SPIRITUAL EXERCISES" EXEMPLIFY HOW PHILOSOPHY CAN BE A DISCIPLINE that begins with mental gymnastics, creates habits, and ultimately transforms both body and mind (aren't they one and the same?)

E1.3.C.1.1.a.i_REF1_PH IT’S MY ROTTEN LUCK THAT THIS HAS HAPPENED TO ME "Be like a headland: the waves beat against it continuously, but it stands fast and around it the boiling water dies down. « It’s my rotten luck that this has happened to me ». On the contrary, « It’s my good luck that, although this has happened to me, I still feel no distress, since I’m unbruised by the present and unconcerned about the future ». What happened could have happened to anyone, but not everyone could have carried on without letting it distress him. So why regard the incident as a piece of bad luck rather than seeing your avoidance of distress as a piece of good luck? Do you generally describe a person as unlucky when his nature worked well? Or do you count it as a malfunction of a person’s nature when it succeeds in securing the outcome it wanted? [...]
Can what happened to you stop you from being fair, high-minded, moderate, conscientious, unhasty, honest, moral, self-reliant, and so on — from possessing all the qualities that, when present, enable a man’s nature to be fulfilled? So then, whenever something happens that might cause you distress, remember to rely on this principle: this is not bad luck, but bearing it valiantly is good luck."
(Marcus Aurelius, Meditations, Book IV, XLIX, 180)

E1.3.C.1.1.a.i_REF2_PH "FORTUNE … FALLS HEAVILY ON THOSE TO WHOM SHE IS UNEXPECTED; the man who is always expecting her easily withstands her. For an enemy’s arrival too scatters those whom it catches off guard; but those who have prepared in advance for the coming conflict, being properly drawn up and equipped, easily withstand the first onslaught, which is the most violent. Never have I trusted Fortune, even when she seemed to offer peace. All those blessings which she kindly bestowed on me — money, public office, influence — I relegated to a place whence she could claim them back without bothering me. I kept a wide gap between them and me, with the result that she has taken them away, not torn them away. [...]
No man has been shattered by the blows of Fortune unless he was first deceived by her favours. Those who loved her gifts as if they were their own forever, who wanted to be admired on account of them, are laid low and grieve when the false and transient pleasures desert their vain and childish minds, ignorant of every stable pleasure. But the man who is not puffed up in good times does not collapse either when they change. His fortitude is already tested and he maintains a mind unconquered in the face of either condition: for in the midst of prosperity he has tried his own strength against adversity."
(Seneca, Consolation to Helvia - his mother)

E1.3.C.1.1.a.i_REF3_PH JE CROIS QU'UNE PENSÉE CONSCIENTE PEUT PÉNÉTRER L'INCONSCIENT SI ELLE EST INTENSÉMENT CULTIVÉE. "There has been a great deal of study by psychologists of the operation of the unconscious upon the conscious, but much less of the operation of the conscious upon the unconscious. Yet the latter is of vast importance in the subject of mental hygiene, and must be understood if rational convictions are ever to operate in the realm of the unconscious. This applies in particular in the matter of worry. It is easy though to tell oneself that such a misfortune would not be so very terrible if it happened, but so long as this remains merely a conscious conviction it will not operate in the watches of the night, or prevent the occurrence of nightmares. My own belief is that a conscious thought can be planted into the unconscious if a sufficient amount of vigour and intensity is pot into it. Most of the unconscious consists of what were once highly emotional conscious thoughts, which have now become buried. It is possible to do this process of burying deliberately, and in this way the  unconscious can be led to do a lot of useful work. I have found, for example, that if I have to write upon some rather difficult topic the best plan is to think about it with very great intensity - the greatest intensity of which I am capable - for a few hours or days, and at the end of that time give orders, so to speak, that the work is to proceed underground. After some months I return consciously to the topic and find that the work has been done. Before I had discovered this technique, I used to spend the intervening months worrying because I was making no progress; I arrived at the solution none the sooner for this worry, and the intervening months
were wasted, whereas now I can devote them to other pursuits. A process in many ways analogous can be adopted with regard to anxieties. When some misfortune threatens, consider seriously and deliberately what is the very worst that could possibly happen. Having looked this possible misfortune in the face, give yourself sound reasons for thinking that after all it would be no such very terrible disaster. Such reasons always exist, since at the worst nothing that happens to oneself has any cosmic importance. When you have looked for some time steadily at the worst possibility and have said to yourself with real conviction, ‘Well, after all, that would not matter so very much’, you will find that your worry diminishes to a quite extraordinary extent. It may be necessary to repeat the process a few times, but in the end, if you have shirked nothing in facing the worst possible issue, you will find that your worry disappears altogether, and is replaced by a kind of exhilaration.[...]"
(Bertrand Russell, The conquest of happiness, 1962)

E1.3.C.1.1.a.i_REF4_PH "THE MEDITATIONS BY MARCUS AURELIUS ARE AN EXERCISE IN SELF-IMPROVEMENT with a specific goal and precise tools. Through repetition, they provoke a series of consequences or demonstrations in the soul. This isn't meditation—it's physical work on the soul, akin to repeating a movement to strengthen a muscle. The soul, being a physical breath, can adopt certain dispositions based on the work imposed upon it. As Marcus Aurelius reminds himself, 'What am I seekink? This or that voluntary disposition'"

E1.3.C.1.1.a.i_REF4.1_PH "THE MARK OF STOICISM IS TO BE OPTIMISTIC AS A MATTER OF PRINCIPLE. WE HAVE ABSOLUTE CONTROL OVER OUR REPRESENTATIONS. There is no evil except moral suffering. As long as we have not committed injustice ourselves, we must consider that we have not suffered evil. Make your soul an impregnable citadel. Marcus Aurelius adds that we can turn any obstacle into a new course of action." For Stoics, the world is a cosmic city in which we all participate, and we are all capable of understanding the same reasoning. Listening to others is a prerequisite for correcting fellow citizens' erroneous beliefs that underpin their actions. For the Stoics, man is by definition a rational being open to propositions that may be true or false. The soul's guiding principle is reason, or daimon. I am intelligence, and the only good that exists is the right disposition of that intelligence. Daimon is divine because reason—logos—is God insofar as it orders the world and gives it its properties. For Seneca, reason is a spark of divinity within us. Logos, or nature, is a disturbing echo in Spinoza. "No one is willingly evil": While Plato believed that seeing the good was enough to do it without involving the will, Marcus Aurelius recognized the resistance we have to acting virtuously, even when we know what virtue is. Stoicism is a practice that uses language to free us from these resistances by identifying them and transforming obstacles into new paths. It changes our approach to reality. The Pensées are an exercise in self-work with a specific goal and precise tools that, through repetition, provoke a series of consequences or demonstrations in the soul. It is not a meditation exercise; it is physical work on the soul. It is like repeating a movement to work a muscle. The soul is a physical entity that can take on a certain disposition depending on the work imposed on it:
1. Reduction to insignificance: For example, when we wage war, it is like a spider catching a fly. This puts things into perspective on a cosmic scale and counteracts the subjective delusion that makes life unbearable.
2. The use of short aphorisms that shake our souls with their impact;
3. Defining things and dividing concepts by breaking them down into their constituent parts to see their essence.
Marcus Aurelius contrasts philosophy, the content of thought weighed down by limiting principles, with rhetoric, the form of persuasive expression. Rhetoric is never an end in itself. The goal is to be happy, which Xenon says is a peaceful life, and this can only be achieved through the acquisition of virtue, a disposition of the soul to do good. Spreading goodness through action means setting an example of virtue for others to follow in order to create a community of minds because harmony of the soul is meaningless without harmony in the world. The goal is not to create oneself because one's identity is important, but rather to become a rational being that does good (the "I" in Pensées pour moi-même) because it contributes to cosmic harmony. This is a philosophical, political, and ethical duty because these spheres are permeable. The tranquility of wisdom is a difficult goal, and the Pensées are a workbook for achieving it. This goal is difficult because it is not a matter of withdrawing from the world; rather, it is a matter of holding all the threads of life at the same time (being an emperor and a philosopher at the same time, according to Marcus Aurelius)."
(Christelle Veillard on the radio program The Stoics or the Art of Evasion, broadcast on Les chemins de la philosophie on France Culture on March 25, 2019)

Listen to the podcast in French: https://www.radiofrance.fr/franceculture/podcasts/les-chemins-de-la-philosophie/le-stoicisme-ou-l-art-de-l-esquive-7842506

E1.3.C.1.1.a.i_REF4.2_PO “THERE IS NO JAIL FOR THE SOUL
There is no jail for the soul,
Which everywhere is free.
One might as well secure the sky
Or chain the open sea.

The body may in bondage sit
Or on a golden throne.
It matters little to the soul,
Whose fate is all its own.

The soul is sovereign in the self,
And ever free to choose.
Without, one may be forced to crawl;
Within, one can refuse.

Without, one can be flayed alive,
But none can touch the soul,
Which, willing only what is good,
Emerges sane and whole.
(Nicholas Gordon, Philosophical poems, 2002)

E1.3.C.1.1.a.i_REF5_PH MONTAIGNE HAS THE SOLUTION AGAINST NIGHT TIME ANGUISH:
Instructing myself betimes to constrain my life, and fit it for
a new state. The true liberty is to be able to do what a man will with himself: "Potentissimus. Potentissimus est qui se habet in potestate [He is most potent who is master of himself."—Seneca, Ep., 94.].»
(Montaigne, Essays, Part III, About Physionomy, Chapter XII)

E1.3.C.1.1.a.ii. IN OUR QUEST TO ABSOLUTELY CONTROL OUR EMOTIONS, don't we risk missing out on a vital part of life?
The virtuous middle ground: Can we guide our emotions without tension?

E1.3.C.1.1.a.ii_REF1_PH CATHERINE WILSON ON EPICUREANISM "The appeal of Stoicism today (as opposed to Epicureanism) can be explained by the high value we place on the ability to control (our lives, our emotions, etc.). In this sense, isn't Stoicism a false way of accepting reality? The Stoic says that we have to learn to accept suffering because it's part of life and we have to brace ourselves - control our reaction. The Epicurean also acknowledges the inevitability of suffering, but welcomes it and sees it in others with greater empathy and recognizes the emotions it provokes. A revival of Epicureanism would therefore depend on a relaxation of the value of "control"."
(Catherine Wilson on Epicureanism, Philosophy Bites)

Listen to the episode: http://philosophybites.com/2016/05/catherine-wilson-on-epicureanism.html

E1.3.C.1.1.a.ii_REF2_PH “FEELING HELPLESS AND CONFUSED IN THE FACE OF RANDOM, UNPATTERNED EVENTS, we seek to order them and, in so doing, gain a sense of control over them,” the great psychiatrist Irvin D. Yalom wrote in his magnificent meditation on uncertainty and our search for meaning. But as our terror of losing control compels us to grasp for order and certainty, we all too often end up creating patterns that ultimately don’t serve us, then repeat those patterns under the illusion of control. These patterns of belief — about who we are, about who others are, about how the world works — come to shape our behavior, which in turn shapes our reality, creating a loop that calls to mind physicist David Bohm’s enduring wisdom: “Reality is what we take to be true. What we take to be true is what we believe… What we believe determines what we take to be true.”"
(Maria Popova, Brain Pickings)

E1.3.C.1.1.a.ii_REF3_PH ACCORDING TO THE STOICS, WE CAN ESCAPE OUR PASSIONS' TYRANNY THROUGH THE PRACTICE OF SPIRITUAL EXERCISES, whereas for Spinoza « we are not a state within a state » and cannot disarm desire by sheer will. While in both thought models, understanding allows us to escape being teared apart by our passions, for Spinoza understanding is not enough. Knowledge needs to be « experienced » as a meditation that allows us to become aware that desire and the common goods of human life are not an end in themselves. This meditation on lived experience makes it possible to establish a hard core that underlies the search for these common goods without being blinded by their representation: We thus no longer see human interactions as the search for honors and power, the search for the means of living as the search for fortune, the search for health as the search for libido. For Spinoza, a partial letting-go may be a consequence of the philosophical process, but it is not a goal in itself.

E1.5.D.3.wE1.3.C.1.1.a.ii_REF3_PH

E1.3.C.1.1.a_REF1_PH IT IS THROUGH THE FORCE OF PHILOSOPHY THAT ONE BECOMES A PHILOSOPHER "The 'spiritual exercises' (a phrase coined by Pierre Hadot to characterize the Stoic exercises of reflection and preparation) are exercises in moral perfection: Philosophy is an asceticism, not a mere theory, which should enable us to live better.
To live in accordance with nature is a maxim interpreted by the Stoic school as living in accordance with the nature of the universe and also with one's own nature as a human being (i.e., in accordance with reason). For Marcus Aurelius, it's both. It's a position in line with the pseudo-Stoicism of amor fati, a formula coined by Nietzsche - but with one important difference - Nietzsche adds to conformity with nature the injunction to love it.
"The duration of human life: a flicker. Its substance: elusive. Sensation: obscure. The body as a whole: quickly rotting. The soul: a whirlpool. Fate: difficult to predict. Reputation: uncertain. In summary, the things of the body flow like a river. The things of the soul are but dreams and smoke. Life is a war and a foreign sojourn, the glory left behind, oblivion. What can make us endure it? Only one thing: philosophy. It consists in protecting one's inner demon from outrages, in being innocent, in being superior to pleasure and pain, in leaving nothing to chance, in accepting events and fate, and above all in waiting for a death that is favorable to thought, since it is nothing but the dissolution of the elements of which every living thing is composed. But if there's nothing to fear, why should we fear change and total dissolution, for this is in accordance with nature. No evil is in harmony with nature.
It is through the force of philosophy that one becomes a philosopher..."
(Jean-Baptiste Gourinat on Les chemins de la philosophie, broadcast on France Culture on 3/27/2019)

Listen to the podcast: https://www.radiofrance.fr/franceculture/podcasts/les-chemins-de-la-philosophie/comment-se-preparer-a-mourir-3129485

E1.3.C.1.1.b. RETROUVONS ICI UN MONTAIGNE STOÏCIEN:
"Chascun est bien ou mal, selon qu'il s'en trouve. Non de qui on le croid, mais qui le croid de soy, est content : et en cella seul la creance se donne essence et verité.
La fortune ne nous fait ny bien ny mal : elle nous en offre seulement la matiere et la semence : laquelle nostre ame, plus puissante qu'elle, tourne et applique comme il luy plaist : seule cause et maistresse de sa condition heureuse ou malheureuse.
Les accessions externes prennent saveur et couleur de l'interne constitution : comme les accoustremens nous eschauffent non de leur chaleur, mais de la nostre, laquelle ils sont propres à couver et nourrir : qui en abrieroit un corps froid, il en tireroit mesme service pour la froideur : ainsi se conserve la neige et la glace."
(Montaigne, Les Essais, Que le goust des biens et des maux despend en bonne partie de l'opinion que nous en avons, I, XL)

E1.3.C.1.1.c. THE STOIC POSITION FORMS THE THIRD MAXIM OF DESCARTES' PROVISIONAL MORALITY in his "Discourse on the Method." This logic holds only if one believes, as Descartes did, that we can change our desires through philosophical self-work.

E1.3.C.1.1.c_REF1_PH "MY THIRD MAXIM WAS ALWAYS TO TRY TO CONQUER MYSELF RATHER THAN FORTUNE, AND TO CHANGE MY DESIRES RATHER THAN THE ORDER OF THE WORLD, and generally to accustom myself to believing that there is nothing that is completely within our power except our thoughts, so that, after we have done our best regarding things external to us, everything that is lacking for us to succeed is, from our point of view, absolutely impossible. And this alone  seemed to me sufficient to prevent me in the future from desiring anything but what I was to acquire, and thus to make me contented. For, our will tending by nature to desire only what our understanding represents to it as somehow possible, it is certain that, if we consider all the goods that are outside us as equally beyond our power, we will have no more regrets about lacking those that seem owed to us as our birthright when we are deprived of them through no fault of our own, than we have in not possessing the kingdoms of China or Mexico, and that, making a virtue of necessity, as they say, we shall no more desire to be healthy if we are sick, or to be free if we are in prison, than we now do to have a body made of a material as incorruptible as diamonds, or wings to fly like birds. But I admit that long exercise is needed as well as frequently repeated meditation, in order to become accustomed to looking at everything from this point of view; and I believe that it is principally in this that the secret of those philosophers consists, who in earlier times were able to free themselves from fortune's domination and who, despite sorrows and poverty, could rival their gods in happiness. For occupying themselves ceaselessly with considering the limits prescribed to them by nature, they so perfectly persuaded themselves that nothing was in their power but their thoughts, that this alone was sufficient to prevent them from having any affection for other things, and they controlled their thoughts so absolutely that in this they had some reason for reckoning themselves richer, more powerful, freer, and happier than any other men who, not having this philosophy, never thus controlled everything they wished to control, however favored by nature and fortune they might be."
(René Descartes, Discours on method, 1637)

E1.3.C.1.1.d. EVERYTHING, WITHOUT EXCEPTION, JOYS AND SORROWS ALIKE, MUST BE WELCOMED WITH THE SAME INNER ATTITUDE OF LOVE AND GRATITUDE. "It is from there that the Stoic conception of amor fati, love of the order of the world, regarded by them as the supreme virtue, is derived. The order of the world is to be loved because it is pure obedience to God. Whatever this universe accords us or inflicts on us, it does so exclusively out of obedience. [...]
All that happens to us throughout the course of our life, having been brought about by the total obedience of this universe to God, places us in contact with the absolute good formed by the divine will; in virtue of this,
everything, without any exception, joys and sorrows alike, ought to be welcomed with the same inward attitude of love and thankfulness."
(Simone Weil, The need for roots, 1943)

E1.3.C.1.1.d_REF1_CUR THE LIBRARY OF LOVE IS A COLLECTION OF ARTISTS' BOOKS. At first glance, the theme of hunting for survival—in accordance with nature's law—may seem an odd choice to illustrate love. However, this selection becomes clearer when paired with Simone Weil's words: "Whatever this universe grants us or inflicts upon us, it does so exclusively through obedience." One might, like Weil, believe in a divine intention behind creation and natural laws, or be agnostic and view these laws as chance occurrences. Either way, it doesn't diminish our ability to love the world and find joy in its enchantment, even when that enchantment includes suffering.

E1.3.C.1.1.d_REF2_PH "IN THE CHRISTIAN MYTH, NATURE IS DESTITUTE. The good is beyond nature. In Buddhism, however, nature is a revelation of the divine. Consequently, Buddhism affirms that the world is sacred-the world with its joys and sorrows. If the divine is everywhere, then eternity is now."
(Joseph Campbell, The power of myth, interview, Épisode 2 - The Message of the Myth - 23'30"-27'30")

E1.3.C.1.1.d_REF3_PH “GOOD PLOUGHLAND.—All rejection and negation betoken a deficiency in fertility. If we were good ploughland, we should allow nothing to be unused or lost, and in every thing, event, or person we should welcome manure, rain, or sunshine.”
(Friedrich Nietzsche, Human, All Too Human II, Aphorism 332, 1878)

E1.3.C.1.2. STOICISM IS THE TYRANNY OF SELF. Nietzsche denounces the Stoic "comedy": "You demand nature conform to the Portico, ensuring no existence but in your image—an eternal, grandiose glorification of Stoicism. With your love of truth, you stubbornly force yourself, with hypnotic fixity, to see nature falsely—in a Stoic way—until you lose the ability to see it otherwise. Some abysmal presumption leads you to embrace the mad hope that by tyrannizing yourselves—for Stoicism is self-tyranny—you can shape all existence."

E1.3.C.1.2_REF1_PH "SO YOU WANT TO LIVE “ACCORDING TO NATURE?” OH, YOU NOBLE STOICS, WHAT A FRAUD IS IN THIS PHRASE! Imagine something like nature, profligate without measure, indifferent without measure, without purpose and regard, without mercy and justice, fertile and barren and uncertain at the same time, think of indifference itself as power – how could you live according to this indifference? Living – isn’t that wanting specifically to be something other than this nature? Isn’t living assessing, preferring, being unfair, being limited, wanting to be different? And assuming your imperative to “live according to nature” basically amounts to “living according to life” – well how could you not? Why make a principle out of what you yourselves are and must be? – But in fact, something quite different is going on: while pretending with delight to read the canon of your law in nature, you want the opposite, you strange actors and self-deceivers! Your pride wants to dictate and annex your morals and ideals onto nature – yes, nature itself –, you demand that it be nature “according to Stoa” and you want to make all existence exist in your own image alone – as a huge eternal glorification and universalization of Stoicism! For all your love of truth, you have forced yourselves so long, so persistently, and with such hypnotic rigidity to have a false, namely Stoic, view of nature, that you can no longer see it any other way, – and some abysmal piece of arrogance finally gives you the madhouse hope that because you know how to tyrannize yourselves – Stoicism is self-tyranny –, nature lets itself be tyrannized as well: because isn’t the Stoic a piece of nature? . . . But this is an old, eternal story: what happened back then with the Stoics still happens today, just as soon as a philosophy begins believing in itself. It always creates the world in its own image, it cannot do otherwise; philosophy is this tyrannical drive itself, the most spiritual will to power, to the “creation of the world,” to the causa prima."
(Friedrich Nietzsche, Beyond good and evil, Aphorism 9, 1886)

E1.3.C.1.3. IS STOIC RESIGNATION A PATH WORTH TAKING ONLY IF WE INTEND TO LEAVE IT? Does all resignation inevitably stem from despair? Could this philosophy be considered wise only at its limits—when it leans towards affirmation, buoyed by an unshakeable hope?

E1.3.C.1.3_REF1_PH "RESIGNATION 
IT IS TO GIVE UP THE SATISFACTION OF A DESIRE, WHICH NEVERTHELESS REMAINS. It is no longer revolt, which says no, nor is it quite acceptance, which says yes. Resignation would rather say « yes but », or « yes in spite of everything », or « too bad », but without quite believing in it. It is like an unfinished, perhaps unfinishable mourning process, which accepts itself as such. It is not wisdom, for lack of joy. It is not - or no longer - unhappiness. It is a kind of dull and comfortable in-between. A double trap. A double failure. Too comfortable to want to get out of it. Too dull for us to enjoy remaining that way. Resignation is often the state of old people, or of those who have grown old before their time. One understands that it displeases. « This word resignation irritates me, said George Sand; in the idea that I have of it, rightly or wrongly, it is a kind of laziness which wants to avoid the inexorable logic of misfortune » (History of my life, X). But it can't do so, or only by habit and renunciation. It is not victory; it is desistance. So it is necessary, sometimes, and insufficient, always. It is like a minimal wisdom, for those incapable of reaching the real thing. Its formula seems to be found, surprisingly, in Gide's Les Nourritures terrestres: « Where you cannot say so much for the better, say so much for the worse. In that attitude lies great promises of happiness ». This is probably an exaggeration, or it presupposes a prior happiness, or an ultimate wisdom, which is no longer resignation but full and complete acceptance. Besides, resignation is only a moment. It is only really worthwhile for those who do not resign themselves to it, or who go beyond it. It is a path only on the condition that one grow out of it."
(André Comte-Sponville, Philosophical Dictionary, 2013)

E1.3.C.1.3_REF1.1_PH "RESIGNATION IS OF TWO SORTS, ONE ROOTED IN DESPAIR, THE OTHER IN UNCONQUERABLE HOPE. The first is bad; the second is good. The man who has suffered such fundamental defeat that he has given up hope of serious achievement may learn the resignation of despair, and, if he does, he will abandon all serious activity. He may camouflage his despair by religious phrases, or by the doctrine thatcontemplation is the true end of man, but whatever disguise he may adopt to conceal his inward defeat, he will remain essentially useless and fundamentally unhappy. The man whose resignation is based on unconquerable hope acts in quite a different way. Hope which is to be unconquerable must be large and impersonal. Whatever my personal activities, I may be defeated by death, or by certain kinds of diseases; I may be overcome by my enemies; I may find that I have embarked upon an unwise course which cannot lead to success. In a thousand ways the failure of purely personal hopes may by unavoidable, but if personal aims have been part of larger hopes for humanity, there is not the same utter defeat when failure comes."
(Bertrand Russell, The conquest of happiness, 1930)

E1.3.C.1.3_REF2_PH WHAT THE TRAGIC PHILOSOPHER DOESN'T 'UNDERSTAND' IS THAT THERE CAN BE A DESIRE FOR SOMETHING CALLED 'NECESSITY' "The idea of necessity, which is neither a demonstrable concept nor a demonstrable vision, constitutes, in the eyes of the tragic thinker, a feeling more akin to a need than to a "conviction of the heart. What the tragic philosopher does not 'understand' is not that other philosophers are too quick to conclude from their desire for being (affirming necessity from the feeling of lack), but rather that there can be a desire for this something called 'necessity'. [...] Against this desire for the necessary, the tragic thinker will oppose his own feeling, which is a desire for unconditional affirmation. There is indeed an antinomy between approval and justification, just as there is between chance and necessity, and for the same reasons. To approve is to deny that 'what exists' must be justified by reason: such justification is potentially negating (to approve only on the condition of justification). For the tragic thinker who affirms chance, the desire for order inherent in the sentiment of necessity is a negating desire, symptomatic of an inability to approve. A fundamental problem of philosophical sensibility, perhaps even of human sensibility in general, once explored by Nietzsche, and one in which the idea of chance - depending on whether it is rejected or affirmed - seems to play a decisive role in the end, beyond any analysis of resentment and guilty conscience".
(Clément Rosset, The Logic of the Worst, 1971)

E1.3.C.1.3_REF3_PH FRANÇOIS JULLIEN SUGGESTS THAT WHILE STOICISM SOUGHT TO RECONNECT WITH WISDOM, IT ACTUALLY INTENSIFIED THE DEMAND FOR LOGICAL COHERENCE WITH NATURE, IMPOSING A MORAL FORCE ON LIFE. This approach resulted in a philosophy that prioritizes consistency and unity, potentially at the expense of the dynamic and ever-changing nature of life. 
"If, then, philosophy, with the Greeks, assimilated itself to knowledge (abandoning 'wisdom' for 'science,' sophia for episteme), fixated itself on truth (as the adequacy of thing and spirit), and thus abandoned life, it did so not out of contempt or rejection of life: for it would have developed, through asceticism, a bad disposition against life, as Nietzsche put it. But she found herself bound to it by the logical bias of chance. It did not choose to abandon life, but because life, even in its metabolism, never ceases to decoincide with itself, as Plato saw so well, it had to denounce this inability of life to be and, for that, to coincide in an essence: it had to think the 'true' life [...] by transferring it to Being and the divine 'beyond', detached from the here and now. Meta-physics - the abandonment of the impossible coincidence of life for the stability of being, with which alone truth can coincide and find its identity - was the construction demanded by the mind. [...]

It's true that one might think that Stoicism, by virtue of the reaction from which it sprang, had already escaped this abstraction: since it supposedly revived wisdom and responded in times of need to the urgent question of 'how to live,' since it also dissociated itself from the thought of Being, which had become detached from life, and sought instead to embrace the logic immanent in life. On the contrary, Stoicism pushed this logical demand for chance to the extreme, thereby accentuating the great imposition of morality on life. For it is no longer simply a matter of approaching and touching (the solid foundation of being, haptein), as Plato conceived it; it is a matter of 'coinciding with' ('nature', homologein), i.e. of constantly accepting the inner adequacy of the world's self-development and showing oneself to be in solidarity with its logic. Once it has been established that all beings are part of the same process of growth (as signified by "nature", phusis), and that this process itself possesses its own rational law of sequence and organization (in which there is indeed 'logic'), it is also legitimate that, in order to coincide with this coherence, not to betray the "cohesion" that makes up the world, but rather to adhere to it, Stoicism can only be, on a daily basis, a coercive training that forces individual affects to submit to the interest of the whole; and therefore, in the name of immanence, its morality can only be expressed in the imperative. Since the ego must act only by bringing its reason into accord with the reason of the universal nature which 'governs the whole' [...], the discipline of desire will consist in refusing to desire anything other than what the nature of the whole wants, and consequently in making every effort to agree joyfully (adsentior, says Seneca) to everything that happens to me, including evil;  and even - supreme coercion - to make such a virtue my only happiness.
Stoicism, taken as a whole (and the very nature of Stoicism is that it can only be taken as a whole), is nothing but a great coincidence machine. [...]

Thought [for the Stoics], therefore, always passes from the same to the same, and is only grasped as a whole and at once, tota simul. Since each part is adapted to the other (nihil est aptius, says Cicero), since there is 'mutual implication' between the elements, since the same necessity pervades and organizes everything, since the same logic extends from tendency to reason, and since there is not the slightest possible discontinuity, it is true that the point of departure should coincide with the point of arrival: that there is no longer, strictly speaking, consequence, but consonance; that there is no longer succession, but always cohesion. In this system, thought doesn't move forward; it can (and must) only go around in circles, confirming and confirming itself; it can only repeat and imprint itself further. It can only be repeated over and over again, the better to constrain the emotions and convince the mind. This construction of Coincidence is a great monument that will remain unchanged through the ages, for what can be added to it? But it is a tomb for life, through its blocade of life, which never ceases to escape and deconcentrate from itself - in order to stay alive.
For such a coincidence to be possible, there must be something sufficiently circumscribed and delimited 'with which' the coincidence can take place. But isn't it precisely the arbitrariness of any system of coincidence, at least in relation to life, to proceed with this division that determines such a vis-à-vis? And first of all, what is this 'I' that would be subject to coincidence? Stoicism endeavored to 'circumscribe' (perigraphein) this coinciding self, isolating it not only from the others, but also from the past self and the future self, from the involuntary emotions resulting from perceived impressions, and also from the continuous flow of events in which this self is swept along. And all this in order to detach from it a pure, truly 'mine' self, alien to everything that influences it, and which, as a 'hegemonic' principle, would be my inner acropolis. But hasn't this self, which has isolated itself so well in order to set itself up as a pure instance of coincidence, which has emptied itself of everything that would be other, in order to belong to itself in its own right, closed itself off in itself, in its inner self, to the point of no longer being able to interact? As a result, does it still retain enough of the idea of a relationship with the 'other' to actually desire coincidence?
Would such a 'self' allow itself to be forcibly extracted from all the 'circumstances' that affect it, and with what could it coincide? [...]
Rather, the peculiarity of the living world is that nothing in its flux and flare, in its flight, allows itself to be sufficiently separated and determined to serve as a concept for any possible adequacy. [...]
We can pray to God, we can fear him, we can worship him, but in relation to 'nature', even if we deify it, we can only wish to support the internal logic of its dynamism, in other words, to agree with it. To elevate it to the status of a great subject, to set it up as the support, even the only support, of reference, to make it the source of adequacy and even of all possible adequacy, and thus to assimilate it to reason, is the hallmark of classical thought and its foundation. In this sense, Spinoza still belongs to this pre-separation thought. 'Agreeing in nature,' convenire natura, is at the heart of his thought, which, as in Stoicism, is based on a generalized equivalence: God is nature (sive), which is also reason - the strength of ethics, as we shall have to show one day, is that it has been able to distribute so well, under its logical apparatus, and almost conceal this great coercion of wisdom."
(François Jullien, De-coïncidence, Where art and existence come from, 2017)

E1.7.C.3.1.d_REF2_PO.wE1.3.C.1.3.

E1.3.C.1.3_REF4_PH "BEYOND CALMING THOUGHTS: ENLIGHTENING THOUGHTS 'THREE GOOD THINGS' - Greatness, tranquility, and sunlight - these three things encompass everything a thinker can desire and demand of himself: his hopes and duties, his claims to the intellectual and moral realm, I might even say his daily way of life and the orientation of the place where he lives. To these three things correspond first thoughts that elevate, then thoughts that soothe, third thoughts that enlighten - but fourth thoughts that participate in these three qualities, thoughts in which all that is earthly is transfigured: this is the realm in which the great trinity of joy reigns."
(Friedrich Nietzsche, Human, Too Human III, Aphorism 332, 1878)

E1.3.C.1.3_REF5_PO “NOT JUST THOSE WHO ENVY AND HATE US
LIMIT AND OPPRESS US; THOSE WHO LOVE US
Limit us no less.
May the Gods grant me, stripped of all
Affections, the cold freedom of the heights
Of nothingness. Wanting little,
A man has everything. Wanting nothing,
He’s free. Not having and not desiring,
He’s equal, though man, to the Gods.”
(Fernando Pessoa, 1 November 1930)

“OF THE GODS I ASK ONLY TO BE IGNORED.
Without good or bad luck, I’ll be free,
Like the wind that’s the life
Of the air, which is nothing.
Hatred and love both seek us out;
Both oppress us, each in its own way.
Those to whom the gods
Grant nothing are free.”
(Fernando Pessoa, A Little Larger Than the Entire Universe Selected Poems)

E1.3.C.2. A SECULAR READING OF AMOR FATI WOULD NOT INTERPRET IT AS RESIGNATION, BUT RATHER AS AN UNCONDITIONAL ACCEPTANCE OF CHANCE DEVOID OF INTENTION. This perspective is proposed by Friedrich Nietzsche in his analysis of humanity's tragic destiny throughout his work, by Albert Camus in "The Myth of Sisyphus," and by Clément Rosset in "The Logic of the Worst." These ideas stem from a tradition predating the “separation of nature and existence” found in Homer's Iliad—with an added dimension of joy. As Stephen Hawking proposes, a universe arising spontaneously from nothingness, without cause, opens up possibilities for wonder and delight.

E1.3.C.2.1. CLÉMENT ROSSET'S UNCONDITIONAL ASSERTION OF CHANCE. 

E1.3.C.2.1_REF1_PH THERE ARE TWO CONTRADICTORY WAYS OF BEING INDIFFERENT: ONE IS TO EXPECT CHANCE WITH CERTAINTY, SINCE EVERYTHING IS CHANCE; THE OTHER IS TO EXPECT NOTHING, SINCE EVERYTHING IS CHANCE. THE INDIFFERENCE OF CELEBRATION, AS OPPOSED TO THE INDIFFERENCE OF BOREDOM. "Pascal, an advocate but an enemy of chance - that is, in a deeper sense, an advocate of a lost nature that he would like to recover - proposed, according to his own logic, a non-celebratory attitude: to live without taking any "part" or "taste" in life.
But here we must distinguish between two different forms of indifference. In fact, there are two contradictory ways of being indifferent: one is to expect chance with certainty, since everything is chance; the other is to expect nothing, since everything is chance. The indifference of celebration, as opposed to the indifference of boredom. Everything here depends on what we value, what we want to happen: if it's being, the world is monotonous, being never happens; if it's chance, the world is a party, chance always happens. The world of celebration is a world of exceptions; the world of boredom is a monotonous world, whose principle of monotony does not derive from a difference in the representation of the world, but from a reversal of expectations: since nothing is the rule, everything becomes similarly exceptional - a thought whose monotony presupposes a sensitized expectation, not to the constant arrival of novelties, but to the vision, through these differences, of the same lack of rules".
(Clément Rosset, The Logic of the Worst, 1971)

E1.3.C.2.1_REF1.1_PH "JOY IN NONSENSE. How can men take joy in nonsense? They do so, wherever there is laughter-in fact, one can almost say that wherever there is happiness there is joy in nonsense. It gives us pleasure to turn experience into its opposite, to turn purposefulness into purposelessness, necessity into arbitrariness, in such a way that the process does no harm and is performed simply out of high spirits. For it frees us momentarily from the forces of necessity, purposefulness, and experience, in which we usually see our merciless masters. We can laugh and play when the expected (which usually frightens us and makes us tense) is discharged without doing harm. It is the slaves' joy at the Saturnalia"
(Friedrich Nietzsche, Humain, All Too Humain, Aphorism 213, 1878)

E1.3.C.2.1_REF2_PH "TRAGIC PHILOSOPHY REGARDS APPROBATION (AND ITS OPPOSITE, SUICIDE) AS THE ONLY ACT WHOSE POSSIBILITY IS LEFT TO THE SUBJECT OF THE ACTION, THE HUMAN BEING - THAT IS, AS THE ONLY FORM OF "ACTION". [...]

Pascal's image takes on its deepest meaning here, because it is the most tragic: man is embarked, in the sense that he is, like a passenger on a long-distance airliner, without possible access to any of the controls. All he can "do" is decide whether or not to be part of the journey, to accept it (which means global approval) or to reject it (which means global disapproval, i.e. suicide). [...]

Most people are content to live without ever taking action, postponing the only form of action recognized by tragic thought. From a tragic perspective, then, only the suicidal and the unconditionally affirmative will have 'acted' in their lives."
(Clément Rosset, The Logic of the Worst, 1971)

E1.3.C.2.1_REF3_PH TRAGIC LAUGHTER IS TAKING PLEASURE IN CHANCE AND CELEBRATING ITS OCCURRENCE. "What makes tragic laughter possible, manifesting a destructive pleasure indifferent to the nature of what is destroyed, is obviously the idea of chance; more precisely, the ability to recognize chance as the anti-principle of all that exists. Only such a recognition makes possible both the vision of an uncompensated disappearance (an emptiness that refers to no fullness) and the pleasure at the spectacle of such a disappearance (which manifests itself precisely in laughter). Tragic laughter, that is, laughter that takes pleasure in chance and celebrates its occurrence through laughter, is therefore completely alien to the universe of meanings, significations, and counter-significations that can be staged [by those who reject chance]: indifference to meaning, but also to nonsense, which is enough to distinguish it profoundly from all other forms of laughter.

Herein lies the great weakness of Stoic and cynical humor, as well as of nonsense humor and Zen humor, as praised by G. Deleuze throughout his Logic of Meaning: that they are conditioned by a demand for order that is considered obvious and necessary in the case of those we are willing to play the joke on. In other words, to be effective only by way of retaliation, they need the interrogation of a third party, an external intervention, to 'show' the substance of their laughter.
(Clément Rosset, The Logic of the Worst, 1971)

E1.3.C.2.1_REF3.1_PH “EXTERMINATING LAUGHTER 
We're going to have to distinguish between two major forms of laughter: one that provides the expected from its laughter; the other that dispenses with it - hence the honest nature of the former, and the scandalous nature of the latter. The former, which finds in irony one of its most common fields of exercise, can be considered as a laugh that “goes a long way”. Long laughter, whose effectiveness is not exhausted by the comic effect, and which extends into the consequences implicitly attached to laughter: the destruction here is compensated for by the a contrario approval of the principles that contributed to the setting up of a comic aggression. Not only did we laugh, but we were right to laugh: in this reason we discover a stable instance that survives the wreckage of what it has just swallowed up. In this way, the ironist, for example, can destroy anything he likes to destroy, but only on condition that he makes clear the ideas in whose name he is acting, the principles on which he bases his executions: he can bring out the grotesque, but in the name of the reasonable; the scandalous, in the name of the tolerable; the nonsensical, in the name of a certain sense. The second kind of laughter, more usually expressed in the form of humor, can be considered, on the other hand, as laughter that runs out of steam: once the comic effect has passed - if, at least, it has succeeded in having an effect - nothing comes to mind that could justify the laughter, offer intellectual consumption any insight into the meaning and scope of the destruction. Short-sighted laughter, therefore, which opens up no perspective, which takes away without giving anything in exchange, and which will often seem frivolous and without scope: from attacking everything indiscriminately, without taking the trouble to organize its attacks into systems that would enable one to spot in them a certain number of themes attacked and, by consequence, a certain number of themes defended, it will often seem, to its contemporaries more particularly focused on this or that target, to be attacking nothing. So it often takes a considerable amount of time to measure its corrosive effectiveness. And yet, with the benefit of hindsight, it appears to be far more deadly than “long laughter”. Only short laughter is, in a way, long-range: in both a chronological and philosophical sense. Chronological: because laughter requires no reference to truths or values that are destined to disappear over time. Philosophical: because it constitutes a more violent assault on any “meaning” than that of long laughter, in that it refuses from the outset any interpretation of the destruction, i.e. any reinvestment of the destroyed meanings in other, less exposed terrain. [...]

What makes tragic laughter possible, manifesting a destructive pleasure indifferent to the nature of what is destroyed, is obviously the idea of chance; more precisely, the ability to recognize chance as the anti-principle of all that exists. Only such a recognition makes possible both the vision of an uncompensated disappearance (an emptiness that refers to no fullness) and the pleasure at the spectacle of such a disappearance (which manifests itself precisely in laughter). Tragic laughter, that is, laughter that takes pleasure in chance and celebrates its occurrence through laughter, is therefore completely alien to the universe of meanings, significations, and counter-significations that can be staged [by those who reject chance]: indifference to meaning, but also to nonsense, which is enough to distinguish it profoundly from all other forms of laughter.

Herein lies the great weakness of Stoic and cynical humor, as well as of nonsense humor and Zen humor, as praised by G. Deleuze throughout his Logic of Meaning: that they are conditioned by a demand for order that is considered obvious and necessary in the case of those we are willing to play the joke on. In other words, to be effective only by way of retaliation, they need the interrogation of a third party, an external intervention, to 'show' the substance of their laughter.

If we never question Diogenes the Cynic or Chrysippus the Stoic, they'll never be able to demonstrate humor. Generally speaking, the weakness of such humor, like that of the laughter described by Kant, stems from the fact that it is a function of an expectation: the role of the third-party questioner, in the case of the Cynics and Stoics, being the symbol of a more fundamental necessity, which is, in the humorist himself, the presence of a prior demand for meaning, indispensable to the appearance of the laughable. Here, the laughable will always be secondary to the initial intuition of a certain laughter, or a certain meaning; moreover, it will have to rely on a certain complicity on the part of others, on the hypothesis of a sensus communis that ultimately joins up with the idea of human 'nature'. The weakness of this laughter can be seen on two levels. In the first place, such laughter is incapable of accessing the thought of chance, and demonstrates in the most obvious way the reasons why it is incapable of doing so: since it declares itself laughing at the thought that order could be a problem, which means that order is that from which only there can be, by way of contrariety, the possibility of the bizarre. In other words, anyone who, when imagining disorder, can only imagine the opposite of order, is admitting that he is, and always will be, unaware of the notions of chance and chaos. Secondly, laughing at the contrarieties of meaning doesn't so much mean ruining meaning as affirming it in extremis and a contrario: as can be seen in many manifestations of Anglo-Saxon nonsense, a model of moral respectability, which often ends up implicitly celebrating an established order, by the very fact that its opposite - nonsense - is deemed hilarious and unthinkable. Hence the remarkable harmlessness of this laughter, which is entertained by nonsense only insofar as it takes nonsense out of the loop, i.e., out of meaning, and ultimately out of seriousness: from the moment it opposes meaning and seriousness, laughter can only provide second-hand disorder, which will moreover often be an alibi.”
(Clément Rosset, Logic of the worse, 1971)

E1.3.C.2.1_REF4_PH AS LONG AS THERE IS NON-TRAGIC HOPE, THERE IS NO HAPPINESS
"HOPE. Pandora brought the jar with the evils and opened it. It was the gods' gift to man, on the outside a beautiful, enticing gift, called the "lucky jar." Then all the evils, those lively, winged beings, flew out of it. Since that time, they roam around and do harm to men by day and night. One single evil had not yet slipped out of the jar. As Zeus had wished, Pandora slammed the top down and it remained inside. So now man has the lucky jar in his house forever and thinks the world of the treasure. It is at his service; he reaches for it when he fancies it. For he does not know that that jar which Pandora brought was the jar of evils, and he takes the remaining evil for the greatest worldly good--it is hope, for Zeus did not want man to throw his life away, no matter how much the other evils might torment him, but rather to go on letting himself be tormented anew. To that end, he gives man hope. In truth, it is the most evil of evils because it prolongs man's torment. [...]"
(Friedrich Nietzsche, Humain, All Too Humain, Aphorism 71,  1878)

E1.3.C.2_REF1_PH DESPAIR (WISDOM OF - )
“I TAKE THE WORD IN A DIFFERENT SENSE: TO DESIGNATE THE ABSENCE, AT LEAST MOMENTARY, OF ALL HOPE, IN OTHER WORDS, OF ALL DESIRE FOR WHAT IS NOT, FOR WHAT WE DO NOT KNOW OR FOR WHAT DOES NOT DEPEND ON US. A state of exception, then, but not without examples. Not because we no longer desire anything - we'd be dead - but because we only desire what is, what we know, what we do. Moments of plenitude: when love, knowledge and action are enough! Moments of serenity. 'There is no hope without fear, nor fear without hope', writes Spinoza (Eth., III, 50, scolie, and def. 13 of affects, explanation). Who can hope for health without fearing illness? Who can hope for victory without fearing defeat? But then serenity, which is the absence of fear, is also the absence of hope. It's a Stoic theme before it's a Spinozist one: the wise man, said Seneca, 'ignores hope and fear' (On the constancy of the wise man, 9). We must therefore 'unlearn to hope', as he said, in order to 'learn to want'. Wisdom of action: wisdom in action. Wisdom of reason: wisdom in truth. Is this just an ideal? Of course it is. But at least we can come close. Spinoza again: 'The more we strive to live under the guidance of reason, the more we strive to make ourselves less dependent on hope and free ourselves from fear' (Eth., IV, 47, scolie). Hence what I have called, to press where it hurts, a 'wisdom of despair'. It's the opposite of faith (which desires what is not, or what it doesn't know). It's the opposite of hope (which desires what isn't or doesn't depend on us). That's why it's the opposite of religion. 'The opposite of despairing is believing', wrote Kierkegaard. You can also turn it around: The opposite of believing is despairing.
