This despair should not be confused with nihilism or inaction. It is not hope that makes us act, but will. It's not hope that makes us live, but love. Look at the Stoics or Spinoza, or, in the East, the Buddha, Krishnamurti or Prajnânpad. The wisdom of pure attention, of righteous action, of lucid acceptance of what is - which alone enables us to confront it without hatred, and to transform it if we can.
'No action,' I'm sometimes told, 'without desire and project.' I agree. But where have you seen that all desire is hope? Sexuality proves the contrary. And that planning and hoping are one and the same thing? Politics, when lucid, proves the opposite. We only hope for what doesn't depend on us; we only project what does. Statesmen know this. It's not a question of selling a dream, as demagogues do, but of forging a will. [...]
'But what about us,' you may ask, 'who are not wise men?' For us, hope continues, and so does fear (or fear, and so does hope)... We have to accept it first, and try to free ourselves from it at least a little. This involves a long process of disillusionment, often painful (hope is the first thing you have to lose in order to free yourself from it), sometimes joyful, invigorating, jubilant (what a joy it is to finally see things as they always have to be seen). It's like a never-ending generalized mourning process, which tends towards joy, as we see in Freud, or feeds on it. That's what authorized me to speak of a cheerful despair. Why should believers have a monopoly on joy? Why should we have to hope to rejoice? That there is, if God does not exist, something despairing in the human condition, is a kind of self-evident fact, since we grow old, since we suffer, since we die. But this self-evident fact has never prevented us from enjoying the present, or acting, or being happy. Quite the opposite is true. How many believers have only waited for happiness after death, or found it here below, as Pascal confessed, 'in the hope of another life'? And how many atheists, on the other hand, know how to enjoy life and its pleasures, how many know how to love it joyfully and desperately, not for what it promised, but for what it is? See Nietzsche, Camus, Rosset. Tragic wisdom: the wisdom of happiness and despair. The two can and must go together. We only hope for what we don't have: the hope of happiness separates us from it. Whereas the person who is fully happy, insofar as we are able, has nothing left to hope for, not even that his happiness will continue (if he hopes it will last, he fears that it will cease: his happiness is already behind him). [...]
Whether we are capable of this, I doubt very much. But it does at least point us in the direction of action rather than hope, of love rather than faith - of desire in action rather than expectation. To live desperately, in the sense in which I use the word, is to live in the present (not in the moment, which is impossible), and this is the truth of living. 
Is it anything other than an ideal? Yes, because at times, we do experience it: in sexuality, contemplation, knowledge or action, for example, when we desire only what is, what we know, what we do or what depends on us. Sometimes, it's of such fullness that there's nothing left, indeed, to hope for - because there's only the present, only reality, only truth, only the whole. These are rare experiences, but they're unforgettable. Experiences of eternity, Spinoza would say, of the kind, as Proust said, that render the idea of death indifferent.”
(André Comte-Sponville, Philosophical Dictionary, 2013)

E1.3.C.2_REF1.1_PH "HOPE IS A CERTAIN KIND OF DESIRE: A DESIRE FOR SOMETHING WE DO NOT HAVE OR THAT DOES NOT EXIST. To hope is to desire without enjoyment. It is also to desire without knowing if the desire will be satisfied. Finally, it is to desire without power, as the satisfaction of the desire does not depend on us. Hope is therefore opposed to will, rational foresight, and love. Will is a desire whose satisfaction depends on us. Rational foresight is when the future is the subject of knowledge or probability calculations. Love is when we desire what is or what we enjoy. This points the way forward: Do not deny yourself hope. Rather, learn to want, to know, and to love.
Hope mainly focuses on the future because it is the object of desire that is most often beyond our enjoyment, knowledge, and action. The past is better known. The present is more accessible. This does not prevent us from hoping for what was ("I hope I didn't hurt him") or what is ("I hope he's recovered"). All we need is to desire something, for it not to depend on us, and for us to be ignorant of what it is. Temporal orientation is less essential to hope than powerlessness and ignorance; no one hopes for what they know or can do. Hope is a sign of weakness. How can it be a virtue? It is the easiest and weakest desire. [...]
"An inconstant joy, born of the idea of something future or past, the outcome of which we doubt to some extent." According to a famous formula in Ethics, "there is no hope without fear, nor fear without hope" (III, 50, scholium, and def. 13 of the affects, explanation). The same doubt necessary for both means they can only exist together. To hope is to fear disappointment, and to fear is to hope for reassurance. If serenity excludes fear, it also excludes hope. I call this cheerful despair, but Spinoza, wiser than I, calls it wisdom or bliss. This theme is Stoic before it is Spinozist: "You will cease to fear," said Hecaton, "if you have ceased to hope." It is also cynical: "Only he who has neither hope nor fear is free," said Demonax. The wise man hopes for nothing; he has ceased to be afraid. He fears nothing because he has ceased to hope for anything. Because he has no desires? On the contrary, it is because he desires only what is—this is no longer hope, but love—or what he can do—this is no longer hope, but will.
One might say that this wisdom is beyond our reach and that hope will always exist alongside weakness, ignorance, and fear. No doubt. Therefore, as I have often said, it is useless to try to rid oneself of all hope; that would make wisdom a new hope, which would immediately separate us from it. Instead, let us develop our power, freedom, and joy. Let us learn to know, act, and love!"
(André Comte-Sponville, Philosophical Dictionary, 2013)

E1.3.C.2_REF2_PH EXISTENCE IS INNOCENT: HOW CAN WE BLAME IT? "Heraclitus looked deeply: he saw no punishment for multiplicity, no atonement for becoming, no guilt in existence. He saw nothing negative in becoming, but rather its opposite - the double affirmation of becoming and the being of becoming, in essence the justification of being. [...]
There is no being beyond becoming, no unity beyond multiplicity; neither multiplicity nor becoming are mere appearances or illusions. But neither are there multiple, eternal realities that would in turn serve as essences beyond appearance. The multiple is the inseparable manifestation, the essential metamorphosis, the constant symptom of the unique. Multiplicity affirms unity; becoming affirms being".

E1.3.C.2_REF2.1_PH "EXISTENCE AND INNOCENCE [...]
Heraclitus denied the duality of worlds, "he denied being itself'. Moreover he made an affirmation of becoming. We have to reflect for a long time to understand what it means to make an affirmation of becoming. In the first place it is doubtless to say that there is only becoming. No doubt it is also to affirm becoming. But we also affirm the being of becoming, we say that becoming affirms being or that being is affirmed in becoming. Heraclitus has two thoughts which are like ciphers: according to one there is no being, everything is becoming; according to the other, being is the being of becoming as such. A working thought which affirms becoming and a contemplative thought which affirms the being of becoming. These two ways of thinking are inseparable, they are the thought of a single element, as Fire and Dike, as Physis and Logos. For there is no being beyond becoming, nothing beyond multiplicity; neither multiplicity nor becoming are appearances or illusions. But neither are there multiple or eternal realities which would be, in turn, like essences beyond appearance. Multiplicity is the inseparable manifestation, essential transformation and constant symptom of unity. Multiplicity is the affirmation of unity; becoming is the affirmation of being. The affirmation of becoming is itself being, the affirmation of multiplicity is itself one. Multiple affirmation is the way in which the one affirms itself. "The one is the many, unity is multiplicity." And indeed, how would multiplicity come forth from unity and how would it continue to come forth from it after an eternity of time if unity was not actually affirmed in multiplicity? "If Heraclitus only perceives a single element it is nevertheless, in a sense, diametrically opposed to that of Parmenides (or of Anaximander). . . The unique must be affirmed in generation and destruction." Heraclitus had taken a deep look, he had seen no chastisement of multiplicity, no expiation of becoming, no culpability of existence. He saw no negativity in becoming, he saw precisely the opposite: the double affirmation of becoming and of the being of becoming - in short the justification of being. Heraclitus is obscure because he leads us to the threshold of the obscure: what is the being of becoming? What is the being inseparable from that which is becoming? Return is the being of that which becomes. Return is the being of becoming itself, the being which is affirmed in becoming. The eternal return as law of becoming, as justice and as being."
(Gilles Deleuze, Nietzsche et la philosophie, 1962)

E1.3.C.2_REF2.2_PH THE STRUGGLE OF THE INNUMERABLE IS PURE JUSTICE! AND BESIDES, THE ONE IS THE MULTIPLE. "It follows that existence is not responsible or even blameworthy. Heraclitus went as far as proclaiming "the struggle of the many is pure justice itself! In fact the one is the many!". The correlation of many and one, of becoming and being forms a game. Affirming becoming and affirming the being of becoming are the two moments of a game which are compounded with a third term, the player, the artist or the child. The player-artist-child, Zeus-child: Dionysus, who the myth presents to us surrounded by bis divine toys. The player temporarily abandons himself to life and temporarily fixes his gaze upon it; the artist places himself provisionally in his work and provisionally above it; the child plays, withdraws from the game and returns to it. In this game of becoming, the being of becoming also plays the game with itself; the aeon (time), says Heraclitus, is a child who plays, plays at draughts. The being of becoming, the eternal return, is the second moment of the game, but also the third term, identical to the two moments and valid for the whole. For the eternal return is the distinct return of the outward movement, the distinct contemplation of the action, but also the return of the outward movement itself and the return of the action; at once moment and cycle of time. We must understand the secret of Heraclitus interpretation; he opposes the instinct of the game to hubris; "It is not guilty pride but the ceaselessly reawoken instinct of the game which calls forth new worlds." Not a theodicy but a cosmodicy, not a sum of injustices to be expiated but justice as the law of this world; not hubris but play, innocence. "That dangerous word hubris is indeed the touchstone for every eraclitean. Here he must show whether he has understood or failed to recognise his master""
(Gilles Deleuze, Nietzsche et la philosophie, 1962)

E1.3.C.2_REF3_PH THE JOYFUL AFFIRMATION OF EXISTENCE RECONNECTS WITH THE TIME "BEFORE THE SEPARATION OF NATURE AND EXISTENCE" - WITH JOY ON TOP. "Where are the good guys in the Iliad? Where are the bad guys? We see only men in pain - warriors in battle, triumphing or succumbing. The demands of justice make only a whisper of tears and complaints at the marble knee of necessity. The passion of justice expresses itself only in this mourning of justice, which demands silence. To condemn or absolve violence would be to condemn or absolve life itself. And life in the Iliad (as in the Bible and in War and Peace) is essentially that which does not allow itself to be assessed, measured, condemned or justified by the living. It judges itself only in its awareness of its indescribability. This acceptance without internal stiffening, consubstantial with existence, is far removed from the Stoic parades.
The daughter of bitterness, the philosophy of the Iliad banishes resentment. It precedes the separation of nature and existence. Here the whole is not a collection of pieces broken apart and put back together by reason, but the active principle of the mutual penetration of all its constituent parts. The unfolding of the inevitable takes place simultaneously in the human heart and in the cosmos. The eternal blindness of history is countered by the creative lucidity of the poet, who names for future generations heroes more divine than gods, more human than humans."
(Rachel Bespaloff, On the Iliad, 1943)

E1.3.C.2_REF3.1_CUR MEDITATE AND REFLECT. "This sculpture is part of a corpus of works created following the trauma of the tsunami that struck Japan in 2011. If the Monumental form evokes a waterspout, or a wave similar to the one that swept over the country after the earthquake, it is also reminiscent of a kind of Buddhist memorial sculpture in front of which one might stand to meditate and reflect. For this work, Murakami collaborated with to Graffiti artists Madsaki and Snipe1."
(Fondation Louis Vuitton, Paris, curator´s review, 2018)

E1.3.C.2_REF4_PH FOR NIETZSCHE, TRAGIC = JOYFUL "To believe in the innocence of the future and the past, to believe in the eternal return. What Nietzsche calls his glad tidings is that existence is no longer treated as blameworthy nor does the will feel guilty for existing. "Will, this is what the liberator and the messenger of joy is called". The glad tidings are tragic thought, for tragedy is not found in the recriminations of ressentiment, the conflicts of bad conscience or the contradictions of a will which feels guilty and responsible. The tragic does not even fight against ressentiment, bad conscience or nihilism. According to Nietzsche it has never been understood that the tragic = the joyful. This is another way of putting the great equation: to will = to create. We have not understood that the tragic is pure and multiple positivity, dynamic gaeity. Affirmation is tragic because it affirms chance and the necessity of chance; because it affirms multiplicity and the unity of multiplicity. The dicethrow is tragic. All the rest is nihilism, Christian and dialectic pathos, caricature of the tragic, comedy of bad conscience."
(Gilles Deleuze, Nietzsche and Philosophie, 1962)

E1.3.C.2_REF4.1_PH REPLACE ALL VALUE JUDGMENTS BY A SINGLE YES "From the moment one admits that the world pursues no end, Nietzsche proposes to welcome its innocence, to affirm that it escapes all judgment since it cannot be judged to possess any intention, and to replace all value judgments by a single yes, a full and exalted adhesion to this world. Thus, from absolute despair will spring infinite joy, from blind servitude, merciless freedom. To be free is precisely to abolish the ends. As soon as one consents to it, the innocence of becoming represents the maximum of freedom. The free spirit loves what is necessary. Nietzsche's deepest thought is that the necessity of phenomena, since it is absolute, without cracks, does not imply any kind of constraint. Total adherence to absolute necessity is his paradoxical definition of freedom. The question "free from what?" is then replaced by "free for what?". Freedom coincides with heroism. It is the asceticism of the Great Man, "the most taut bow that exists."
(Albert Camus, The Rebel)

E1.3.C.2_REF4.2_PH "AMONG ALL IMAGES, SUNRISE GIVES AN INSTANTANEOUS LESSON. IT DEFINES A LYRICISM OF IMMEDIATENESS. To Nietzsche, it does not suggest a panorama, but an action. Foe him, it is not of the order of contemplation, it is of the order of decision. The Nietzschean sunrise is the act of an irrevocable decision. It is nothing other than the eternal return of strength, it is the myth of the eternal return, translated from the passive to the active. And the doctrine of the eternal return will be better understood if it is included in the account of the awakenings of the will to power. Who knows how to rise like a sun, in a single shoot, knows how to launch his being in a destiny each day reassumed, each day reconquered by a renewed amor fati. In his agreement with the cosmic forces of return, it seems that the Nietzschean dreamer can say to the night: "I will make the sun rise. I am the night watchman who will proclaim the hour of awakening, the night is but a long wait for awakening." From then on, the consciousness of the eternal return is a consciousness of the projected will. It is our being which finds itself, which returns to the same consciousness, to the same certainty of being a will, it is our being which projects the world again. One understands the Nietzschean universe poorly if one does not put in the first place dynamic imagination, if one conceives the universe as an immense mill which turns endlessly by crushing the same wheat. Such a universe is dead, it is annihilated by fate. A Nietzschean cosmos lives in moments nurtured by an impulse that areis always young. It is a story of rising suns."
(Gaston Bachelard, Air and Dreams - Essay on the Imagination of Movement, 1943)

E1.3.C.2_REF4.3_CUR TRAGIC ILLUSION
In this installation by Robert Barry, seemingly unrelated words are formed into large letters on the floor. Although there is no articulated message, viewers are compeled to construct a meaning, if only by associating words that are in close proximity. “TRAGIC” and “ILLUSION” are juxtaposed: isn't the tragic an illusion masking reality? This reality, though tragic, becomes a wellspring of joy. As Deleuze, drawing on Nietzsche, helps us understand: the tragic equals the joyful.

E1.3.C.2 _REF4.4_PH WE REALLY DO BECOME, FOR BRIEF INSTANTS, THE ORIGINAL BEING ITSELF AND WE EXPERIENCE ITS INSATIABLE THIRST FOR EXISTENCE "To counter the despair that has driven science and religion, Nietzsche proposes the tragic—the most sublime expression of music—which partakes in the profound being [...] Tragic philosophy offers an essential consolation: "We really become, for brief moments, the original being itself, and experience its insatiable thirst for existence." Recognizing the countless forms of existence leads us to accept as necessary "the struggle, agony, and destruction of phenomena." We experience not only terror and pity, as interpreters of Aristotelian catharsis have noted, but also, more importantly, the joy of living through participation in the "living unity" (Kroner I, p. 117).".
(Angèle Kremer-Marietti, Introduction to Nietzsche's The Birth of Tragedy, 1994)

E1.3.C.2_REF4.4.a_PH IT IS ONE OF NIETZSCHE'S THESES THAT "MAN WANTS TO KNOW OUT OF FEAR IF HE IS PREY, OR OUT OF WILL TO POWER, IF HE IS AT THE TOP OF THE FOOD CHAIN - EITHER WAY, HE CANNOT STAND DOUBT. In "The Gay Science", Nietzsche speaks of the "dangerous perhaps" which constitute the engine of philosophical research. Precisely because uncertainty or the lack of answers drive the human animal mad, this ignorance must be remedied. What Nietzsche says is that knowledge is a consequence of this mental system of protection: the first motor of knowledge is fear (of not knowing, of the next day ...). The second motor is the will to master reality. To know is to tame reality in such a way that our relationship with the world becomes viable. This viability demands falsifications that make our view of the world overly utilitarian; this also applies to logic and to science. Damien Hirst, seems to echo this idea in the sculpture "Remnants of Apollo". From Apollo (who, for Nietzsche, represents  the one who "disguises" reality so that it is acceptable - unlike Dionysus who embraces it as it is), there is only one of his foot left, on which crawls a disturbing incarnation of science - a wrinkled, albino lab mouse growing a human ear on its back to be transplanted on a human later. There it wanders, squeaking among the ruins of an era (or a civilization?).

E1.3.C.2_REF4.4.b_SCI A 3D EAR GRAFTED ON A MOUSE

“In a stunning example of reality surpassing science fiction, American researchers have achieved a major breakthrough in regenerative medicine. By successfully implanting a 3D-printed ear on a mouse, they've taken a significant step towards developing laboratory-grown organs for transplantation. This groundbreaking experiment not only demonstrated the feasibility of implanting 3D-printed organs but also proved their viability. Remarkably, after just two months, the plastic component of the ear had completely disappeared, replaced by living cartilage.

The artificial ear was created using a special ink with two key ingredients: a biodegradable, body-safe plastic that provided the desired shape, and a gel containing living cells. This innovative approach resulted in an ear that closely resembled a human one in structure while being composed of living cells. The combination of these elements is what makes this ear truly unique.

The real test came when the ear was transplanted under a mouse's skin. The results were nothing short of miraculous. Not only did the graft take, but within two months, the plastic scaffold had completely disappeared, replaced by living cartilage. The 3D-printed ear essentially became a real, functioning organ, complete with blood vessels nourishing it. This remarkable achievement extends beyond ears—the researchers also successfully replicated this experiment with 3D-printed bones and muscles, marking a significant leap forward in regenerative medicine.”
(Mélanie Gomez, Europe 1, 02/18/2016)

E1.3.C.2_REF4.5_PH LIFE IS DEVALUED IN THE NAME OF FICTITIOUS HIGHER VALUES (SUCH AS GOD OR THE GOOD) THAT DENY AND ANNIHILATE LIFE. We then react against these higher values by denying their validity, leading to the devaluation of these values themselves. This leads to a vision of life without values or purpose, where everything is mere appearance and life continues to be devalued.
"NIHILISM
In the word nihilism nihil does not signify non-being but primarily a value of nil. Life takes on a value of nil insofar as it is denied and depreciated. Depreciation always presupposes a fiction: it is by means of fiction that one falsifies and depreciates, it is by means of fiction that something is opposed to life. The whole of life then becomes unreal, it is represented as appearance, it takes on a value of nil in its entirety. The idea of another world, of a supersensible world in all its forms (God, essence, the good, truth), the idea of values superior to life, is not one example among many but the constitutive element of all fiction. Values superior to life are inseparable from their effect: the depreciation of life, the negation of this world. And if they are inseparable from this effect it is because their principle is a will to deny, to depreciate. We must be careful not to think that higher values form a threshold where the will stops, as if, confronted by the divine, we were released from the constraint of willing. It is not the will that denies itself in higher values, it is higher values that are related to a will to deny, to annihilate life. "Nothingness of the will": this Schopenhauerian concept is only a symptom; it means primarily a will to annihilation, a will to nothingness . . . "but it is and remains a will". Nihil in "nihilism" means negation as quality of the will to power. Thus, in its primary and basic sense, nihilism signifies the value of nil taken on by life, the fiction of higher values which give it this value and the will to nothingness which is expressed in these higher values.

Nihilism has a second, more colloquial sense. It no longer signifies a will but rather a reaction. The supersensible world and higher values are reacted against, their existence is denied, they are refused all validity - this is no longer the devaluation of life in the name of higher values but rather the devaluation of higher values themselves. Devaluation no longer signifies life taking on the value of nil, the null value, but the nullity of values, of higher values. The sensational news spreads: there is nothing to be seen behind the curtain, "The characteristics which have been assigned to the 'real being' of things are the characteristics of non-being, of nothingness". Thus the nihilist denies God, the good and even truth — all the forms of the supersensible.  Nothing is true, nothing is good, God is dead. The nothingness of the will is no longer merely the symptom of a will to nothingness, but ultimately a negation of all will, a taedium vitae. There is no longer any human or earthly will. "Here is snow; here life has grown silent; the last crows whose cries are audible here are called 'wherefore?', 'in vain!', nada - here nothing will grow or prosper any longer". This second sense would be familiar but no less incomprehensible if we did not see how it derives from and presupposes the first. Previously life was depreciated from the height of higher values, it was denied in the name of these values. Here, on the contrary, only life remains, but it is still a depreciated life which now continues in a world without values, stripped of meaning and purpose, sliding ever further towards its nothingness. Previously essence was opposed to appearance, life was turned into an appearance. Now essence is denied but appearance is retained: everything is merely appearance, life which is left to us remains for itself an appearance. The first sense of nihilism found its principle in the will to deny as will to power. The second sense, "the pessimism of weakness", finds its principle in the reactive life completely solitary and naked, in reactive forces reduced to themselves. The first sense is a negative nihilism; the second sense a reactive nihilism."
(Gilles Deleuze, Nietzsche and Philosophy, 1962)

E1.3.C.2_REF4.5.a_PH PITY IS A PRACTICE OF NIHILISM IN THAT IT IS ASSOCIATED WITH REACTIVE FORCES THAT DEVALUE LIFE, CULMINATING IN A STATE WHERE LIFE BECOMES SELF-SUFFICIENT BUT DEVOID OF HIGHER ASPIRATIONS OR VALUES.
"ANALYSIS OF PITY

— What is pity? It is this tolerance for states of life close to zero. Pity is the love of life, but of the weak, sick, reactive life. It is militant and announces the final victory of the poor, the suffering, the powerless and the small. It is divine and gives them this victory. Who feels pity? Precisely those who can only tolerate life when it is reactive, those who need this life and this triumph, those who build their temples on the marshy ground of such a life. Those who hate everything which is active in life, those who use life to deny and depreciate life, to oppose it to itself. Pity, in Nietzsche's symbolism, always designates this complex of will to nothingness and reactive forces, this affinity or tolerance of one for the other. "Pity is practical nihilism. . . pity persuades to nothingnessl . . .One does not say 'nothingness": one says 'the Beyond'; or 'God'; or 'true life'; or Nirvana, redemption, blessedness . . . This innocent rhetoric from the domain of religio-moral idiosyncracy at once appears much less innocent when one grasps which tendency is here draping the mantle of sublime words about itself: the tendency hostile to life". Pity for the reactive life in the name of higher values, God's pity for the reactive man: we can guess what kind of will is hidden in this way of loving life, in this God of mercy, in these higher values.

God suffocates from pity: it is as if the reactive life had blocked up his throat. The reactive man puts God to death because he can no longer bear there being a witness, he wants to be alone with his triumph and his strength. He puts himself in God's place: he no longer knows any values which are superior to life, but only a reactive life that is satisfied with itself and claims to secrete its own values. The weapons which God gave him, ressentiment, even bad conscience – all the forms of his triumph - are turned against and opposed to God. Ressentiment becomes atheistic, but this atheism is still ressentiment, always ressentiment, always bad conscience. God's murderer is the reactive man, "the ugliest man", "rumbling with bile and full of secret shame". He reacts against God's pity, "There is also good taste in pity: that said at last: Away with such a god. Better no god, better to produce destiny on one's own account, better to be a fool, better to be God oneself!". - How far will he go along this road? As far as the great disgust. It is better to have no values at all than higher values, it is better to have no will at all, better to have a nothingness of will than a will to nothingness. It is better to fade away passively. It is the prophet, "prophet of great weariness", who announces the consequences of the death of God: the reactive life left alone with itself, no longer even having the will to disappear, dreaming of a passive extinction. "Everything is empty, everything is past! . . . All our wells have dried up, even the sea has receded. The earth wants to break open, but the depths will not devour us! Alas, where is there still a sea in which one could drown . . . Truly we have grown too weary even to die." The last man is the descendant of God's murderer: it is better to have no will at all, better to have a single herd. "Nobody grows rich or poor any more: both are too much of a burden. Who still wants to rule? Who still wants to obey? Both are too much of a burden. No herdsman and one herd. Everyone wants the same thing, everyone is the same . . ."

Told in this way the story still leads to the same conclusion: negative nihilism is replaced by reactive nihilism, reactive nihilism ends in passive nihilism. From God to God's murderer, from God's murderer to the last man. But this outcome is known to the prophet. There are many avatars, many variations on the nihilist theme, before we reach this point. The reactive life strives for a long time to secrete its own values, the reactive man takes the place of God: adaptation, evolution, progress, happiness for all and the good of the community; the God-man, the moral man, the truthful man and the social man. These are the new values that are recommended in place of higher values, these are the new characters proposed in place of God. The last men
still say: "We have invented happiness" […]

Values can change, be renewed or even disappear. What does not change and does not disappear is the nihilistic perspective which governs this history from beginning to end and from which all these values (as well as their absence) arise. This is why Nietzsche can think that nihilism is not an event in history but the motor of the history of man as universal history. Negative, reactive and passive nihilism: for Nietzsche one and the same history is marked out by Judaism, Christianity, the reformation, free thought, democratic and socialist ideology etc. Up until the last man."
(Gilles Deleuze, Nietzsche and Philosophy, 1962)

E1.5.B.5.5.d.wE1.3.C.2_REF4.5_PH

E1.3.C.2_REF4.5.b_PH LAUGHTER, PLAY, AND DANCE ARE FORMS OF AFFIRMATION, TRANSFORMING PAIN INTO JOY AND NEGATION INTO AFFIRMATION. This transformation leads to a change in the quality of values, promoting active forces over reactive ones.
"IS MAN ESSENTIALLY "REACTIVE"? […]
The higher man remains within the abstract element of activity, he never raises himself, even in thought, to the element of affirmation. The higher man claims to reverse values, to convert reaction into action. Zarathustra speaks of something else: transmuting values, converting negation into affirmation. But reaction will never become action without this deeper conversion: negation must first become a power of affirming. Separated from the conditions which would make it viable, the enterprise of the higher man is abortive, not accidentally but in principle and essence. Instead of forming a becoming-active it nourishes the opposite becoming, becoming-reactive. Instead of reversing values, values are changed, made to exchange places while retaining the nihilistic perspective from which they derive. Instead of training forces and making them active they organize associations of reactive forces. Conversely the conditions which would make the enterprise of higher man viable are conditions which would change its nature: Dionysian affirmation rather than man's species activity. The element of affirmation is the superhuman element. The element of affirmation is what man lacks - even and above all the higher man. Nietzsche expresses this lack symbolically as the deficiency at the heart of man in four ways: 1) There are things that the higher man does not know how to do: to laugh, to play and to dance. To laugh is to affirm life, even the suffering in life. To play is to affirm chance and the necessity of chance. To dance is to affirm becoming and the being of becoming. [...]

10) AFFIRMATION AND NEGATION
"Transmutation or transvaluation means: 
1) Change of quality in the will to power. Values and their value no longer derive from the negative, but from affirmation as such. In place of a depreciated life we have life which is affirmed - and the expression "in place of' is still incorrect. It is the place itself which changes, there is no longer any place for another world. The element of values changes place and nature, the value of values changes its principle and the whole of evaluation changes character. […]

4) Reign of affirmation in the will to power. Only affirmation subsists as an independent power; the negative shoots out from it like lightning, but also becomes absorbed into it, disappearing into it like a soluble fire. In the man who wants to perish the negative announces the superhuman, but only affirmation produces what the negative announces. There is no other power but affirmation, no other quality, no other element: the whole of negation is converted in its substance, transmuted in its quality, nothing remains of its own power or autonomy. This is the conversion of heavy into light, of low into high, of pain into joy. This trinity of dance, play and laughter creates the transubstantiation of nothingness, the transmutation of the negative and the transvaluation or change of power of negation. What Zarathustra calls "the Communion".
5) Critique of known values. The values known up to the present lose all their value. Negation reappears here but always in the form of a power of affirming, as the inseparable consequence of affirmation and transmutation. Sovereign affirmation is inseparable from the destruction of all known values, it turns this destruction into a total destruction.
6) Reversal of the relation of forces. Affirmation constitutes becoming active as the universal becoming of forces. Reactive forces are denied, all forces become active. The reversal of values and the establishment of active values are all operations which presuppose the transmutation of values, the conversion of the negative into affirmation.

We are now perhaps in a position to understand Nietzsche's texts concerning affirmation, negation and their relations. In the first place, negation and affirmation are opposed as two qualities of the will to power, two ratios of the will to power. They are both opposites, but also wholes which exclude their opposite. We can say that negation has dominated our thought, our ways of feeling and evaluating, up to the present day. In truth, it's part of being human. And with man, it's the whole world that sinks and becomes sick, it's the whole of life that is devalued, the whole of the known that slides into its own nothingness. Conversely, affirmation manifests itself only above humanity, outside humanity, in the superhuman that it produces, in the unknown that it brings with it. But the superhuman, the unknown, is also the whole that drives out the negative. The superhuman as a species is also "the superior species of all that is." Zarathustra says yes and amen "in an enormous and unlimited way"; he himself is "the eternal affirmation of all things. "I bless and affirm always, provided you are around me, clear sky, abyss of light! I carry my blessing affirmation into all abysses". As long as the negative reigns, we would look in vain for the grain of affirmation here below and in the other world: what we call affirmation is grotesque, a sad ghost stirring the chains of the negative. But when transmutation occurs, it is the negation that dissolves, nothing remains of it as an independent force, in quality or in reason: "Supreme constellation of Being, which no desire reaches, which no negation tarnishes, eternal affirmation of Being, eternally I am your affirmation." [...]"
(Gilles Deleuze, Nietzsche et la philosophie, 1962)

E1.3.C.2_REF4.5.c_PH A « LARK MIRROR » IS A SMALL MIRROR USED TO ATTRACT AND TRAP SMALL BIRDS ATTRACTED TO SHINY THINGS. The expression « lark mirror » is used to refer metaphorically to an apparently attractive offer that is really just trying to attract gullible people.
"The question is: how do reactive forces triumph? That is, when do they prevail over active forces, do the reactive forces become dominant in their turn, aggressive and subjugating, do they all together form a greater force which would be active in its turn? Nietzsche answers: reactive forces, even when they unite, do not form a greater force that would be active. They proceed quite differently: they decompose; they separate the active force from what it can; they subtract from the active force a part or almost all of its power; and by this they do not become active, but on the contrary make the active force join them, become itself reactive in a new sense. [...]
This separation is based on a fiction, on a mystification or falsification. It is the will of nothingness that develops the negative and reversed image, that  effectuates the subtraction. [...]
The active force is separated from what it can by a fiction, but it does not become any less reactive all the same, it is even by this means that it becomes really reactive. Hence Nietzsche's use of the words « vile », « corrupt », « slave »: these words designate the state of reactive forces that put themselves on top, that draw the active force into a trap, replacing the masters by slaves who do not cease to be slaves."
(Gilles Deleuze, Nietzsche and Philosophy, 1962)

E1.3.C.2_REF4.5.d_PH "WHEN REACTIVE FORCES ARE NO LONGER ACTED UPON, THEY PROJECT AN INVERTED IMAGE — what Nietzsche calls a fiction: the fiction of a supra-sensible world opposed to this one, the fiction of a God contradicting life. Nietzsche distinguishes this fiction from the active power of dreams and from the positive image of gods that affirm and glorify life: « While the world of dreams reflects reality, the world of fiction only distorts, depreciates, and denies it. » This fiction governs the entire evolution of resentment, operating by separating the active force from its potential (falsification), accusing and treating it as guilty (depreciation), and overturning the corresponding values (negation). Through this fiction, reactive forces portray themselves as superior. « To be able to say no to everything that represents life's upward movement—to everything noble, powerful, beautiful, self-affirming on earth—the instinct of resentment, having become genius, had to invent another world, from which this affirmation of life appeared as evil, a reprehensible thing in itself. »"
(Gilles Deleuze, Nietzsche and philosophy, 1962)

E1.3.C.2_REF4.5.e_PH "WE DO NOT REALLY KNOW WHAT A MAN DENUDED OF RESSENTIMENT WOULD BE LIKE. A man who would not accuse or depreciate existence - would he still be a man, would he think like a man? Would he not already be something other than a man, almost the Overman? To have ressentiment or not to have ressentiment - there is no greater difference, beyond psychology, beyond history, beyond metaphysics. It is the true difference or transcendental typology - the genealogical and hierarchical difference.
Nietzsche presents the aim of his philosophy as the freeing of thought from nihilism and its various forms. Now, this implies a new way of thinking, an overthrow of the principle on which thought depends, a straightening out of the genealogical principle itself, a "transmutation".
 "
(Gilles Deleuze, Nietzsche et la philosophie, 1962)

E1.3.C.2_REF4.5.f_PH “UNSPOKEN EXPECTATIONS ARE PREMEDITATED RESENTMENTS.”
(Neil Strauss)

E1.3.C.2_REF5_PH BEWARE: TO SAY YES TO NIHLILISM IS A CARICURE OF AFFIRMATION
"Nietzsche's thesis can be summarized as follows: the yes that does not know how to say no (the donkey's yes) is a caricature of affirmation. Precisely because it says yes to everything that is no - because it supports nihilism - it remains at the service of the power to deny and to the demon whose burdens it carries. The Dionysian yes, on the contrary, is the one who knows how to say no: it is pure affirmation, it has defeated nihilism and deprived negation of any autonomous power, this because it has put the negative at the service of the powers of affirmation. To affirm is to create, not to bear, to draw on, to assume. The donkey entertains a ridiculous thought: « To think and to take a thing seriously, to assume its weight, it is all the same thing for them, they have no other experience. » [...]
To affirm is not to take on a burden, to bear what is, but to free, to liberate what lives. To affirm is to lighten: not to burden life with the weight of superior values, but to create new values that are those of life, that make life light and active. Strictly speaking, there is creation only to the extent that, far from separating life from what it can, we use the surplus to invent new forms of life. « And what you have called the world, you must begin by creating it: your reason, your imagination, your will, your love must become this world. »"
(Gilles Deleuze, Nietzsche and Philosophy, 1962)

E1.3.C.2_REF5.1_PH TRUE AFFIRMATION, IN NIETZSCHE'S PHILOSOPHY, ISN'T ABOUT MERELY ACCEPTING OR ENDURING WHAT EXISTS. RATHER, IT'S ABOUT CREATING AND LIBERATING LIFE. THIS CONCEPT FOCUSES ON FORGING NEW VALUES THAT ENRICH AND UPLIFT LIFE INSTEAD OF WEIGHING IT DOWN.
"THE SENSE OF AFFIRMATION  [...]

But the real and its acceptance remain what they are, false positivity and false affirmation. Faced with "the men of the present" Zarathustra says: "the unfamiliar things of the future and whatever frightened stray birds, are truly more familiar and more genial than your 'reality'. For thus you speak: 'We are complete realists and without belief or superstition': thus you thump your chests - alas, even without having chests! But how should you be able to believe, you motley-spotted men! - you who are paintings of all that has ever been believed! . . . Unworthy of belief: that is what / call you, you realists! . . You are unfruitful . . . You are half-open doors at which grave-diggers wait. [...]

Nietzsche wants to say three things:
1) Being, the true and the real are the avatars of nihilism. Ways of mutilating life, of denying it, of making it reactive by submitting it to the labour of the negative, by loading it with the heaviest burdens. Nietzsche has no more belief in the self-sufficiency of the real than he has in that of the true: he thinks of them as the manifestations of a will, a will to depreciate life, to oppose life to life.
2) Affirmation conceived of as acceptance, as affirmation of that which is, as truthfulness of the true or positivity of the real, is a false affirmation. It is the yes of the ass. The ass does not know how to say no because he says yes to everything which is no. The ass or the camel is the opposite of the lion; in the lion negation becomes a power of affirming, but in them affirmation remains at the service of the negative, a simple power of denying.
3) This false conception of affirmation is still a way of preserving man. As long as being is a burden the reactive man is there to carry it. Where could being be better affirmed than in the desert? And where could man be better preserved. "The last man lives the longest." Beneath the sun of being he loses even the taste for dying, disappearing into the desert to dream at length of a passive extinction. - Nietzsche's whole philosophy is opposed to the postulates of being, of man and of acceptance. "Being: we have no other representation of it than the fact of living. How could that which is dead have being?". The world is neither true nor real but living. And the living world is will to power, will to falsehood, which is actualised in many different powers. To actualise the will to falsehood under any power whatever, to actualise the will to power under any quality whatever, is always to evaluate. To live is to evaluate. There is no truth of the world as it is thought, no reality of the sensible world, all is evaluation, even and above all the sensible and the real. "The will to appearance, to illusion, to deception, to becoming and change (to objectified deception) here counts as more profound, primeval, 'metaphysical', than
the will to truth, to reality, to mere appearance: - the last is itself merely a form of the will to illusion". Being, truth and reality are themselves only valid as evaluations, that is to say as lies. But, in this capacity, as means of actualising the will through one of its powers, they have, up to now served the power or quality of the negative. Being, truth and reality itself are like the divine in which life is opposed to life. The ruler is then negation as quality of the will to power which, opposing life to life, denies the whole of it and makes it triumph as reactive in particular. By contrast, the other quality of the will to power is a power through which willing is adequate to the whole of life, a higher power of the false, a quality through which the whole of life and its particularity is affirmed and has become active. To affirm is still to evaluate, but to evaluate from the perspective of a will which enjoys its own difference in life instead of suffering the pains of the opposition to
this life that it has itself inspired. To affirm is not to take responsibility for, to take on the burden of what is, but to release, to set free what lives. To affirm is to unburden: not to load life with the weight of higher values, but to create new values which are those of life, which make life light and active. There is creation, properly speaking, only insofar as we make use of excess in order to invent new forms of life rather than separating life from what it can do. "And you yourselves should create what you have hitherto called the World: the World should be formed in your image by your reason, your will and your love!". But this task is not completed in man. Going as far as he can man raises negation to a power of affirming. But
affirming in its full power, affirming affirmation itself — this is beyond man's strength. "To create new values - even the lion is incapable of that: but to create itself freedom for new creation - that the lion can do". The sense of affirmation
can only emerge if these three fundamental points in Nietzsche's philosophy are borne in mind: not the true nor the real but evaluation; not affirmation as acceptance but as creation; not man but the Overman as a new form of life. Nietzsche attaches so much importance to art because art realises the whole of this programme: the highest power of the false, Dionysian affirmation or the genius of the superhuman.

Nietzsche's argument can be summarised as follows: the yes which does not know how to say no (the yes of the ass) is a caricature of affirmation. This is precisely because it says yes to everything which is no, because it puts up with nihilism it continues to serve the power of denying - which is like a demon whose every burden it carries. The Dionysian yes, on the contrary, knows how to say no: it is pure affirmation, it has conquered nihilism and divested negation of all autonomous power. But it has done this because it has placed the negative at the service of the powers of affirming. To affirm is to create, not to bear, put up with or accept. A ridiculous image of thought is formed in the head of the ass. " 'Thinking' and 'taking
something seriously', giving it 'weighty consideration' - to them these things go together: that is the only way they have 'experienced' it"
(Gilles Deleuze, Nietzsche and Philosophy, 1962)

E1.3.D. ARE WE HUMANLY CAPABLE OF THIS?
Is it possible to fully embrace reality without yearning for something more? Are we truly capable of such acceptance?

E1.3.D.1. IS EXTRA-LUCIDITY COMPATIBLE WITH HAPPINESS? DOESN'T REALITY SYSTEMATICALLY CRUSH US?

E1.3.D.1.1. "IS THERE A POSSIBLE ALLIANCE BETWEEN LUCIDITY AND JOY?" It's certain that such an alliance exists, as evidenced by the joy that combines unshakeable cheerfulness with an ever-present sense of life's utter futility. Examples abound: Nietzsche's "gai savoir," Spanish humor, and Mozartian gladness, to name a few. Yet paradoxically, this union of joy and lucidity defies reason, rendering it theoretically impossible. As Cioran aptly notes, "to exist is equivalent to a protest against the truth." This verdict is sound—existence is, indeed, indefensible. Consequently, any sensible argument in its favor inevitably devolves into nonsense, as illustrated by this medieval adage from Martinus von Biberach:

"I come from I know not where,
I am I know not who,
I die I know not when,
I go I know not where,
I'm surprised to be so cheerful."
(I marvel at being so joyful.)

(Clément Rosset, The Major Force, 1983)

E1.3.D.1.2. THE WAKE OF JOY, EVEN IF IT IS NOT JOY, REMAINS A SURE BULWARK AGAINST RESENTMENT AND LOW SELF-ESTEEM.
"The brave person knows the value and despair of transience. Yet, they refuse to build resentment upon it. Instead, they are determined to avoid resentment. This is indeed a struggle, as resentment seems the most rational response to life's shortcomings in relation to reason. It would be logical for reason to foster bitterness from life's disappointments and inadequacies. However, the courageous individual—who in this sense has access to the unspeakable—maintains an inner sense and will for joy. No one knows if they'll truly experience it; claiming so would be utopian and fanciful. But at least they will have stood in its wake. And even if the wake of joy is not joy itself, it remains a sure bulwark against resentment and low self-esteem.
The brave thus align with Bergsonian optimism. This optimism might appear provocative or intellectually lacking. Yet, it's rather a sign of determination to act. While joy isn't certain, the resolve for joy is within reach of courageous people. This resolve isn't futile—it prevents barbarism. There's no ethics of despair, and Bergson recognizes the importance of belonging to 'those whose desires are on earth.' Rootedness in personal existence is the surest path to eternity and the feeling that finitude won't have the last word, at least symbolically."
(Cynthia Fleury, The End of Courage, 2010)

E1.3.D.1.2_REF1_PH JOY IS NOT IDENTIFIED WITH GRACE, BUT WITH THE STATE OF GRACE, THAT VOLUNTARY STATE WHICH TESTIFIES TO THE WILLINGNESS OF THE BRAVE TO REJOICE AND TO THEIR FIERCE ACTIVITY. "The man of duration [...] finds his joy on the spot, in the very immanence and exciting present of his historical here and now. Bergson "recovers all lost time. And so does the courageous. "To become is not to die slowly or to languish [...] but to realize oneself infinitely". Bergson's optimism may seem blissful and ineffectual. 
But [those who have the courage to say everything] are not naive. On the contrary, they are the most clear-sighted. To bet on optimism is, above all, to refuse to bet on fantasy. Refusing to bet on the escape from reality as the source of happiness. To bet on optimism is to take responsibility for our destiny. The moral maxim is therefore simple: "There is no need to be tragic, just be serious. The impact of melancholy, its grandiosity, its undeniable accuracy and intelligence do not protect it from its ineffectiveness. What's the point of so much panache if it fails? Melancholy is a demonic adversary that knows how to bring you down by making you its servant. Who hasn't thought that being melancholy makes you smarter than everyone else? Who hasn't thought that this is the very side of intelligence, the price to pay? Courage maintains that there are other epistemologies. "There is therefore no place in Bergsonism for complexes of failure, impasse, or disappointment, nor for the convicts of Schopenhauerian nonsense [...]. The knights of the absurd will find no ally in Bergson. The symptom of these triumphs is joy. In this sense, Bergson's ethics is a philosophy of joy, insofar as joy is identified not with grace, but with the state of grace, that voluntary state that testifies to the willingness of the brave to rejoice and to their fierce activity. The epistemology of courage is not a pathos of abandonment, but an open metaphysics that defines in the courageous moment an access to eternity and meaning. If the Bergsonian ethic of joy is an epistemology of courage, it's because it knows that "man is in forced residence in becoming," that he is "the forced laborer of temporality."
(Cynthia Fleury, The End of Courage, 2010)

E1.3.D.1.2_REF2_CUR “URS FISCHER'S WORK OFTEN EXPLORES THE THEME OF DECOMPOSITION, highlighting the fragility of existence. In "With You" (2007), Fischer takes this concept to its extreme by destroying the floor of Gavin Brown's Enterprise gallery. He excavates a pit nearly 2.5 meters deep, creating a monumental void. This isn't a homage to artists like Gordon Matta-Clark, Michael Heizer, or Walter De Maria. Instead, it's a continuation of Fischer's artistic vision—a cathartic sculpture that's both exuberant and somber. The work embraces absurdity as an unavoidable aspect of reality, finding delight in this acceptance.”
(Caroline Cros, What is sculture today, 2008)

E1.3.D.1.2_REF3_CUR "THE VIRGIN MARY is often depicted as melancholic when she holds Jesus in her arms. Yet there are portraits of the Virgin and Child in which she is cheerful: a sign of the painter's wisdom, whose love of reality includes an awareness of death - or a sign of the artist's atheism? We prefer the former."
(Pierre Schindler)

E1.3.D.1.2_REF4_PO “I SAY YES

I could say
that life is beautiful, and very much so,
and that the revolution walks with flowering feet
in the fields of my country,
with rubber feet
in the great Brazilian cities
and that my heart
is a sun of hope between lungs
and clouds

I could say that my people
is a party just hear the voice
of Clara Nunes
in the spinning
of the mix of races during carnival
on the Avenue.
But no. The poet is lying.

We grind life into blood
and samba
while the whole night rolls
over the unequal homeland. Life
we make it ours
happy and sad, singing
in the midst of hunger
and saying yes
in the midst of violence and loneliness saying
yes -
for the wonder of beauty
for Tereza's flame
for my lost son
on this vast continent
for the wounded Vianinha
for our fallen brother
for love and what it denies
for what it gives and what it blinds
for what will finally come,
I'm not saying that life is beautiful
nor do I deny it:
- I say yes”
(Ferreira Gullar, In the Vertigo of Daylight, 1975-1980 - translated by Pierre Schindler)

E1.3.D.1.2_REF5_PO “BEES SWARM ALONG THE
FRAGILE EDGE OF DARKNESS
Bees swarm along the fragile edge of darkness.
Open wounds attract blood-hungry flies.
Near my heart lie savage little souls
Neatly arrayed to feast upon my life.
In eight days God will be through with miracles.
Even so, life is a gift of love.

So how does one enjoy this gift of love,
Even as one moves from light to darkness?
There is no moment free of miracles,
However swift and deep one's passion flies.

Glory is the dancing quark of life,
Alight with love and lust in all our souls.
Born of the cataclysm, our burgeoning souls
Race towards infinity, love
Infinite, lust infinite, life
Eternal as light billowing into darkness.
Little do we see how far it flies
As we spin through Earth-bound miracles.
Nor can we comprehend these miracles.
Darkness is the center of our souls,
Like still black water in the moonlight. Love
Is of this emptiness; unburdened, it flies
Swiftly in widening circles, skimming the darkness,
A motion outward at the heart of life.

More cry than ocean, more wish than star, life
Is the lyric of an explosion of miracles.
Dream and dung, it is the gospel of darkness;
In a petrie dish, a canticle of souls.
Earth is the stage for a concert of passionate love:
Lettuces and roses, gulls and flies.

Let flies and fish and redwoods sing of life
Equally, of love and miracles;
Nor shall our souls deny their birth in darkness.”
(Nicholas Gordon, Philosophical poems, 2002)

E1.3.D.1.3. THE AFFIRMATION OF REALITY COMES—PERHAPS PRIMARILY—THROUGH FULLY ACCEPTING OUR OWN LIMITATIONS

E1.3.D.1.3_REF1_PH WHEN JACQUES THE FATALIST BLAMES HIMSELF FOR ALWAYS WANTING SOMETHING OTHER THAN HIS LOT AND FINALLY TAKES THE PARTY OF BEING AS HE IS. "Jacques. - My master, one does not know what to rejoice about or what to grieve about in life. Good brings evil, evil brings good. We walk in the night under what is written above, equally foolish in our desires, our joys, and our sorrows. When I cry, I often find that I'm a fool. 
The Master. - And when you laugh? 
James. - I still think I'm a fool, but I can't stop crying or laughing, and that makes me so angry. I've tried a hundred times. I haven't slept a wink all night... 
The Master. - No, no, tell me what you tried. 
Jacques. - To make fun of everything. Ah, if only I had succeeded. 
The Master. - What good would it have done you? 
James. - To be free of worries, to need nothing more, to be in perfect control of myself, to find myself with my head against a street-corner bollard as well as on a good pillow. That's how I am sometimes, but the devil is that it doesn't last, and as hard and firm as a rock on the big occasions, it often happens that a small contradiction, a trifle unlocks me, it's to give oneself bellows. I've given up, I've decided to be as I am, and I've seen, on reflection, that it's almost the same thing, adding that it doesn't matter how you are. It's a different, easier and more convenient resignation.
(Denis Diderot, James the Fatalist, 1778-1780)

E1.3.D.1.3_REF2_PH FOR GEORGE ORWELL, "TO BE HUMAN IS ESSENTIALLY NOT TO SEEK PERFECTION."
George Orwell is asking us not only to recognize that perfection is not of this world, but to understand that to seek it is to turn our backs on what it means to be human. It's a disturbing statement: for centuries, a whole current of thought has invited us to strive for a state of perfection identified with a kind of detachment or domination of reason over our entire being. And so we all regret not being a little more perfect, believing that if we were, we'd be happier - but we're wrong.
You regret a judgment you unjustly hurled at another person? great! You're getting in touch with the depth of your existence. Instead of beating yourself up over and over again for not living up to your idea of perfection, of always being right, always being precise, you're honestly facing the abyss of your own heart, and that allows you to develop a real tenderness for yourself. Yes, sometimes you're clumsy, sometimes you're inadequate, sometimes you're a bit of an idiot! Do you know what makes a great writer? His ability to show us what is petty and mediocre in all of us. That's the genius of Dostoyevsky or Proust: they reveal all the twists and turns of our lies, our cowardice, our jealousy. They do this not out of cruelty or despair, but in order to grasp the profound reality of our existence, and it's a relief to finally be able to enter into the complexity, but also the beauty, of our lives. Bad writers stay on the surface of feelings, sometimes even suffocating us with their false promises of happiness. Contrary to popular belief, the former are driven by an infinite tenderness that allows them to see the human being in all its dimensions, while the latter simply don't have access to it, or are afraid to. They're afraid of real life, they're afraid of their hearts. But how do we make peace with our imperfections? 

Think of someone you love: think of their flaws, their wounds, and see them not as what hinders their existence, but as what makes them beautiful. Knowing that we're imperfect makes us more tolerant and caring for others.
(Fabrice Midal, 3 minutes of philosophy to become human again)

Listen to the podcast: https://www.franceculture.fr/emissions/3-minutes-de-philosophie-pour-redevenir-humain/accepter-ses-imperfections-pour-sublimer-son-humanite

E1.3.D.1.4. LACAN OBSERVED THE FADING OF THE SUBJECT WHEN CONFRONTED WITH REALITY

E1.3.D.1.4_REF1_PH TAKING A LOOK AT THE SECRET OF THINGS WHEN NO ONE IS THERE "In the art of still life, as it triumphed in the 17th century [...] in Dutch painting with [Heda and Kalf], those sumptuous meals that magically glow in the darkness [...] aren't they not superlatively present, real in their very unreality, through their hallucinatory presence, as if the painter was showing us the triumph of inviolated things ? [They] show themselves on the stage of the painting with all the more magnificence that there is no longer a spectator in the room to gaze at them, as if the painter was attempting the imaginary operation which consists of taking a look at the secret of things when no one is there to see them any longer. This eclipse of the subject is all the more evident as the painter explicitly suggests it in the painting : the crumpled tablecloth, the fallen and often broken glass, the shiny blade of a knife balanced on the edge of the table, the blackish material crumbling from the disemboweled cake, the murky glow of white wine in the Rhine glass, all these clues suggest that the guest fell to the ground, struck down by the poison of this fantastic meal. [...]
We understand the affinity sometimes underlined by Lacan between the principle of reality, the death drive and the violence of desire : the presence of things is all the more irrefutable that we feel more annihilated by their revelation, a trial that doesn't dismiss desire, if desire is the temptation of transgression, the crossing of a limit, in theses painting even of the absolute limit, that which separates the conscious from the unconscious and being from nothingness."
(Jacques Darriulat, Lacan, Listening to madness, III-Reality, 2019)

Read the essay in its integrality: http://www.jdarriulat.net/Auteurs/Lacan/Ecouterlafolie/Ecouterlafolie3.html

E1.3.D.1.5. "AT TIMES, IN THAT CHAOS, THAT PILING UP OF DAYS,
the sound of a single word rings in the ear,
some brief, syllabic cry,
like ‘love,’ perhaps, or possibly merely ‘hi!’
But before I can make it out, static or haze
trouble the scanning lines that undulate
and wave like the loosened ripples of your hair [...]
I notice a sleeve’s hem, as my eyes fall,
and an elbow bending itself. Coordinates show
my location as paradise, that sovereign, blessed
place where all purpose and longing is set at rest.
This is a planet without vistas, with no
converging lines, with no prospects at all. [...]
I find myself, as it were, on a mountain peak.
Beyond me there is ... Chronos and thin air.
Preserve these words. The paradise men seek
is a dead end, a worn-out, battered cape
bent into crooked shape,
a cone, a finial cap, a steel ship’s bow
from which the lookout never shouts, “Land ho!”
All you can tell for certain is the time.
That said, there’s nothing left but to police
the revolving hands. The eye drowns silently
in the clockface as in a broad, bottomless sea.
In paradise all clocks refuse to chime
for fear they might, in striking, disturb the peace.
Double all absences, multiply by two
whatever’s missing, and you’ll have some clue
to what it’s like here. A number, in any case,
is also a word and, as such, a device
or gesture that melts away without a trace,
like a small cube of ice. [...]
Consisting of Love, of dirty words, a blend
of ashes, the fear of death, the fragile case
of the bone, and the groin’s jeopardy, an erect
body at seaside is the foreskin of space,
letting semen through. His cheek tear-silver-flecked,
man juts forth into Time; man is his own end. [...]"
(Joseph Brodsky, Collected poems in English, Lullaby of Cape Cod, 1975)

E1.3.A.1.2_REF2_LIT.wE1.3.D.1.

E1.3.D.1.7. CENTERING… IS THE DISCIPLINE OF BRINGING IN (I.E., OF SYMPATHY OR EMPATHY) RATHER THAN OF LEAVING OUT. OF SAYING “YES, YES” TO WHAT WE BEHOLD. TO WHAT IS HOLY AND TO WHAT IS UNBEARABLE.  "Centering is a verb. It is an ongoing process… centering is not a model, but a way of balancing, a spiritual resource in times of conflict, an imagination. It seems in certain lights to be an alchemical vessel, a retort, which bears an integration of purposes, an integration of levels of consciousness. It can be called to, like a divine ear. […]
Centering… is the discipline of bringing in (i.e., of sympathy or empathy) rather than of leaving out. Of saying “Yes, Yes” to what we behold. To what is holy and to what is unbearable. But my experience tells me now that there is an important crucial stage of saying Yes to a No. For resistance also must be embraced. Not only accepting resistance but practicing it. [...]
The hardest and most rewarding lesson has been to learn to experience antipathy objectively, with warmth. For antipathy follows a gesture of separating, and the goal, which is to be both separate and connected, requires that one move inwardly in opposite directions. Toward self-definition and toward community. Toward ethical individualism and toward social justice. It is this fusing of the opposites that Centering enables. […]
In centering the clay on the potter’s wheel, one centers down, yes, and then one immediately centers up! Down and up, wide and narrow, letting focus bear within it an expanded consciousness and letting a widened awareness (empathetic) have the commitment to detail of a focused attention. Not “either… or,” but “both… and.” You can perhaps feel the inner movement of a Centering consciousness that plays dynamically in the tides of inner and outer, self and other, in an instinctive hope toward wholeness.[…]
“Center” and “centered” have come to be fairly widely used. They tend to imply a connection with the navel, with one-pointedness, on the way to bliss, realization, and inner peace. But these are not the goals of the Centering process. For it is a continual engagement with experience, not a withdrawal from it. It begins with pain and ends with paradox. It wrestles with evil and the daimonic as it does with angels and repentance. It is an activity of consciousness, not a stage of spiritual achievement. […]
I have found that Centering, like clay, … bears the future within it. For it contains a space for ongoing development and differentiation. In other words, it proves to be an open image, a vessel, holding a content that is life itself. […]
It could be said that fidelity to the processes of Centering is a path to full breathing, to a balance willingly at risk. […]
The deeper we go into these realms, the more contact we make with another’s reality. The sharper the sense of pain and bliss as they interweave through the heartbreak and luck of life, the more the line between self and other may dissolve. […]
I am an odd bird in both academic and craft worlds, perhaps because I am a poet, and thus, by calling, busy with seeing the similarities between things ordinarily thought to be different, busy with feeling the sense of relatedness grow through my limbs like a smoke-tree wafting and fusing its images, busy with the innerness of outerness, eating life in its layers like a magic cake made of silica sounds shapes and temperatures and all the things that appear to be separated stacked together in transparencies of color, and it is perhaps my vocation to swallow it whole. The expanding universe. The resilient appetite. The continuous play. The changing, changeful person, mobile and intact, finding his way on. […]
For life — I am sure of this — is not transforming energy, but transforming person. Energy is the means. Being is not what but whom. It is Presence in whom and before whom we show ourselves. Let us ride our lives like natural beasts, like tempests, like the bounce of a ball or the slightest ambiguous hovering of ash, the drift of scent: let us stick to those currents that can carry us, membering them with our souls. Our world personifies us, we know ourselves by it. Let us then speak to each other in our most intimate concern."
(M.C. Richards, Centering: In Pottery, Poetry, and the Person, 1964)

E1.3.D.1.8. ANGER TRULY FELT AT ITS CENTER IS THE ESSENTIAL LIVING FLAME OF BEING FULLY ALIVE AND FULLY HERE
"ANGER is the deepest form of compassion, for another, for the world, for the self, for a life, for the body, for a family and for all our ideals, all vulnerable and all, possibly about to be hurt. Stripped of physical imprisonment and violent reaction, anger is the purest form of care, the internal living flame of anger always illuminates what we belong to, what we wish to protect and what we are willing to hazard ourselves for. What we usually call anger is only what is left of its essence when we are overwhelmed by its accompanying vulnerability, when it reaches the lost surface of our mind or our body’s incapacity to hold it, or when it touches the limits of our understanding. What we name as anger is actually only the incoherent physical incapacity to sustain this deep form of care in our outer daily life; the unwillingness to be large enough and generous enough to hold what we love helplessly in our bodies or our mind with the clarity and breadth of our whole being. [...]
What we have named as anger on the surface is the violent outer response to our own inner powerlessness, a powerlessness connected to such a profound sense of rawness and care that it can find no proper outer body or identity or voice, or way of life to hold it. What we call anger is often simply the unwillingness to live the full measure of our fears or of our not knowing, in the face of our love for a wife, in the depth of our caring for a son, in our wanting the best, in the face of simply being alive and loving those with whom we live.
Our anger breaks to the surface most often through our feeling there is something profoundly wrong with this powerlessness and vulnerability… Anger in its pure state is the measure of the way we are implicated in the world and made vulnerable through love in all its specifics. [...]
Anger truly felt at its center is the essential living flame of being fully alive and fully here; it is a quality to be followed to its source, to be prized, to be tended, and an invitation to finding a way to bring that source fully into the world through making the mind clearer and more generous, the heart more compassionate and the body larger and strong enough to hold it. What we call anger on the surface only serves to define its true underlying quality by being a complete but absolute mirror-opposite of its true internal essence."
(David Whyte, Consolations: The Solace, Nourishment and Underlying Meaning of Everyday Words)
