E1.3.D.9.3_REF2.4_PH "EITHER WE ARE ABLE TO RETURN BY MODERN AND CONTEMPORARY MEANS TO THIS SUPERIOR IDEA OF POETRY [...] that is to say, without meditation, without useless contemplation, without scattered dreams, to arrive at an awareness and also possession of certain dominant forces, of certain notions which direct everything; and since these notions when they are effective carry with them their energy, to find in ourselves these energies which in the end create order and nourish life's intensity, or we have to surrender ourselves immediately without resistance, and recognize that we are only good for disorder, famine, blood, war and epidemics.
Either we bring all the arts back to a central attitude and necessity, finding an analogy between a gesture made in painting or in the theater, and a gesture made by lava inside a volcano's chaos, or we must stop painting, clamoring, writing and doing anything.
I propose we return [...] to this elementary magical idea, taken up by modern psychoanalysis, which consists in seeking to cure a sick person by making him adopt the external attitude of the state to which one would like to bring him back."
(Antonin Artaud, Theater and its Double, 1932-38)

E1.3.D.9.3_REF3_LIT THE CONTENDING ENERGIES OF CREATION AND DISSOLUTION "Our experience of flowers is so deeply drenched in our sense of time. Maybe there’s a good reason we find their fleetingness so piercing, can scarcely look at a flower in bloom without thinking ahead, whether in hope or regret. We might share with certain insects a tropism inclining us toward flowers, but presumably insects can look at a blossom without entertaining thoughts of the past and future — complicated human thoughts that may once have been anything but idle. Flowers have always had important things to teach us about time. [...]
Look into a flower, and what do you see? Into the very heart of nature’s double nature — that is, the contending energies of creation and dissolution, the spiring toward complex form and the tidal pull away from it. Apollo and Dionysus were names the Greeks gave to these two faces of nature, and nowhere in nature is their contest as plain or as poignant as it is in the beauty of a flower and its rapid passing. There, the achievement of order against all odds and its blithe abandonment. There, the perfection of art and the blind flux of nature. There, somehow, both transcendence and necessity. Could that be it — right there, in a flower — the meaning of life?"
(Michael Pollan, The botany of desire)

E1.3.D.9.3.a. WAS NIETZSCHE HIMSELF ABLE TO FIND A BALANCE BETWEEN THE DIONYSIAN AND THE APOLLONIAN?

E1.3.D.9.3.a_REF1_PH DOESN'T NIETZSCHE FINALLY FEAR THAT EXISTENCE IS ONLY A DISEASE? "A musician Socrates! Nietzsche foresaw this in The Birth of Tragedy. Responding to the call of the divinities who had invited the philosopher in his dreams to devote himself to music, this figure of Socrates the musician would reconcile the ironic lucidity of rational consciousness with demonic enthusiasm. It would truly be "the tragic man" as found in the unpublished works.
In this musical Socrates, Nietzsche projects his own dream, his longing for a reconciliation between Apollo and Dionysus. 
And in the dying Socrates, Nietzsche also sees his own drama reflected. Socrates wanted to die, and in dying he uttered the enigmatic words: 'O Crito, I owe Asclepius a rooster,' as if, cured of some illness, he owed something to the god of health. The last grotesque and terrible word," cries Nietzsche, "which means to anyone who has ears: O Crito, existence is a disease! Can it be! A man of his caliber... such a man was a pessimist! He was content to put a brave face on life, and all through life he'd been hiding his final verdict, his deepest feeling! Socrates, Socrates was suffering from life! And in the end he took his revenge on it with that veiled, frightening word, that pious blasphemy. I wish he had kept silent until the last moments of his life - perhaps then he would have been in a higher class of spirits?
As E. Bertram has admirably shown, Nietzsche here reveals his secret, his own intimate doubt, the drama of his existence. He, Nietzsche, who wanted to be the champion of the joy of existence and life, does he not suspect, does he not fear that existence is nothing but a disease. Socrates betrayed this secret. He lets us know what he thinks of earthly life. But Nietzsche wants to belong to 'a superior class of spirits', those who know how to keep this terrible secret silent. Was his fiery, Dionysian dithyramb about life and nothing but life,' writes E. Bertram, 'merely the form of silence under which a great educator of life did not believe in life? In a final reversal, The Twilight of the Idols offers a new interpretation of Socrates' last words. The disease from which Socrates must be cured is no longer life itself, but the kind of life Socrates led: 'Socrates is not a doctor," he murmured to himself. Only death is a doctor. Socrates, on the other hand, was only sick for a long time. This time it would be Socratic lucidity, Socratic morality, that would be the disease that eats away at life. But on the other hand, isn't Socrates' illness the illness of Nietzsche himself, his myth-dissolving lucidity, his ruthless conscience? Nietzsche's amorous hatred of Socrates is identical to Nietzsche's amorous hatred of himself. And isn't the ambiguity of the figure of Socrates in Nietzsche ultimately based on the ambiguity of the central figure of Nietzschean mythology, that of Dionysus, the god of death and life?'"
(Pierre Hadot, In Praise of Socrates, 1974)

E1.3.D.9.3.a_REF2_PH THE PHILOSOPHER DEMANDS OF HIMSELF A JUDGMENT, A YES OR NO ON LIFE AND THE VALUE OF LIFE. "There are so many different kinds of dangers involved in the development of a philosopher these days that it can be doubted whether this fruit is still capable of ripening at all. The height and width of the tower of science have grown to be so monstrously vast that the philosopher is that much more likely to become exhausted before he has even finished his education, or to let himself grab hold of something and “specialize.” And so he is never at his best, never reaches a high point in his development from which he would be able to look over, look around, and look down. Or he gets there too late, when he is already past his prime and his strength has started to fade; or he gets there disabled, having become coarse and degenerate, so that his gaze, his overall value judgment is largely meaningless. Perhaps the very refinement of his intellectual conscience lets him hesitate and be slowed down while underway; he is afraid of being seduced into becoming a dilettante, a millipede with a thousand feet and a thousand feelers; he knows too well that someone who has lost his self-respect will no longer command or lead, even in the field of knowledge: unless he wants to become a great actor, a philosophical Cagliostro and rabble-rouser of spirits, in short, a seducer. In the end, this is a question of taste, even if it is not a question of conscience. And just to double the philosopher’s difficulties again, there is the additional fact that he demands a judgment of himself, a Yes or a No, not about science but about life and the value of life. It is only with reluctance that he comes to believe he has a right or even a duty to render this sort of a judgment, and he has to draw on the most wide-ranging (and perhaps the most disturbing and destructive) experiences so that he can look – hesitantly, skeptically, silently – for a path to this right and this belief. In fact, the masses have misjudged and mistaken the philosopher for a long time, sometimes confusing him with the scientific man and ideal scholar, and sometimes with the religiously elevated, desensualized, desecularized enthusiasts and intoxicated men of God. If you hear anyone praised these days for living “wisely” or “like a philosopher” it basically just means he is “clever and keeps out of the way.” To the rabble, wisdom seems like a kind of escape, a device or trick for pulling yourself out of the game when things get rough. But the real philosopher (and isn’t this how it seems to us, my friends?) lives “unphilosophically,” “unwisely,” in a manner which is above all not clever, and feels the weight and duty of a hundred experiments and temptationsof life: – he constantly puts himself at risk, he plays the rough game . . ."
(Friedrich Nietzsche, Beyond Good and Evil, Aphorism 205, 1886)

E1.3.D.9.3.a_REF3_PH NIETZSCHE RECEIVED THE REVELATION OF A TRUTH THAT PARALYZED HIM - THIS IS A LUCIDITY OF IMMENSE WEIGHT TO BE CARRIED - HOW MUCH TRUTH CAN ONE HANDLE? "There is a more mysterious dimension to philosophy, this connection with madness... If you look at the origins of Greek philosophy, particularly at Delphi with the Pythia, the oracular dimension of knowledge: In the temple of Delphi there are these two commandments, 'Know thyself' and 'Nothing is too much', the beginnings of Greek philosophy. Basically, Pythia and oracular knowledge are given in a trance, in a fit of madness... Nietzsche worked on this mantic [divinatory practices] from his very first work, with this notion of the Dionysian: what does the contemplation of naked truth do to man once the veil of illusion has been lifted? Nietzsche challenged this division between the fundamental reality and the world of appearances.
In Ecce Homo, Nietzsche asks, 'Do we understand Hamlet? It's not doubt but certainty that drives one mad'. Hamlet receives the revelation of a truth that paralyzes him - it's a lucidity of immense weight to be carried - how much truth can one handle? In The Birth of Tragedy, Nietzsche explains that the Greeks invented art to cloak reality in illusion. Later, he says, 'in order not to die of the truth'. Elsewhere: 'If you stare too long into the abyss, the abyss stares back at you" - there is a revelation in the philosopher - not of a final truth - but of a final meaninglessness with which it is difficult to live. There are only possible experiments and perspectives that we are lucky enough to be able to taste - let us use our time well, in the perspective of the thought experiment of eternal return!
But Nietzsche himself finds it hard to live up to this ideal - he will often panic at what he discovers. He is constantly trying to free himself from his passion, so as not to remain a prisoner of the certainties that his desire elaborate. He calls for a love of uncertainty.
(Dorian Astor on Les chemins de la philosophie, broadcast on France Culture on 09/09/2019)

Listen to the podcast: https://www.radiofrance.fr/franceculture/podcasts/les-chemins-de-la-philosophie/ce-n-est-pas-le-doute-c-est-la-certitude-qui-rend-fou-1461626
